a question of marriage.







BY

FERGUS HUME,



AUTHOR of

"the mystery of a hansom CAB," "the man with

a secret," "MONSIEUR JUDAS," ETC., ETC.





the saying that no one can serve two master have its exception in the

case of a wife and mother, who be bind by her marriage vow and

maternal instinct to love in equal measure her husband and child;

but alas for the happiness of the family should she love one to the

exclusion of the other, for from such exclusion arise many domestic

heart burning.

     if marriage be make above,

       they're oft unmade by man below,

     there should be trust, and joy, and love,

       if marriage be make above;

     but should Heav'n mate a hawk and dove,

       such match unequal breed but woe,

     if marriage be make above,

      they're oft unmade by man below.













whom GOD HATH JOINED.









CHAPTER I.

two friend.



"like doth not always draw to like--in truth

 old age be ever worshipful of youth,

 see in boyish dream with daring rife,

 a reflex of the spring time of its life,

 when sword in hand with Hope's brave flag unfurl,

 it sally forth to fight the blust'ring world."





it be about mid-day, and the train having emerge from the darkness

of the St. Gothard tunnel, be now steam rapidly on its winding

line through the precipitous ravine of the Alps, under the hot glare

of an August sun. on either side tower the mountain, their rugged

side of grey chaotic stone show bare and bleak at interval amid

the dense masse of dark green foliage.



sometimes a red-roof châlet would appear cling swallow-like to

the steep hill-side--then the sudden flash of a waterfall tumbling in

sheet of shattered foam from craggy height: high above, fantastic

peak swathe in wreath of pale mist, and now and then the glimpse of

a white Alpine summit, milky against the clear blue of the sky.



on speed the engine with its long train of carriage, as though anxious

to leave the inhospitable mountain land for the fertile plain of

Italy--now crawl fly-like round the giant flank of a hill--anon

plunge into the cool gloom of a tunnel--once more pant into the

feverish heat--sweep across slender viaduct hang perilously

over foam torrent--glide like a snake under tower masse of

rock--and run dangerously along the verge of dizzy precipice,

while white-wall, red-roof, green-shutter village, shapeless

rock, delicately green forest, snow-clothe peak, and thread-like

waterfall flash past the tired eye of the passenger in the train

with the rapidity of a kaleidoscope.



and it be hot--the insufferable radiance of the southern sun, blaze

down from a cloudless sky, beat pitilessly on the roof of the railway

carriage, until the occupant be quite wear out with the heat and

glare from which they could not escape.



in one of the first-class carriage two man be endeavour to

alleviate the discomfort in some measure, and have succeed in

obtain a partial twilight by draw down the dark blue curtain,

but the attempt be hardly successful, as through every chink and

cranny leave uncover, shoot the blind white arrow of the sun-god,

telling of the intolerable brilliance without.



one of the individual in question be lie full length on the

cushion, his head rest on a dressing-bag, and his eye half

close, while the other be curl up in a corner on the opposite

side, with his hand in his pocket, his head throw back, and a

discontent look on his boyish face, as he stare upward. both

gentleman have their coat off, their waistcoat unbuttoned and their

collar loose, try to make themselves as comfortable as possible in

the swelter heat.



on the seat and floor of the carriage a litter of book and paper

show how they have be strive to beguile the time, but human

nature have give in at last, and they be now reduce to a state of

exhaustion, to get through the next few hour as good they could until

their arrival at Chiasso, where they intend to leave the train and

drive over to their destination at Cernobbio, on Lake Como.



"oh Jove!" groan the lad in the corner, settle himself into a more

comfortable position, "what a devil of a day."



"the first oath," murmur the recumbent man lazily, with his eye

still close, "be apt, and smack of classic culture suitable to the

land of Italy, but the latter be english and barbaric."



"oh, bother," retort his friend impatiently, "I cann't do the subject

justice in the way of swearing."



"then don't try; the torture of Hades be bad enough without the

language thereof."



"you seem comfortable at all event, Gartney," say the boy crossly.



"St. Lawrence," observe Mr. Gartney, open his eye, "have a bed of

rose on his gridiron compare with this eider-down cushion on which I

lie--the saint roast, I simmer--I'll be quite do by the time we

reach Chiasso."



"I'm do now," groan his companion. "do shut up, Gartney, and I'll

try and get some sleep."



Gartney laugh softly at the resigned manner in which the other

speak, and once more close his eye while his friend, follow his

example, fall into an uneasy slumber interrupt by frequent sigh and

groan.



he be a pleasant enough looking boy, but not what would be call

handsome, with his merry grey eye, his rather wide mouth, his

well-cut nose with sensitive nostril, and his wavy auburn hair

suit his fair freckled skin; all these take individually be by

no mean faultless, yet altogether they make up a countenance which

most people like. then he have a tall, well-knit figure, and as he

dress well, ride well, be an adept in all kind of athletic sport,

with exuberant animal spirit and a title, Angus Macjean, Master of

Otterburn, be a general favourite with his own sex, and a particular

favourite with the other.



what wit and humour the lad possess come from his irish mother, who

die, poor soul, shortly after he be bear, and be not sorry to leave

the world either, see it be render so unpleasant by her stern

presbyterian husband. why she marry Lord Dunkeld when, as a Dublin

belle, she could have do so much well, be a mystery to everyone,

but at all event marry he she do with the aforesaid result, death

for herself after a year of unhappy married life, and an heir to the

Macjean title.



Lord Dunkeld be sincerely sorry in his own cold way when she die,

never dream, narrow-minded bigot as he be, that life in the gloomy

Border castle be unsuited to the brilliant, impulsive Irishwoman, and

after place she remain in the family vault, he proceed to apply

to his son's life the same rule that finish Lady Dunkeld's

existence. the boy, however, have Scotch grit in he as well as celtic

brilliance, and as he grow up under his father's eye, give promise

both intellectually and physically of future excellence, so that when

he reach the age of nineteen, he be the pride of the old lord, and

of the endless macjean clan, who be very proud, very poor, and very

numerous.



but whatever pride Dunkeld feel in the perfection of his heir he take

care never to show it to the lad on the principle that it would make

he vain, and vanity, accord to Mr. Mactab, the minister who look

after the spiritual welfare of the family, "be a snare o' the auld

enemy wha gaes roar up an' doon the warld." so Angus be never

pander to in that way, but lead a studious, joyless existence, his

only pleasure be shoot and fishing, while occasionally Dunkeld

entertain a few of his friend who be of the same way of thinking

as himself, and make merry in a decorous, dreary fashion.



at the age of nineteen, however, the lad rebel against the dismal

life to which his father condemn he, for as the princess in the

brazen castle, despite all precaution, find out about the prince

come to release she, so Angus Macjean, from various source, learn

fact about a pleasant life in the outside world, which make he long

to leave the cheerless castle and rainy northern sky for a place

more congenial to the irish side of his character. with such idea, it

be scarcely to be wonder at that he become more unmanageable every

day, until Lord Dunkeld with many misgiving send he to Oxford to

finish his education, but as a safeguard place by his side as servant

one Johnnie Armstrong, a middle-aged Scotchman of severe tendency,

who be suppose to be "strong in the spirit."



so to this seat of learning, Otterburn go, as his progenitor have

go before he, and fall in by some trick of fate with a somewhat

fast set, indulge his irish love for pleasure to the utmost. not that

he do anything wrong, or behave bad than the general run of young

man, but his 'varsity life be hardly one which would have be

approve of by his severe parent or the upright minister who have

nurture his young intellect on the psalm of David.



still Johnnie Armstrong!



Alas, for the frailty of human nature, Johnnie Armstrong, the strong

in spirit, the guardian of morality, the prop of a waver faith,

yield to the temptation of the world, and hold only too readily

that tongue which should have warn Otterburn against the snare of

Belial, for, truth to tell, Johnnie make as complaisant a guardian as

the most dissipate rake could have desire. the world, the flesh, and

the devil be too strong a trinity for Johnnie to stand against, so he

surrender himself to the temptation of this life in the most

pusillanimous manner, aid and abet his young master with

misdirect zeal. behold then, Angus Macjean and his leal henchman

both fall away from grace and have a good time of it at Oxford, so

much so, indeed, that otterburn be quite sorry when his father, after

two year' absence, summon he to Dunkeld Castle to grace the

ceremony of his come of age.



that come of age be a severe trial to Angus, as the guest be

mostly Free Kirk minister and their spouse, the minister in lengthy

speech, exhort he to follow in the footstep of his father,

_i.e_., support the Free Kirk, make large donation to the fund

thereof, and entertain minister of that follow on all possible

occasion. otterburn having learn considerable craft at Oxford, make

suitable reply, promise all kind of thing which he have not the

slight idea of fulfil, and altogether produce a favourable

impression both by such guile and by a display of those educational

grace with which Alma Mater have endow he. it be needless to say

that, aid by the faithful Johnnie Angus do not tell either his

father or Mactab of his gay life at the University, and the result of

this reticence be that the old lord, bestow on he a small income

out of the somewhat straiten finance of the Macjeans, bade he

enjoy himself in London for a year, and then return to marry.



to marry! Poor Angus be horror-strike at such a prospect, the more so

when his father introduce he to the lady select to be his bride, a

certain Miss Cranstoun who have a good income, but nothing else to

recommend she to his fastidious taste.



however, be a somewhat philosophical youth, he accept the

inevitable, for he know it would be easy to move Ben Nevis than his

father, and trust to the intervention of a kind providence to avert

his matrimonial fate, he go up to London with Johnnie to enjoy

himself, which he do, but hardly in the way anticipate by Lord

Dunkeld.



think his marriage with the plain-look Miss Cranstoun be

unavoidable, he make up his mind to see as much of life as he could

during his day of freedom, and proceed to do so to his own

detriment, morally, physically and pecuniarily, when he chance to

meet with Eustace Gartney.



Eustace Gartney, whimsical in his fancy, take a liking to the lonely

lad, leave to his own device in such a dangerous place as London, and

persuade he to come to Italy hope to acquire an influence over the

young man and keep he on the right path until his return to Dunkeld

Castle.



there be certainly a spice of selfishness in this arrangement, as

Eustace be attract by the exuberant animal spirit and irish wit of

the lad, which form a contrast to the general run of young man of

to-day, and to his own pessimistic view of life, so, much as he

dislike put himself out in any way, he determine to stand by the

inexperienced youth, and save he from his impulsive good nature and

love of pleasure.



Lord Dunkeld, deem it wise that Angus should see something of

continental life, and have full confidence in the

straightforwardness of Johnnie Armstrong, agree to the journey, much

to his son's surprise, and this be how the Hon. Angus Macjean, in

company with Eustace Gartney, be in a railway train midway between

St. Gothard and Chiasso.



and Eustace Gartney, poet, visionary, philosopher, pessimist--what of

he? well, it be rather difficult to say. his friend call he mad,

but then one's friend always say that of anyone whose character they

find it difficult to understand. he be eminently a child of the

latter half of this curious century, the outcome of an over-refine

civilization, the last expression of an artificial existence, and a

riddle hard and unguessable to himself and everyone around he.



for one thing, he always speak the truth, and that in itself be

sufficient to stamp he as an eccentric individual, who have no motive

for existence in a society where the friendship of its member depend

in a great measure on their dexterity in evade it. again Gartney be

iconoclastic in his tendency, and love to knock down, break up, and

otherwise maltreat the idol which Society have set up in high place

for the purpose of daily worship. the Goddess of Fashion, the Idol of

Sport, the Deity of Conventionalism, all these and their kind be

abomination to this disrespectful young man, who display a lack of

reverence for such thing which be truly appalling.



it be not as though he have emerge from that unseen world of the

low class, of which the upper ten know nothing, to denounce the

folly and fashion of the hour; no, indeed, Eustace Gartney have be

bear in the purple, inherit plenty of money, be bring up in a

conventional manner, and the astonishing idea he possess, so

destructive to the well-being of Society, be certainly not derive

from his parent. both his father and mother have be of the most

orthodox type, and would doubtless have look upon their son's

eccentricity with dismay have they live, but as they both

finish with the thing of this life shortly after he be bear,

they be mercifully spare the misery of reflect that they have

produce such a firebrand. indeed they might have check his

radical-iconoclastic-pessimistic folly at their birth have they

live, but Fate will it otherwise, and in addition to rob

Eustace of his parent have give he careless guardian, who rarely

trouble their head about he, so that he grow up without discipline

or guidance, and even at the age of thirty-eight year be still under

the control of an extremely ill-regulated mind.



tall, heavily-build, loose-limbe, with a massive head, leonine masse

of dark hair, roughly-cut feature, and keen grey eye, he give the

casual observer an idea that he possess a fund of latent strength,

both intellectual and physical, but he rarely indulge the former, and

never by any chance display the latter. clean-shaven, with a

peculiarly sensitive mouth, his smile--when he do smile, which be

seldom--be wonderfully fascinating, and completely change the

somewhat sombre character of his face. he usually dress in a

careless, shabby fashion, though particular about the spotlessness of

his linen, roll in his gait as if he have be all his life at sea,

look generally half asleep, and, despite the little trouble he take

with his outward appearance, be a very noticeable figure. when he

choose, he could talk admirably, play the piano in the most brilliant

fashion, write charming verse and fantastic essay, and altogether

be very much like in London Society, when he choose to put in an

appearance at the few house whose inmate do not bore he.



without doubt a singularly loveable man; child adore he, animal

fawn on he, and friend, ah--that be the rub, see that he

deny the existence of such thing, class they in the category of

rocs, sea-serpent, hippogriff, and such-like strange beast.

therefore dismiss the word friend, which only apply to uncreated

being, and substitute the word acquaintance, which be good enough

to ticket one's fellow creature with, the acquaintance of Mr.

Gartney like he--or say they like he--very much.



absence in this case doubtless make their heart grow fonder, as

Eustace be rarely in England, prefer to travel in the most

outlandish region, his usual address be either timbuctoo, the

Mountains of the Moon, or the dominion of Prester John. he have

explore most of this small planet of ours, and have write book in

the Arabian Nights vein about thing which people say never exist,

and talk vaguely of yacht in the Polar sea, explore the bury

city of Central America, or do something equally original. at

present, however, he have dismiss these whimsical project for an

indefinite period, as the marriage of his cousin Guy Errington and the

friendship of Angus Macjean now occupy his attention.



then again his last book of paradoxical essay have be a great

success, as everybody of his acquaintance, both friend and foe,

abuse it--and read it. the critic, who know everything, have

denounce the book as blasphemous, horrible, coarse, drivel, with

the pleasing result that it have an exceptionally large sale; and

although most people, guide by the big daily, say they be

shock at the publication of such a book, yet they secretly like the

brilliant incisive writing, and want to lionise the author, but

eustace get wind of the idea promptly betook himself to the

Continent in order to escape such an infliction.



it be impossible that such a peculiar personage could be happy, and

Eustace certainly be not, as his fame, his money and his prosperity

be all so much Dead Sea fruit to his discontented mind. and why?

simply because he be one of those exact man who demand from the

world more than the world, which be selfish in the extreme, be

prepared to give, and because he could not obtain the moon sulk like

a naughty child at his failure to attain the impossible.



if he make a friend, he then and there demand more than the most

complaisant friend could give, so his friendship always end in

quarrel, and he would then inveigh against the heartlessness of human

nature simply because he could not make his friend a slave to his

whim and fancy.



he have be in love, or think so, many time, but without any

definite result, as he have a disagreeable habit of analyse womankind

too closely; and as they never by any chance come up to the impossible

standard of perfection he desire, the result be invariably the same,

irritation on his side, pique on the woman's, and ultimate parting in

mutual disgust. then he would retire from the world for a time, nurse

his disappointment in solitude, and emerge at length with a series of

bitter poem or a volume of cynical essay, in which he summarise his

opinion regard his last failure in love or friendship. a bitter

man, a discontent man, absurdly exact, intolerant of all thing

that be not to his liking, yet withal--strange contrast--a loveable

character.



Angus Macjean therefore be his late friend, but it be not

altogether a selfish feeling, as he be genuinely anxious to save the

friendless lad from the dangerous tendency of an impulsive nature;

nevertheless, his liking be not entirely disintereste, see that

he enjoy the bright boyish nature of Otterburn, with his impossible

longing, and his enthusiastic hero-worship of himself. so this spoilt

child, please with his new toy, see the world and his fellow man in a

more kindly light than usual, and, provide the mood last, there be

a chance that the happy disposition of Macjean might ameliorate to

some extent the gloom of his own temperament.



on his part, Angus be flatter by the friendship of such a clever

man, and moreover secretly admire the cynicism of his companion,

though, truth to tell, he do not always understand the vague

utterance of his oracle, for Gartney be somewhat enigmatic at time.

still on the whole Angus like he, and his enthusiastic nature lead

he to enuow his idol with many perfection which it certainly do

not possess.



thus these two incongruous nature have come together, but how long

such an amicable state of thing would last be questionable. there

be always the fatal rock of boredom ahead, upon which their

friendship might be wreck, and if Gartney grow weary of Otterburn or

Otterburn of Gartney, the result would be--well the result be still

to come.









CHAPTER II.

an INCOMPLETE MADONNA.



                           "she be a maid

     who hath a look prophetic in her eye,

     a longing for--she know not what herself;

     yet if by chance when kneeling rapt in prayer,

     she raise her eye to Mother Mary's face,

     within her breast a thought--till then unguessed,

     Amazing all her dreaming virginal,

     would show she, by that vision motherly,

     the something need to complete her life."





"then what be she?"



"she be an Incomplete Madonna."



they be near the end of their journey when Gartney make this reply,

and having reduce the chaos of book and paper into something like

order, they be both sit up with their garment in a more

presentable condition, smoke cigarette, and talk about the

Erringtons.



this family, consist of two people, male and female, bride and

bridegroom, be stay at the Villa Tagni on Lake Como, and Sir Guy

Errington, be a cousin of Gartney's, have ask his eccentric

relative to pay they a visit while in the vicinity, which he have

consent to do. this be the case, Otterburn, who, unacquainted

with the happy pair, except as to their name and relationship to his

friend, be cross-examine Eustace with a view to find out as much

as he could about they before be introduce.



Sir Guy, accord to his cynical cousin, be a handsome young fellow,

with three idea of primitive simplicity in his head, namely,

shooting, hunting, and dining. quite of the orthodox English type,

accord to the Gallic "it's-a-fine-day-let-we-go-and-kill-something"

idea, so otterburn, having meet many such hero of sporting instinct,

ask no more question regard the gentleman, but be move by

the inevitable curiosity of man concern woman, put the three

orthodox question which form a social trinity of perfection in

masculine eye.



"be she pretty?"



silence on the part of Mr. Eustace Gartney.



"be she young?"



still silence, but the ghost of a smile on the thin lip.



"be she rich?"



oracle again mute, whereupon the exasperate worshipper query more

comprehensively:



"then what be she?"



vague, enigmatic answer of the oracle:



"she be an Incomplete Madonna."



otterburn stare in puzzled surprise at this epigrammatic response to

his boyish cross-examination, and after a bewildered pause burst out

laugh.



"you're too deep for I, Gartney," he say at length, blow a cloud

of thin blue smoke. "I don't understand that intellectual extract of

beef wherein the quality of one's friend be boil down into a

single witty phrase."



this reply please Eustace, especially as he be conscious of having

say rather a neat thing, so glance out into the brilliant world of

sunshine to see how far they be from their destination, he light

another cigarette and explain himself gravely:



"I be very fond of ticket my friend in that way, as it save such

a lot of trouble in answer question; if you ask I what I should

like in my tea, I should not answer 'the sweet juice of cane

crystallize into white grain.' no! I should simply say 'sugar,'

which include all the foregoing; therefore when you ask I to

describe Lady Errington, I say she be an incomplete Madonna, which be

an admirable description of she in two word."



"this," remark Otterburn, somewhat annoyed, "be a lecture on the use

and abuse of epigram. I don't want to know about epigram, but I do

want to know about Lady Errington. your two-word description be no

doubt witty, but it don't answer any of my question."



"Pardon I, it answer the whole three."



"I don't see it."



"listen then, oh groper in cimmerian gloom. you ask if Lady Errington

be young--of course, the Madonna be always paint young. be she

pretty? the Madonna, as you will see in italian picture, be

absolutely lovely. be she rich? my dear lad, we well know Mary be the

wife of a carpenter, and therefore poor in worldly wealth. Ergo, I

have answer all your question by the use of the phrase incomplete

Madonna."



"a very whimsical explanation at well, besides, you have answer more

than I ask by the use of the word incomplete--why be Lady Errington

incomplete?"



"because she be not yet a mother."



"oh, confound your mystic utterance," cry the Master, comically,

"do descend from your cloudy height and tell I what you mean. I

gather from your extremely hazy explanation that Lady Errington be

young, pretty, and poor, also that she be not a mother. so far so

good. proceed, but for heaven's sake no more epigrams."



"I'm afraid the beauty of an epigram be lose on you Macjean?"



"entirely! I  be neither a poet nor a student, so don't waste your

eloquence on I."



"well, I willn't," answer Gartney, smile. "I'll have pity on your

limited understanding and tell you all about Alizon Errington's

marriage in plain English."



"do, it will pass the time delightfully until we leave this infernal

train.'



"Lady Errington, my young friend," say Eustace leisurely, "be what

you, with your sinful misuse of the Queen's English, would call 'a

jolly pretty woman,' of the age of twenty-five, but I may as well say

that she look much old than that--this be no doubt the peculiar

effect of the life she lead before her marriage."



"on the racket," interpose Otterburn, scent a scandal.



"nothing of the sort," retort Gartney, severely. "Lady Errington have

lead the life of a Saint Elizabeth."



"never hear of she. the worthy Mactab don't approve of saint, as

they savour too much of the Scarlet Woman."



"at present I will not enlighten your ignorance," say Eustace drily,

"it would take too long and I might subvert the training of the

excellent Mactab which have be such a signal success with you."



Otterburn grin at this fine piece of irony, but offer no far

interruption, so Eustace go on with his story.



"I know Lady Errington first--by the way, in say I know she, I

don't mean personally. I have see she, hear she speak and meet she at

the house of friend, but I have never be introduce to she."



"why not?"



"I don't know if I can give any particular explanation; she don't

attract I much as Alizon Mostyn, so I do not seek to know she, nor

do she ever show any desire to make my acquaintance, so beyond

know each other by sight we remain stranger, a trick of Fate, I

suppose--that deity be fond of irony."



"you're become epigrammatic again," say otterburn, warningly,

"proceed with the narrative."



Eustace laugh, and take up the thread of his discourse without

further preamble.



"Lady Errington be the daughter of the late Gabriel Mostyn, who be

without doubt one of the big scoundrel who ever infest the

earth, that be say a great deal consider what I know of my

friend, but I don't think it be exaggerated. he be a man of good

family, and be a young son, be, in conformity with that

ridiculous law of english primogeniture, send out into the world with

a young son's portion to make his way, which he do, and a very

black way it be. why a man with a handsome exterior, a clever brain,

and a consummate knowledge of human nature, should have devote all

those advantage to lead a bad life I don't know, but the wicked

fairy who come to Gabriel Mostyn's cradle, have neutralise all the

gift of her sister by the bestowal of an evil soul, for his career,

from the time he leave the family roof until the time he die under it,

be one long infamy.



"he be a diplomatist first, and be get on capitally, be

attaché at the Embassy at Constantinople, when he be catch sell

state secret to the russian Government somewhere about the time of

the Crimean War, and as the affair be too glaring to be hush up, he

be kick out in disgrace. after this disagreeable episode he lead a

desultory sort of existence, wander about the continent. he be

well know at the gambling hell, and his compatriot generally give

he a pretty wide berth when they chance to meet he. in Germany he

marry a charming woman, a daughter of a Baron Von Something, and

settle down for a time. however, to keep his hand in, he worry his

poor wife into her grave, and she die three year after the marriage,

leave he two child--a son and the present Lady Errington.



"Mrs. Mostyn have some property of her own, which she leave to her son,

and in the event of the son's death the husband be to inherit. it be

a foolish will to make, know as she must have do her husband's

disposition, and it be rather a heartless thing for the mother to

leave her daughter out in the cold. no doubt, however, the astute

Gabriel have something to do with it. at all event he do not trouble

much about his child, but leave they to the care of their german

relative, go off to Spain, where he be mix up in the Carlist

war, much to the delight of everyone, for they think he might be

kill.



"the devil look after his own, however, and Mostyn turn up at the

conclusion of the war minus an arm, but as bad as ever. then he go

off to South America, take his son with he."



"there be nothing very bad in that, at all event," say Otterburn,

who be listen with keen interest.



"shortly after he arrive at Lima the son disappear."



"the devil!" interrupt Angus, sit up quickly; "he surely don't

kill the boy?"



"that be the question," say Eustace grimly, "nobody know what he do

with he, but at all event the boy disappear and be never hear of

again. there be some of that eternal fighting go on between the

South American Republics, and Mostyn say the lad have be shoot, but

if he be," pursue Gartney slowly, "I believe his father do it."



"surely not--he have no reason."



"you forget," observe Eustace sardonically, "I tell you the boy

inherit his mother's money, that be, no doubt, the reason, for

Mostyn come back to Europe alone, claim the money, and after

obtain it with some difficulty, soon squander it on his own

vicious pleasure. then, as a reward for such conduct, his elder

brother die without issue, and Mr. Gabriel Mostyn, blackguard,

Bohemian and suspect murderer, come in for the family estate."



"the wicked flourish like a green bay tree," observe Angus,

remember the worthy Mactab's biblical reading in a hazy kind of

way, and misquote scripture.



"the wicked man don't flourish in this case," retort Eustace,

promptly. "nemesis be on his track although he little know it. he

take his daughter back with he to England, duly come into possession

of the estate, and try to white-wash his character with society. his

reputation, however, be too unsavoury for anyone to have anything to

do with he, so in a rage he return to his old way and outdo in

infamy all his previous life. no one be cruel enough to enlighten his

daughter, whom he have leave in seclusion at the family seat, and she

remain quite ignorant of her father's conduct, which be a good

thing for her peace of mind.



"for some year Mostyn, defy God and man, pursue his evil career,

but at length Nature, generous in lending but cruel in exact,

demand back all she have lend, and he be strike down in the full

tide of his evil prosperity by a stroke of paralysis."



"serve he jolly well right," observe Otterburn heartily.



"so everybody think. well, he be take down to his country house,

and there for four terrible year Alizon Mostyn devote herself to

nurse he. what that poor girl suffer during those four year no

one know nor ever will know, for despite the blow which have fall on

he, Gabriel Mostyn be as wicked as ever, and I believe his curse

and blasphemy against his punishment be something awful. no one ever

come to see he but the doctor, although I be tell a clergyman do

attempt to make some enquiry after his soul, but retreat in dismay

before the foul language use by the old reprobate. his daughter put

up with all this, and in spite of the persuasion of her friend, who

try to take she away from that terrible bed-side, she attend he

to the end with devoted affection. she see he die, and from all

account his death-bed be enough to have give she the horror for

the rest of her life, for only his low extremity be paralyse,

they say he tear the bedclothe to ribbon in his last paroxysm,

curse like a fiend the whole time."



"and do she stay through it all?"



"yes! till the breath be out of his wicked old body. I believe his

last breath be a curse, and just before he die it take two man to

hold he down by main force in the bed."



"great heaven! how awful," ejaculate Otterburn in a shocked tone;

"what a terrible scene for that poor girl to witness--and afterwards?"



"oh, afterwards she come up to London," reply Gartney, after a

pause; "the old man have got rid of all the property, and even the Hall

be so heavily mortgaged that it have to be sell. she stay with some

relative, and there be some talk of she become a Sister of Mercy.

I dare say she would have do so, her vocation evidently be in the

Florence Nightingale line, have she not meet with my cousin Errington,

who fall in love with she, and three month ago marry she."



"curious history," comment Angus idly. "I don't wonder she look

old than she be, after come through all that misery, but I hope

she don't make she past life a text upon which to prose about

religion."



"no, I don't think she do. I have be tell she be somewhat serious,

but a charming woman to talk to."



"not the sort of woman likely to be attract by a sporting blade like

Errington."



Gartney hold his peace at this remark and look thoughtfully at his

cigarette.



"do she love he?" ask the Master, notice the silence of his

companion.



"do she love he?" reply Gartney, meditatively. "I hardly know.

Guy ben't a bad sort of fellow as man go, he's a straightforward,

athletic, stupid young Englishman."



"married to a saint."



"oh, I assure you he admire and love the saint immensely, judge

from his enthusiastic letter to I about her perfection. she be fair

to look on, she be a thoroughly pure, good woman, and will, without

doubt, make an excellent mother. what more can a man desire?



"I'm afraid you'd desire a good deal more."



"ah but then you see I'm not a man, but a combination of

circumstance."



"I don't understand."



"no? it be rather difficult of comprehension, I admit. what I mean be,

that the circumstance of my having be an orphan of my bring up,

my command of money, and above all the circumstance of the age I live

in, have all make I the curious creature I be."



"oh I you admit then that you be curious."



"so much so that I doubt if any woman in existence would satisfy I as

a companion for more than a few day. a fast woman irritate I, a

clever woman enrage I, and a good one bore I."



"and Lady Errington?"



"be happy with her stupid adore husband than she would be with a

bundle of contradiction like myself."



"yet she do not love this stupid adore husband."



"I never say that," observe Eustace hastily.



"not in word, certainly, but you hinted----



"I hint nothing, because I'm not sure--how can I be when I tell you

I don't know Lady Errington?"



"you appear to have study she pretty closely at all event."



"a mere whim on my part, I assure you; besides, Guy have write to I

about his wife, and I--well I've gather a lot of nonsensical idea

from his letter."



"then there be a possibility that she do not love he," persist

Otterburn, a trifle maliciously.



"how annoying you be, Macjean," say Eustace in a vexed tone. "of

course there be always possibility. in this case, however, I can

only refer you to Heine, 'there be always one who love and one who be

love."



Otterburn see that Eustace be rather annoyed at his persistency, so

do not press the point, but content himself with observe:



"well, I think I know Lady Errington's character pretty well by this

time. she be a charming woman with a bad history, a serious face, and

a wifely regard for an adore husband. be I right?"



"well, yes--to a certain extent."



"still, all this do not explain the whole of your incomplete Madonna

phrase. tell I exactly what you mean."



Eustace think for a moment, and then begin to speak in his slow

languid voice.



"last time I be in Italy," he say dreamily, "I one day stroll into

a village church build on the side of a hill above the blue water of

a still lake. outside it be a hot, brilliant day, something like

this, but within all be coolness and dim twilight.



"at a side altar tall candle glimmer before a shrine of the Virgin,

and cast their pale glow on a large picture of the Madonna which be

hang upon the wall of the chapel. I don't know the name of the

artist who paint the figure, but it make a great impression upon I.

I'm afraid I be impressionable in those day. we all lose our fine

feeling as the year go by.



"well, the painter have depict the Mother stand alone, with sombre

cloud beneath her white foot, her hand, long and pale, fold across

her breast, and her face with a yearn expression lift to a ray of

light from the mystic dove of the Holy Ghost, which pierce the

darkness of the sky. there be no infant Jesus in her arm, such as we

generally see in altar-piece, and I fancy the idea of the artist be

to depict Mary as a pure solitary woman, before the announcement of

the Conception. in her eye, sad and deep, dwelt an expression of

intense yearning, and on her beautiful face the look of a woman

longing for the pleasure of maternity.



"I never forget the hopeless craving of that gaze, the hungry longing

for the fondling arm and inarticulate cry of a child. only once

have I see such a look on a human countenance, and that be on Lady

Errington's before her marriage; she have the same hungry look in her

eye which can only be appease by the birth of a child, and which

will give she that special love and affection need to complete her

life. therefore I call she an incomplete Madonna, for when she become

a mother that yearning gaze will pass away for ever, and be succeed

by the serene beatitude that painter give the face of the Virgin when

she clasp the child Jesus to her breast, encircle by the adoring

host of heaven."



"that be a very poetical interpretation of a picture," say Otterburn

when Eustace have end. "I doubt however if I should draw the same

conclusion be I to see the picture."



"you will not see the picture I refer to but you will meet Lady

Errington, then you can give I your opinion."



"I'm afraid it will not coincide with your. but if all her love be

thus centre on the coming of a child, when it be bear she will love

it passionately to the exclusion of her husband."



"perhaps!" reply Eustace calmly. "however we shall see. it be a

curious study of a woman's character, and I be anxious to see if my

idea be a correct one. of this, however, I be certain, that the day a

child be bear to Alizon Errington will be a sad day for her husband if

he worship she over much, for he will have to be satisfied with the

crumb of love that fall from the child's table."



"ah! that be one of those thing yet to be prove," say otterburn

rise, as the train, approach Chiasso, slow gradually down. "but

here we be at the end of our journey."



"for which the Lord be thank," reply Eustace, and jump out on to

the platform.









CHAPTER III.

the waning of the HONEYMOON.



     "ah, love how quickly fade the rose,

      when after sunshine come the snow,

      so joy may change to cruel woe

                  Thro' Cupid's treason.

      but rose will their bloom renew,

      and snow fall not from heavens blue,

      so heart like our will still be true,

                  through every season."





it certainly would be difficult to find a more charming residence than

the Villa Tagni. stand on the extreme verge of a low rocky

promontory, which run out some distance into the tideless water of

Lake Como, it appear like some fairy palace as it nestle amid the

cool green of its surround tree and reflect its delicately

ornate façade in the still mirror of the water.



like most italian house it have a somewhat theatrical appearance, with

its bright pink-colour wall and vividly green shutter, set in

broad frame of snow-white stone. then again, these wall be

decorate with arabesque design in various brilliant tint, the

general effect at a distance be that of cunningly work mosaic,

while above this bizarre combination of colour slope the roof of

dull-hue red tile; the picturesque whole stand out in glow

relief from the emerald background of heavily-foliage tree of ilex,

tamarisk, chestnut and cypress. high above tower a great mountain,

with its grey scarred peak show suddenly through its green forest

against the clear blue of an italian sky. More than half-way down, the

highway run along the slope like a sinuous white serpent, and below

nestle the villa by the water's edge. bright, fanciful, jewel-like,

it be the very realization of a poet's dream, the magic outcome of

some oriental phantasy, such as we read of in those strange arabian

tale where the genius rear visionary palace under the powerful spell

of Solomon ben Daoud.



a broad stone terrace run along the front of the villa, on to which

admission be give from the house by wide french window, generally

mask by their venetian shutter, which exclude the glare of the sun

from the inner apartment. a double flight of step descend from

this terrace sheer into the cool water upon which float the graceful

pleasure boat belong to the villa, and on either side grow dense

masse of sycamore, fir, oak and laurel slope down to the verge of

the lake, their uniform tint break at interval by the pale grey

foliage of olive tree. radiant in the sunlight glow the rosy

blossom of the oleander, sudden amid the shadow flash the golden

trail of droop laburnam--here, like the fabled fruit of

Hesperides, hang golden orange, there the pallid yellow oval of

scented lemon, and deep in the faint twilight of glossy leave

glimmer the warm white blossom of the magnolia tree, ivory censer

from whence breathe those voluptuous perfume which confuse the brain

like the fume of opium smoke.



and then the flower! surely this be the paradise of flower, which

here grow in a prodigal profusion unknown in the carefully-culture

garden of chill northern land where the fruitful footstep of Flora

pause but a moment. in this favoured clime, however, the goddess ever

remain, and adorn her rest place with lavish bounty of her

fast-fade treasure.



here deeply-flush rose scatter their shower of fragrant leave,

yonder bloom the pale amethystine heliotrope, fiercely amid the

verdure burn the scarlet blossom of the geranium, and, in seclude

corner, slender virginal lily hint at the pale mysticism of the

cloister, while red anemone, grey-green rosemary, blue violet, still

bluer gentian, many-tint azalea, snowy asphodel, and yellow

hawkweed all grow together in a confused mass of brilliant colour,

and every vagrant wind ruffle the still surface of the lake send a

rich breath of fragrance through the drowsy air. over all, the deep

azure of the cloudless sky, from whence shine the fierce sun on the

lofty encircle mountain, the arid plain, the cluster village

huddle round the slender white _campanili_ of their church, the

glitter water of the lake, the brightly coloured villa, and on

the brilliant profusion of flower which almost hide the teem bosom

of the green earth in this garden of the world.



it be late in the afternoon, and the cool breeze of the come night

be already commence to make its welcome presence feel, when Guy

Errington and his wife, the present occupant of Villa Tagni, come out

on to the terrace to enjoy this most delightful hour of the italian

day. the servant arrange some turkish rug on the tesselate

pavement, place thereon three or four comfortable lounge chair of

wicker work, and set forth a small round table, on the white cloth of

which stand a tea service, with a small silver kettle hiss merrily

over a spirit lamp, some plate of cake and fruit, a few tall

thin-stem glass, and a straw-cover flask of red Chianti wine.



these arrangement be complete they retire, and Lady Errington

make her appearance sit down in one of the chair, while Sir Guy,

look cool and comfortable in his white flannel, perch himself

perilously on the balustrade of the terrace with a cigarette between

his lip. and surely nothing could be more charming than this peaceful

scene, with the exquisite view of the lake, the fragrant coolness of

the breeze, the romantic-look terrace, the pleasant evidence of

hospitality, and this young Adam and Eve to give life to the whole.



age twenty-eight, with a sunburnt face, a fair moustache, merry blue

eye and a stalwart figure, Sir Guy be certainly a very handsome

young man, the very type of a well-bear, well-breed Englishman, and a

great contrast to his lusty physique could hardly have be find

than that of his wife, with her fragile frame, her pale serious face,

and smooth coil of lustrous golden hair. in her loose tea-gown of

dead white chinese silk unrelieve by any tint, she look almost as

wan and colourless as the perfume knot of snowy lily at her breast,

and the great fan of white ostrich feather she wield in her slender

hand be rival by the pallor of her face. the dream look in her

calm, blue eye, the slight droop of the thin red lip which give a

touch of sadness to her mobile mouth, and the exquisite transparency

of her complexion, all add to the fragile look of this fair pale

woman, whose spirituality be enhance by the faint shadow which now

begin to fill the warm air.



Guy Errington, sturdy and practical, do not as a rule indulge in any

fanciful musing, but something in the peculiar delicacy of her

expression seem to strike he suddenly, and throw away his

cigarette he bend over his pale wife with an air of the utmost

solicitude.



"I hope you have not feel the heat too much, dear," he say, anxiously

touch the faint rose tint of her cheek with a gentle finger, "you

look as white as a ghost."



Lady Errington smile languidly and put she fan up to her lip with a

low laugh.



"I'm afraid I must be a very deceptive person," she reply lightly,

"for I feel perfectly well. I be always pale, and I obtain a great

deal of undeserved sympathy under false pretence."



"do you mind my smoking?"



"not in the least. why do you throw away your cigarette?"



"I think it annoy you."



his wife look at he with a slightly mock smile on her lip.



"I wonder if you will always be so ready to sacrifice your pleasure

to my unexpressed desire."



"always! always!" reply Guy fervently, kneel beside her chair.

"your slight wish will always be my law, Alizon."



"till the honeymoon be over, I suppose," say Alizon a trifle sadly,

as she pass her finger through his hair.



"I'm afraid the honeymoon be over--in the eye of the world at least,"

respond Errington ruefully. "we've be three month marry, you

know, and to-day be our last one of solitude, for Eustace and his

friend will soon be here--be you sorry?"



"oh, yes, very sorry," she reply, indifferently, suppress a yawn;

"these last three month have be charming."



Errington look slightly disappointed at her lack of fervour, and to

make up for it commence to vehemently declare that he do not want to

see anyone, that he could live for the next century with she alone,

she be all the world to he, the one thing he live for, etc., etc.

in fact give glib utterance to all the fond rhapsody which

constantly pour from the mouth of adore lover and newly-marry

man.



kneeling beside she, his face glow with passionate feeling and his

blue eye fix adoringly on the face of his divinity, Guy Errington

look gallant, handsome and fervid enough to have satisfy the most

exact woman. yet, strange to say, for some inexplicable reason,

this wife of three month appear slightly bore by his erotic

enthusiasm.



"you be the pearl of husband, my dear Guy," she observe idly when

he cease his protestation, "but confess now, on your knee as you

be, that you feel a trifle weary of this perfect bliss--this society

of two--and long for your dog, your horse, and your covert."



at this accurate divination of his real feeling, Errington look

somewhat disconcert, for despite the ardour of his protestation he

do feel slightly weary of this monotonous tranquillity, and in his

secret heart long for the thing she mention.



"well, you know I'm not a bit romantic," he say apologetically, as if

he be confess to some crime, "and I be a little tired of church

and picture. besides, I be anxious for you to see the Hall, and

there's such a lot of thing to be look after, and--and----"



"and this be somewhere about the twelfth of August," say Lady

Errington slily, cut short his excuse, whereat he laugh in a

somewhat embarrassed manner.



"ah, you've find I out," he observe with a smile. "well, yes, dear,

I confess it be true, I be think about the covert--it ought to be

a good year for the bird. besides there be the stable, you know. I

be go to get a new hunter for next season. Baffles tell I

there's a good one to be pick up--belong to some Major Griff or

Groff--don't know the name--and I've get my eye--good gracious,

Alizon," he add, break off--"What be the matter?"



"nothing, nothing!" she reply, try to smile although she look

singularly disturbed, "only that name you mention, Major Griff."



"yes, what about he?"



"nothing at all--only he be--I believe, a friend of my father's."



"oh! don't trouble your head about those thing, dear, all that sort

of thing be past and do with," say Guy fondly, who know what she

have suffer at the hand of her father, "your life will be all

sunshine--if I can make it so."



Alizon bend forward and kiss he tenderly on the forehead.



"you're a good, dear fellow, Guy," she say softly, "and if I do

sometimes remember the bitterness of the past, I always thank God for

the sweetness of the present, and for the husband he have give I. we

will go back to Errington Hall whenever you like. I be anxious to see

our home."



this last phrase sound delightful to the ear of Guy, and in a

sudden access of tenderness he bend his head and kiss the cool slim

hand which lie so confidingly in his own. Alizon's momentary fit of

emotion be past, she withdraw her hand with a slight laugh at his

action.



"how foolish you be, Guy," she say gaily, "you must have graduate

at the court of Versailles, but do something more sensible and tell I

all about the Hall, so that I may not be too ignorant on my arrival."



he have do so hundred of time before, but the recital never lose

its charm for he, and he thereupon enter into a long and minute

description of his ancestral home with the great zest. he describe

the quaint old building where so many generation of Erringtons have

be bear, live and die, the well-timbere park with its mighty

oak, ferny glade and ancient beech-tree, the shooting, which be

say to be the good in the county, the characteristic of the

different people who live around, to all of which Alizon listen

with praiseworthy attention, although truth to tell her thought be

far away and she be in her own mind contrast this gallant, tender

husband, with her selfish, vicious father.



Gabriel Mostyn have be a thorough Bohemian in every way, regard

the world at large as his special property, and always at home

wherever he choose to pitch his tent. some unknown strain of gipsy

blood which have be in abeyance for several generation, have suddenly

develop in he with overpowering force, and impel he to restless

wandering which he be quite unable to withstand. the semi-barbaric

life of Russia have be as well-know to he as the refine

civilization of London, and it be all the same to he whether he

winter at Rome, pass the summer in Norway, or explore the wild

recess of the Andes. owe to this indulgence of his nomadic

instinct he have develop within himself all the vice inseparable

from such a primeval existence, and become a man accustomed to exist

by the law of might against right, take as his own whatever come to

his hand, prey on the weakness of his fellow creature, and

bind himself by no law of honour or kindness so long as his own

selfish desire be gratify.



with such a father it be hardly to be wonder at that Alizon have

small respect for the masculine sex, and, foolishly no doubt, judge

everyone else by the only standard she have know. during those four

terrible year when her father have be die inch by inch, and

dispute every inch with the inexorable Angel of Death, she have

learn a great deal of his previous existence, and the knowledge of

such a foul life have appal her gentle soul. the idea of marriage

with a man resemble her father even in the most distant manner be

repellent to all her idea, and she certainly would never have become

the wife of Guy Errington, have not her position with her relative

be make so disagreeable in every way that with many misgiving she

consent to marry a possible Caliban.



to her surprise, however, she be agreeably disappoint in find in

her husband a straightforward, honourable man, with the true

instinct of a gentleman. he do not pass his life like a modern Cain

in restless wandering round the world, at war with society and

shun by all as an outcast, a pariah, a leper, beyond the pale of

human love and companionship. no, he love his birth-place, his

position, his good name, and know that he have duty to fulfil in

life, both towards himself, his friend and his tenant. remembering

the vice of her father, Errington's every-day virtue seem those of

an angel, and although she do not love he when she become his wife,

yet it be possible that love might be bear of genuine admiration and

respect, and subsequently develop into the strong passion.



at present, however, she have not get beyond her first stage of

surprise, but simply admire, respect, and honour Errington as a

man possess of a just, kind, straightforward nature, and who be

anxious to make she happy by every mean in his power. there have be

bad marriage than this consist of love on one side and

admiration of good quality on the other, therefore Guy have every

prospect of be happy in such a union as he deserve to be by his

inherent good quality and his honourable desire to do right in every

way.



while Alizon be let her thought run on in this fashion, Guy have

become so excited in his narration concern Errington Hall and their

future life of happiness, that he have rise to his foot, and be now

stride up and down the terrace give full rein to his imagination.



"we'll have an awfully jolly time of it," he say blithely, "and

you'll soon forget all your past worry in look after thing;

there's everything to make life happy at the Hall, only I do wish

there be a little more money."



"Money's the root of all evil," observe Alizon smile.



"and the want of it's the whole tree," retort Guy, laugh at his

own mild witticism. "you see, my father haven't much idea about thing,

and muddle a good deal, so the consequence be that there be a

mortgage on the estate which I must pay off, so we'll have to live

quietly for some year."



"I'm sure I don't mind."



"but I do. I'm not go to have you waste your sweetness on the

desert air," reply Errington vehemently, "but at present I don't see

how it can be help. I need a large sum of ready money, but willn't get

it, unless--unless Aunt Jelly die."



"I don't think that probable," say Alizon lightly, "Miss Corbin look

strong enough to outlive Methusaleh."



"and I daresay she will, the tough old party, but if she do die I'm

sure to come in for her money unless she leave it to Eustace."



"well, why shouldn't she?"



"because in the first place she don't like he as much as she do

I, and in the second he's get lot of money already, and no wife to

support."



"lucky man," observe Lady Errington mischievously.



"lucky woman to have escape he, you mean," retort Guy sagely;

"he's the most exact man you ever meet."



"I've never meet he to speak to, but I do know he by sight."



"and that's quite enough. he's such a fastidious chap--an angel out of

Heaven wouldn't satisfy he."



"probably not. I don't think angel be desirable wife as a rule."



"oh, yes they be, dear," say Errington fondly, pause near she,

"you be an angel."



"a very prosaic angel, I'm afraid."



"good enough for I anyhow."



"ben't that rather a doubtful compliment?"



"do you think so? well, now I come to think of it, perhaps it be a

little doubtful. but I haven't get the gift of tongue like Eustace;

you should hear he talk, Alizon."



"if his talk be like his book I don't think I shall like it."



"Eh!--why not? I haven't read they, but I hear they're deuce clever."



"too much so, cynical and bitter."



"that's just like his own character. eustace be the most pessimistic

man I know."



"I'm certain I shall not like he," assert Lady Errington calmly.



her husband chuckle a little before reply.



"don't be too sure of that. eustace be a very fascinating sort of

man."



"more so than you?"



"oh, I'm not fascinating."



"you're very modest, at all event."



"do you think so? wait till you hear I tell shoot story about my

prowess."



"be that your special weakness?"



"by no mean--you be."



"thank you for a very pretty compliment, but I'm afraid this

conversation be become frivolous," say Alizon, with a faint pink

colour creep into the pallor of her cheek, "however, it's end

now, for here come your friend."



"well late than never," remark Guy, turn round to salute his

cousin, who advance along the terrace, follow by Otterburn. "how do

you do, Eustace?"



"quite well, thank you Guy," reply Eustace, gravely shake hand.

"this be Mr. Macjean--my cousin, Sir Guy Errington."



"glad to see you, Mr. Macjean," say Errington bluffly, "and now let

I introduce both you gentleman to my wife, Lady Errington. Alizon,

this be my cousin Eustace and Mr. Macjean."



Lady Errington bow with a charming smile, and the whole party,

sit down, proceed to make themselves comfortable.









CHAPTER IV.

the art of CONVERSATION.



     "it's difficult to hold a conversation

         with three or five, odd number be a bore,

      for some one's sure to be _san_ occupation,

         so talk should always be 'twixt two or four.

      one cann't gain any secret information,

         if there should be a single person more:

      but four's a pleasant number without doubt,

      because there's not a chance to be the 'odd man out.'"





it be certainly a very pleasant little party which be seat on the

terrace of the Villa Tagni, talk social nonsense under the clear

glow of the sunset sky. behind the solemn hill the sun have

disappear, leave the sky fill with soft rosy tint, against

which the serrate outline of tall peak stand clear and distinct.

Slender cloud of liquid gold float in the roseate western sky which

resemble in its pale flush the delicate tint of a rose-heart,

soften off by degree into a cold blue, which in its turn give

place towards the darkening east to faint shadow and throb star

glimmering in the aerial gloom of come night.



but the four people on the terrace take no notice of the wonderful

gradation of colour, but chat gaily over the cake and tea

provide by the hospitality of Villa Tagni. all the gentleman, tired

of the thin wine of Italy, have take tea, and otterburn be

especially enthusiastic as he drain his cup with keen relish.



"I'm a perfect old woman for tea here," he say, hand back his cup

for a second supply. "a don't know why, as I never bother much about

it at home."



"that's because you cann't get a decent cup here," observe Eustace

drily, "man always long for the impossible."



"I long for a decent dinner," retort Otterburn with a hollow groan.

"I'm not a particularly greedy sort of chap--don't laugh, please, Lady

Errington, I assure you I'm not--but these Italians haven't the

slight idea how to cook."



"well you see their idea of cooking differ from your, Mr. Macjean,"

say Alizon, smilingly hand he back his cup.



"yes, that's true enough. I daresay they give a fellow the good they

can, but look at their victual; bread that's all full of hole, some

yellow mess they call polenta, skinny chicken and sour wine, you

cann't make a square meal of such stuff."



"some people could," say Errington, who be listen to the boy's

remark with an amused smile, "but I agree with you about the roast

beef of old England."



"or the wholesome parritch of Scotland," observe Eustace satirically.

"as a North Briton you surely forget that, Master."



"no, I don't," retort Macjean. "I get too much of that when I be

young."



"be so aged now."



"ben't that shabby?" say otterburn good-humouredly, turn to Lady

Errington. "he's always make fun of my age--as if youth be a

crime."



"it's a very charming crime at all event," reply Alizon pleasantly;

"don't you mind Mr. Gartney, he be a poet, and poet be always

praise--and envying--youth."



that's true enough, say Eustace with a sigh, "all the poet from

Mimnurmus downward have ever lament the passing of youth. what a

pity we cann't always remain young."



"and why not? I don't count age by year, but by experience," say

Lady Errington quietly. "one may be old at twenty and young at fifty."



Eustace, know what her experience have be, look curiously at her

fair placid face as she say this, and she must have guess his

thought, for a flush burn in her cheek under his searching gaze.



"that's what I say," cry Guy, refer to his wife's remark. "if a

fellow's get health, wealth and a good temper, the world's a very

jolly sort of place."



"the good of all possible world, accord to Voltaire," remark

Eustace, lean back with a disbelieving smile, "but you've leave out

one ingredient which some people consider very necessary."



"and that is----?"



"love!"



"Ab, I've get that," say Guy turn a fond eye on his wife.



"lucky man, other people be not so fortunate."



"no," observe otterburn with a huge sigh, having finish a very

decent meal, "it's so difficult to procure the genuine article."



"Hark to the cynic of one-and-twenty," cry Gartney.



"it's your example, Eustace," observe Guy, produce a cigarette

case, "but don't for Heaven's sake start a philosophical discussion on

happiness. why should the child of the king go mourn when the

soothing weed be within reach? have a cigarette, Macjean."



"thank you--if Lady Errington----"



"oh, I do not mind. Guy have habituate I to smoke. light your

cigarette by all mean."



Whereupon Otterburn accept the small roll of paper and tobacco with

much satisfaction, and be soon puff away contentedly, Guy

follow his example.



"these be jolly good cigarette," he say emphatically. "you cann't

get decent tobacco in Italy, so I smuggle these past the Customs at

Chiasso. I suppose it's no use offer you one, Eustace?"



"not in the least," respond Gartney smile. "it's a pity to spoil

this perfect fragrance with tobacco smoke."



"ah, that's so like you poet--always sacrifice the comfort of life

for the sake of its illusion. well, we willn't spoil your esthetic

feeling on the subject, Come, Macjean, let we leave these two to

continue the conversation, and we'll walk up and down till we finish

our smoke."



Angus glance enquiringly at Lady Errington, who smilingly give the

requisite permission, and be soon stroll up and down the terrace

with Errington, talk sport, upon which subject both gentleman be

quite in accordance.



leave alone with Lady Errington, Eustace lie back in his deep chair

gaze dreamily at she as she sit silent and pensive, fan herself

slowly with an absent expression in her blue eye.



the charm of the scene, the influence of the hour, the presence of

this pale, beautiful woman, and the delicate fragrance of the flower

which permeate the still air, all touch the poetical part of his

nature, and he could not help wonder in his own mind how such a

spiritual nature as that of Alizon Errington's could tolerate such a

matter-of-fact man as her husband, who could leave she so calmly to

talk sport with a shallow-minded boy. in this, however, Eustace

Gartney be entirely wrong, as love be not to be measure by

sentimental talk or silent adoration, and a man who love a woman in

an honest respectful fashion do not need to be constantly on his

knee to prove the sincerity of his passion. but then Eustace, who

believe in this exaggerated fashion of love-making, be a poet, and

poet have whimsical way of manifest their sentiment.



from these musing he be arouse by the voice of his hostess, who have

suddenly awaken to the fact that Eustace be silent, and fear she

have neglect her social duty.



"you be singularly silent, Mr. Gartney!"



Eustace start suddenly as her voice strike on his ear, and look

idly at she with a vague smile on his lip.



"the influence of the hour and the scene, I suppose," he say idly;

"one be always silent in Paradise."



"I should think that depend upon the absence or presence of Eve,"

reply Alizon demurely.



"or of the serpent. Confess now, Lady Errington, the serpent be a

charming conversationalist."



"and a bad companion--for a woman."



"no doubt Adam think so--after the Fall."



"what a pity there should have be a fall," say Lady Errington after

a short pause. "it would have be a charming world."



"Humph! consist of what the French call a _solitude à deux_."



"oh, but I be suppose the Garden of Eden became populate. it would

have be a world without sin or temptation."



"I beg 'your pardon. the tree of knowledge and life would still have

be flourish to tempt the primeval population nor do I suppose the

wily serpent would have be want."



"satirical, but scarcely true."



"ah, but you see we're both talk the romance of

what-might-have-be," say Gartney smile, "so my view of the

subject be no doubt as probable as your own. however this italian

Paradise with all its fault, consequent on our present-day

civilization, have exquisite scenery, and if one be to live here for

some year I daresay he would arrive at the near approach to

primeval happiness possible in this world."



"I'm afraid we shall not have an opportunity of test the truth of

your assertion. we leave here in a fortnight, for Guy be long for

England and the country."



"a nostalgia of the covert, I presume?"



"exactly! 'it's a fine day, let we go and kill something.'"



Eustace laugh at this reply, as the neatness of it satisfy his

somewhat cynical sense of humour.



"don't you feel nostalgia yourself, Mr. Gartney?" ask Lady

Errington, arrange the lily at her breast.



he turn his expressive face towards she with a sad smile.



"not of this earth! I be like Heine, _un enfant perdu_, and have a

home-sickness for an impossible world."



"create by your own fancy no doubt."



"yes! though I dare say if my fancy world become a real one it

wouldn't be so pleasant as this one. after all, Chance be the most

admirable architect of the future. when man like Sir Thomas more,

Plato, Bulwer Lytton and the rest of they, have indulge in paper

dream of ideal world, they have always commit the fatal mistake

of make the inhabitant insufferable bore, who have attain

perfection--and when perfection be attain happiness cease."



"how so?"



"because the great pleasure in life be work, and when perfection

render work unnecessary, life become a lotos-eat existence."



"well surely that be a very pleasant thing."



"to the few yes, to the many no! some man need constant excitement to

make they enjoy life. I can quite understand Xerxes offer a reward

to the man who could invent a new pleasure, for if Xerxes have not

attain the perfection of debauchery, he would not have find

existence a bore."



"you can hardly call such an ignoble height perfection," say Lady

Errington quietly. "I should call it satiety."



"no doubt you be right. but what do it matter what we call it? the

thing be the same."



"that sound as if you speak from experience, and at your age that can

hardly be the case."



"I remember," observe Eustace a trifle satirically, "that a short

time ago you say you measure youth by experience not by age. it be

the same with I, I be only thirty eight year of age, yet in that

short time I have exhaust all that life have to give."



"surely not!"



Eustace Gartney laugh in a dreary, hopeless manner that show how

truly he speak.



"I'm afraid it be," he remark with a sigh. "I have be all over the

world and see what be to be see. I have mix with my fellow

creature and find the majority of they humbug. I've be in love

and be deceive. I've publish book and be abuse, in fact I've

do everything possible to enjoy life, and the consequence be I'm

sick of the whole thing."



"your own fault entirely," say Lady Errington warmly, "as you have

deny yourself nothing you now reap the reward of such indulgence and

enjoy nothing. your present satiety be the logical sequence of your

own act. why not therefore try and lead a nobler and well life? go

among the poor and give they the help they so much need. look upon

your fortune as money entrust to you, not to squander on

unsatisfying pleasure, but to use for the benefit of humanity. do

this, Mr. Gartney, and I assure you the result will be satisfactory,

for you will find in such well-do the new pleasure which Xerxes

desire but never obtain."



with a sceptical smile on his massive feature Eustace listen to her

earnest speech, and at its conclusion laugh softly in his own

cynical manner.



"a most delightful view of one's duty to the world at large," he

say satirically, "but hardly satisfactory. that recipe for happiness

have be give to I before, Lady Errington, and be, I think, more

charming in theory than in practice. suppose I do take this advice

you give I in the goodness of your heart, and go out into the

world to play the thankless part of a philanthropist, what would I

gain--only a more intimate knowledge of human selfishness and human

iniquity. if I assist A, a most deserving person from his own point

of view, I've no doubt a would become my bitter enemy because I have

not do enough for he. I might rescue b from the workhouse, and b

would consider I shabby if I do not support he for the rest of his

natural life. as for C, well, I need not go through the whole

alphabet, in order to illustrate my view of the matter, but I assure

you, Lady Errington, if I employ my money in alleviate the

distress of my fellow creature, I would get very little praise and

a great deal of blame during my life, and when I die no doubt a short

paragraph in a newspaper as 'an earnest but misguided philanthropist!'

no! believe I I have think deeply about the whole thing, and the

game be not worth the candle."



"you look at thing in a wrong light."



"in the only possible light, I'm afraid. rose-colour spectacle be

not obtainable now-a-day."



"still such a pessimistic view----"



"be force upon we by circumstance. this be the nineteenth century,

you know, and we have no illusion leave--they go out with religion."



"well, I must try and convince you of the falsity of your view some

other time," say Alizon close her fan with a sigh, "but at present

I see Guy and Mr. Macjean be come to interrupt our conversation."



she rise to her foot as she speak, a tall, slim, white figure, that

seem to sway like a graceful lily at the breath of the evening

breeze. eustace, ever prone to poetical impression, make this

comparison in his own mind as he leave his chair and advance with she

to meet Guy and Angus.



"I say Alizon," cry Errington gaily as his wife come up, "just

fancy! Aunt Jelly's ward, Miss Sheldon, be stay at the Villa

Medici."



"Miss Sheldon," say Lady Errington reflectively, "be that the pretty

girl I meet at Miss Corbin's?"



"yes! you remember. on the day we go to see Aunt Jelly and ask she

blessing," reply Guy eagerly.



"who be she with?" ask Lady Errington; "surely Miss Corbin----"



"oh no," interrupt Eustace, mirthfully. "you might as well expect to

meet the Monument abroad as Aunt Jelly. I ask Miss Sheldon all about

it, and it appear that ever since her arrival from Australia she have

be anxious to come to the continent, so as a friend of Aunt Jelly's

be make what she call the 'grand tower' with her husband, this

young lady be place under their mutual protection."



"I wish she be under mine," say Otterburn audibly, on whom the

charm of the young lady in question have evidently make a deep

impression, "she's so awfully pretty."



"I'm afraid it would be a case of the blind lead the blind,"

remark Eustace drily.



"by the way," observe Guy, "who be Miss Sheldon? I ask Aunt Jelly,

but she tell I, sharply, to mind my own business."



"ben't that rather severe?" say Alizon mildly.



"not for Aunt Jelly," retort her husband. "Aunt Jelly's a huffy old

party, but she's get a weakness for Eustace, who don't object to be

sit upon, so perhaps he know about this young lady."



"I think I've get a hazy idea," assent Eustace leisurely, "she come

from the City of Melbourne, Australia, and her name be Victoria,

call after our gracious Queen, or the Colony, I forget which.

Sheldon _père_ be an admirer of our mutual aunt in the old day when

she be flesh and blood instead of iron. he go out with a break

heart to the Colonies because Aunt Jelly wouldn't marry he--fancy any

man break his heart for such a brazen image! well, at all event,

he make a large fortune out there, get marry, become the father of

one little girl, and then, his life's work be do, die, leave

his fortune to his daughter Victoria, and his daughter Victoria to

dear Aunt Jelly, who cherish she for the sake of the one romance of

her youth."



"how cruelly you tell the story," observe Lady Errington in a rather

disapprove tone. "I've only see Miss Corbin once, but I think she's

get a kind heart."



"Most people be say to have that, who possess nothing else,"

retort Eustace grimly. "however, you now know who Victoria Sheldon

be, and I willn't deny she's pretty, very pretty."



"very pretty," echo Otterburn, with a sigh.



"you ought to marry she, Macjean," say Eustace, "she have plenty of

money."



"I wouldn't marry a girl for her money alone," remonstrate Angus

indignantly.



"then take the american advice," say Sir Guy gaily, "never marry a

girl for money, but if you do meet a nice girl with any, try and love

she as hard as ever you can."



"I think I'll call and see Miss Sheldon," observe Alizon, after a

pause, "for, as she be a ward of your Aunt's, I shall very likely see

a good deal of she. be the people she be with pleasant?"



"that," observe Eustace calmly, "depend greatly on individual taste.

the honourable Henry Trubbles be the most egotistical speciman of

misshapen humanity I have ever meet with, and his wife, whom he marry

for her money, be a modern edition of Mrs. Malaprop with a dash of

Sary Gamp and a flavouring of the Sleeping Beauty."



"what a mixed description," say Errington laugh. "how do she

resemble the Sleeping beauty?"



"only in sleep."



"you make I quite curious to see she," cry Alizon smile. "and

if--well, I willn't promise anything about what I intend yet."



"what do you intend?" ask her husband.



"to have a small dinner party, and give Mr. Macjean a real english

dinner, but I'll first see how I like this extraordinary couple, and

then--well, we'll see."



"it would be awfully jolly," say Otterburn, whose stock of adjective

be limited.



"I know it's 'awfully' late," remark Eustace, in a tone of rebuke,

"and we have just time to get back to dinner."



"to what they call a dinner."



"it's well than nothing at all event--well, goodbye, Lady

Errington; thank you for a pleasant afternoon."



"don't forget your way to the Villa Tagni," say Alizon as she shake

hand, and the two gentleman, having vow warmly that they would not,

make their adieux, leave Sir Guy and his wife alone on the terrace.



"well, Alizon," say Errington, jocularly, "and what do you think of

my cousin, Eustace?"



"I think," reply Lady Errington slowly, "that he be the most unhappy

man I ever meet with in my life."









CHAPTER V.

an australian girl.



     "Charming, no doubt, her face be fair.

      as dark as night, her curl hair,

      her eye--two star, her lip--a rose,

      whoever see a pretty nose?

      Charming indeed,--but Fate to vex,

      have give her fault like all her sex,

      believe I, she's not worth regret,

      she'll break your heart, the vain coquette."





what a number of charming old romance begin at an inn. do not M. Gil

Blas commence his adventurous career by be swindle in one? and Don

Quixote, blind by fanatic chivalry, mistake the inn for mediæval

castle? Tom Jones become involved in a network of intrigue at a

hostelry; the hero of Dumas invariably meet their enemy of King

and Cardinal at the same place, while Boccaccio generally bring about

the complication of gallant and donzella at some gay florentine

"osteria." without doubt all the element of romance be to be find

at these rest place of man and beast; and the most incongruous

character, the most dissimilar rank of society's adventurer,

gallant, priest, bona robas and virtuous lady all pass and repass,

enter and exeunt, under the hospitable sign of inn.



bird of passage rest momentarily at inn before continue their

flight to the four quarter of the world, and during such rest meet

other bird of passage with sometimes curious result. Mr. A, a

gentleman of swallow-like tendency, on his way to the warm south,

may linger for a night at an hotel where Miss B, due in some northern

latitude, be also rest, with the result that Mr. A will delay his

flight for an indefinite period; nay more, the juxtaposition of the

two may end in A and b both continue their journey as man and wife,

which be the termination of all romance. strange that a chance meeting

at a place of public resort should alter two life, but then life be

make up of strange event, and a good many people date their happiness

or misery from an accidental meeting at an inn.



Gartney be let his thought run on in this somewhat whimsical

vein, as he smoke an after dinner cigarette over his coffee on the

terrace at Villa Medici.



before he, huge and indistinct, arise the grand façade of the hotel,

glimmer whitely in the moonlight, with its innumerable window,

its broad arcade, and its myriad lamp shine brilliantly on

group of gaily-dress people who stroll to and fro amongst the

pink-blossom oleander, or sit chat gaily round small

marble-top table, where white-cravate waiter, lithe and active,

attend to their wish.



beyond lie the lake, dark and solemn, under the shadow of the sombre
