mountain, at whose base gleam orange-coloured point of light,

telling of the presence of distant village, while high above in the

cold, blue sky, glow the yellow orb of the moon and the glimmering

star. through the leave of sycamore, tamarisk, and magnolia sigh

the soft breath of the night-wind, fill the air with cool odour,

and the sound of music, render thin and fairy-like by distance,

float across the still water from some slow-move boat.



an historic place this Villa Medici, with its palatial hall, its

innumerable chamber, and its stately flight of white-marble step;

for it be here that the great Emperor intend to rest for a time in

his victorious career, an intention never carry out, although

everything be prepare for his reception, and the hotel guest now

dine in the small saloon hung round with yellow damask stamp with

the imperial 'N' and kingly crown.



then again it be here that unhappy Caroline of Brunswick, who become

Queen of England in name only, keep her state as Princess of Wales,

and try to find in the calm seclusion of Como that peace deny to

she in the land of her adoption. ah, yes, the Villa Medici be

connect with the life of some great personage, but now that they

all have vanish from the world's stage, whereon they play some

curious part, the Villa be turn into an hotel, and stranger from

far America, and still far Australia, reside in the many chamber,

and wander with delight through the enchanting garden which nature,

aid by art, have make a paradise of beauty.



"Poor Caroline," murmur Gartney to himself, as he think of all

this, "no one have a good word to say for she, and yet, I daresay, she

be a good deal well than the first gentleman in Europe. it be just

as well she die, for George would never have give she any right as

queen-consort. no doubt she pass some of her happy day here,

although she always hanker after the forbidden glory of Windsor

and Buckingham Palace."



his meditation be interrupt at this point by a gay laugh, and on

look up he see Victoria Sheldon come towards he escort by the

Master of Otterburn, who be evidently tell she some funny story,

judge from the amusement his conversation seem to afford she.



she be certainly a very pretty girl, one of those feminine beauty

who strike the beholder at first sight with a sense of indescribable

charm. a brilliantly tint brunette, overflow with exuberant

vitality, she have all the intense colouring and freshness of a

southern rise at that time when the cold rain draw its perfume

strongly forth in the chill morning air.



her eye, hair, eyebrow and long lash be dark as night; red as

coral the lip, which when part show two row of pearly tooth;

full and soft the round of the cheek, and a peach-like skin with a

rosy glow of delicate colour under the velvety surface. she be the

modern realization of that vivacious Julia whom Herrick describe so

charmingly in his dainty poem. and as a matter of fact the skin of

this young girl have all the brilliant colouring of the south, no doubt

assimilate by her system under the sultry glow of australian sky.

have an excellent figure, dainty hand and foot, with a perfect

taste in dress, and boundless vivacity, there be no doubt that

Victoria Sheldon be a feminine personality eminently attractive to

the strong sex.



as to her nature, it be quite in unison with her outward

appearance--bright, sparkling, vivacious, albeit somewhat shallow, yet

not without a certain veneer of surface knowledge. eminently womanly,

capricious in the extreme, witty, amusing, tireless, she have one of

those attractive nature which charm everyone in a singularly magnetic

fashion. some man, eccentric in their liking, admire those

semi-masculine woman who have mission, support the right of their

sex on lecture platform, emulate masculine peculiarity to the good

of their ability, and pass noisy life in shriek aimlessly

against the tyranny of mankind. those man who approve of such

semi-masculine tendency, certainly would not have admire the

womanly characteristic of Victoria, but the connoisseur of feminine

beauty, the judge of a brilliant personality, and the appreciator of a

witty nature, would each see in she the realisation of an extremely

difficult ideal.



the Master, young and rash, be just at that delightful age when every

woman appear a goddess to the uncultured fancy of youth; judge then

the effect produce upon his impressionable nature by this riant

vision of strongly vitalise beauty. he do not even make an attempt

at resistance in any way, but prone as god Dagon on the threshold of

his temple, he fall before the powerful divinity of this young girl,

and she produce on he the same effect as Phryne do on her judge

when she display the full splendour of her charm in the Areopagus

under the clear blue of athenian sky. Mactab, severe, ascetic and

self-mortifying, oppose to every form of admiration of the flesh,

would have blush for the grovel idolatory of his quondam pupil;

but no doubt the sunny climate of Italy aid in a great measure this

worship of Venus, and Angus Macjean, Master of Otterburn, prostrate

himself in abject worship before this outward manifestation of carnal

beauty.



Eustace see this, and be selfishly annoyed thereat, because he have

take a fancy to otterburn, and think that he (otterburn) should

agree with he (Eustace) in despise the sex feminine, which be

foolish in the extreme on the part of such an acute observer of human

nature; but then he be blind by egotism, and that vice distort

every vision. still he could not deny that physically she be

wonderfully pretty, despite his feeling of animosity against she for

come between himself and his friend. therefore he admire she

greatly from an æsthetic point of view, while Victoria, with the keen

instinct of a woman, scent an enemy and neither admire nor like

Eustace the cynic in the small degree.



"my dear Mr. Macjean," she say in answer to the remonstrance of

Angus who want everyone to like his friend as much as he do

himself. "your friend be a pessimist, and I don't like that class of

people; they always take a delight in analyse one's motive, which

be disagreeable--to the person concern. a flower be charming, but

those who pull it to piece in order to find out how it be make--be

not. I don't like analyst--they destroy one's illusion."



this plain-speak young lady's chaperone be enjoy an after-dinner

nap; the Hon. Henry be talk irish politic with an Irish M.P., who

do not believe in Home Rule out of contradiction to the rest of his

countryman who do. so Victoria Sheldon, feel in a most delightful

humour, be chat gaily with Otterburn, when they thus chance on

the melancholy Eustace, moralise on the mutability of human life.



"a penny for your thought, Mr. Gartney," say Victoria, pause

before he with a gay smile on her lip.



"they're not worth it," reply Eustace, look approvingly at the

charming girl before he, in her dainty white dinner dress, with a

bunch of vividly scarlet geranium at her breast. "I'll sell they as

bankrupt stock."

     "my heart be dead,

      and pleasure hath flee,

      but the rise you give I bloom fresh and red."

dangerous ground again.

a large, fat, good-natured woman be Mrs. Trubbles, with a broad red

how dirty the street be! I'm afraid they haven't get a Board of

Works. what do this man say?--Bill something--who be he talk to?"



"Biglietti," explain Victoria, as they pause at the gangway.

"ticket--you've get they, Mr. Macjean."



"yes, here they be," say Angus, and, hand they to the officer in

charge, they go ashore.



"what little man," say Victoria, catch sight of some of the

military, "they look like tin soldier."



"they don't seem very well feed," observe Mrs. Trubbles meditatively;

"I don't think the food be good--very bad quality, I'm afraid. dear

I, there's a fountain."



"it's more like a squirt," say otterburn laughing.



"plenty of water about this place," pursue Mrs. Trubbles, put up

her eyeglass, "but I don't think these foreigner make enough use of

it. oh, dear! dear! what a dreadful smell, they really ought to look

after the drain well. I'm so afraid of typhoid. Mr. Macjean, would

you mind smoking?--it's safe."



Mr. Macjean be only too delighted, and having light a cigarette,

be soon blow wreath of smoke as they all walk up one of the

narrow street, on their way to the Duomo.



"we must do the church, you know," remark Angus with great gravity,

"it's the big lion of Como--build by some one call Roderer or

Rodari--I'm not certain about the name. sound like a champagne brand,

don't it? it be build somewhere about the thirteenth or fourteenth

century--I'm not sure which."



"you don't seem very sure of anything beyond the fact that there be a

church," say Miss Sheldon disparagingly, "and as it's straight before

you, we can be certain it exist. they say it's all build of white

marble."



"it don't look like it then," remark Mrs. Trubbles emphatically,

"a good coat of paint wouldn't hurt it."



"oh, that would spoil it," chorus both the young people, whereupon

Mrs. Trubbles shake her head, and hold firmly to her original

suggestion.



having admire the ornate front, with its delicate Renaissance

carving they go out of the burn sunshine into the cool twilight

of the cathedral.



some service be go on as they enter, and in the dim distance

they see the high altar glitter with gold and silver ornament,

beneath gorgeous drapery of yellow damask depend from the

ceiling, and innumerable taper flare like beautiful glitter star

against the brilliant background.



number of worshipper, with bent head, be kneel on the chill

marble pavement, tell their bead, or silently move their lip in

prayer, while a priest in splendid vestment, attend by a long train

of white-robe acolyte, officiate at the altar, and at interval the

melodious thunder of the organ break through the monotonous voice of

the choir. placid-look image of saint, dusky picture of the

Virgin throne amid the hierarchy of heaven, before which burn the

lambent flame of slender white candle, many-colour tapestry

represent biblical scene, heavy gold brocade hanging,

elaborately-carve shrine and the sudden flash of precious metal and

strangely-set jewel appear in every nook and corner of the immense

building, while from the silver censer of the acolyte arise the

drowsy incense, in white cloud of sensuous perfume, towards the

gild splendour of the huge dome. here, from the lofty roof, the rapt

face of evangelist, saint, angel and virgin, look gravely

downward; there, slender shafts of sunlight, stream in through the

paint window, tint the white monument of the dead with rainbow

hue, and under all this subdue splendour of colour and beauty,

soften by the dusky twilight, kneel a mixed congregation.

Bare-foot _contadini_ from distant hill village, devoutly tell

their bead next to some dark-visage soldier in all the bravery of

military trapping, and delicately beautiful lady, array in the

late milanese fashion, kneel beside bare-breast peasant with

sinewy figure full of the lithe grace and suppress fierceness of

italian manhood.



"I wonder what Mactab would say to all this?" mutter Otterburn

involuntarily, as he think of the severe humility and bareness of

the Kirk o' Tabbylugs.



"who be Mactab?" ask Victoria in a subdue whisper. Angus chuckle

quietly.



"do I never tell you of Mactab?" he whispered--"oh! I  must. he's a

prominent minister of the Free Kirk, of the severe principle."



"what be his principle?"



"eh! what? oh, he haven't get any principal! he's a Free Kirk, I tell

you. all this heathenish worship would make he take a fit. he

believe in nothing, not even an organ, so the Mactab congregation

sing dreadfully out of tune, but they make up for this by strength of

lung. they could give that wheezy old 'kist o' whustle' fit in

psalmody."



at this moment Mrs. Trubbles, who have be gaze complacently about

she with the same sort of interest as she would have take in a

theatre, intimate that she have see enough, and lead the way out into

the hot sunshine.



"I'm rather tired of church," say the matron in her deep voice

"we've see such a lot of they in France."



"oh, France ben't in it with Italy in that line," observe Angus, in

his slangy way. "there be more church than public-house here."



"well, that's a very good thing," reply Victoria.



"I should think so, consider how thin the wine be," retort

Macjean, pause before a variegated kind of arcade; "but look

here--this be the market."



"oh, how pretty!" cry Victoria, note the picturesque colouring of

the different pile of fruit--"just like a scene out of Romeo and

Juliet."



"and there be Juliet say the Master wickedly, wave his stick in the

direction of a ponderous female who be lean from a project iron

balcony chattering to a lady below with shrill volubility over some

skinny-look poultry.



"Juliet in her old age buy Romeo's dinner," reply Victoria,

serenely. "don't, please, take the romance out of everything."



"no; I leave that to Gartney."



"Horrid man!" say the girl, viciously; "he would disillusionise an

angel."



"there be one or two thing, my dear Victoria," observe Mrs.

Trubbles at this moment--"there be one or two thing I should like to

take home with I as a kind of mementum of Italy. a fan or a

shell-box--you know, dear; a box with 'a Present from Como' on it.

now, what be the italian for 'a Present from Como'?"



"I'm sure I don't know," say Miss Sheldon, suppress a smile.

"however, here's an old curiosity shop. let we go in and spy out the

land."



"I cann't talk the language myself," say Mrs. Trubbles, doubtfully, as

her bulky figure fill up the door, "but Victoria----"



"be much bad," interrupt that young lady, quickly. "I know French,

but not italian, except parrot-like in singing. now Mr. Macjean----"



"I'm bad of all," explain Otterburn, in the most brazen manner.

"'Questo e troppo' be all I know."



"Translate, please."



"it mean 'that be too much."



"a very good sentence to know," say the matron, decidedly. "I believe

these foreign people be rarely honest. I shall learn it--'Question he

troppus.' be that right?"



"not quite; only three word wrong. 'questo e troppo.'"



"'Questo e troppo,'" repeat Mrs. Trubbles, carefully. "what a pity

these foreigner don't learn English. it's so much well than their

own gibberish."



"I'm afraid we'll have to go in for the primitive language of sign,"

cry Victoria gaily, as they stand in front of the diminutive counter

behind which a smile Italian be gesticulate politely.



it would take a long time to describe the difficulty of that

shopping. how the shopkeeper, assist by his tragic-look wife,

rave wildly in Italian, and his three customer endeavour vainly to

find out what they both mean. sometimes one person would speak, then

the other four would join in, the most powerful voice take the lead.

what with "Gran' Dio's" and "Per Bacco's" from the seller, and

"Basta, basta," "Questo e troppo," and "Si, si" from the buyer, the

whole transaction be quite operatic in character.



Mrs. Trubbles' system of shopping be very simple.



when the shopkeeper say two lira, she reply one; if he request

five, she offer four, always keep the price down, be convince

in her own mind that these foreigner be try to swindle she, an

idea abhorrent to her sturdy british spirit.



"I've get a conversation book somewhere," she say at last, fish in

a capacious pocket; "it's get question in three language."



"and the truth in none," observe Angus, _sotto voce_.



"oh, here it be!" exclaim Mrs. Trubbles, produce a kind of

pamphlet. "here, Mister Signor," hold up an olive-wood

paper-cutter, "Wie viel."



a shrug of the shoulder and a gesture of dismay from the shopkeeper,

who do not understand German.



"why, he don't know his own language!" say Mrs. Trubbles, with

great contempt. "they need a School Board here."



"I think," suggest Victoria, who be suffocate with laughter, "I

think you be talk german."



"dear! dear! you don't say so?" say the lady meekly, somewhat after

the fashion of M. Jourdain, who have talk prose for year and do not

know it. "yes, quite right. these book be so muddling. where's the

italian? oh, here; 'Quanto, quanto?'" shake the paper-cutter

frantically. "Quanto, signor?"



"tre lire."



"bother the man! I'm not talk about a tray!" cry Mrs. Trubbles,

in an exasperate tone. "Here!--this! use your eye. paper-cutter.

'Papero cuttero. Quanto?'"



"tre lire, signora."



"he mean three franc," explain Victoria.



"oh, do he. I'll give he two."



"Questo e troppo," say Otterburn, bring forward his only bit of

italian with great ostentation. "two--due--lira, signor. ah, che la

morte."



"no, no," from the shopkeeper, "non e molto."



"now what do that mean?" cry the matron, refer to her

text-book. "here it be: 'not much,'--si, si; far too much, too molto,

due--due lira," produce they triumphantly from her purse.



with many deprecate shrug and asseveration in fluent Italian that

such a sale would ruin he, the shopkeeper at last accept the two

lira, and Mrs. Trubbles with great satisfaction secure what she

want. they then buy a few more thing by pursue the same system

of beat down the price, and all three ultimately leave the shop

with the firm conviction that they have secure bargain, which they

decidedly have not.



"these pig of English," observe the astute shopkeeper to his wife,

"always talk a lot, but they pay in the end."



then the three innocent abroad wander aimlessly through the narrow

street, see the statue of the great electrician, Volta, the ruin

battlement, the church of St. Abbondio, and other object of

interest. afterwards they have some refreshment at a café, the

proprietor of which Mrs. Trubbles, who be a spendthrift in London

but a miser abroad, denounce as robber, and then be fortunate

enough to catch a steamer just start for Cernobbio.



"oh dear! dear!" moan Mrs. Trubbles, with a weary sigh, as she sit

down in a comfortable seat--"what with their language, their lie, and

their nobby-stone street, I'm quite worn out."



"I think one visit be quite enough for Como," say Victoria, as the

town recede into the far distance. "when do we leave this place, Mrs.

Trubbles?"



"in a week, dear," murmur the lady in a sleepy tone. "my husband

will get all his politic settle by that time, I hope."



"I hope so, too. I'm tired of the lake."



"don't say that," say Otterburn, reproachfully; "I'll be sorry to

leave the Villa Medici."



"you needn't. we can go; you can stay."



"I don't want to stay if you go."



clearly this obtuse young man be irrepressible, and as he be now

get on dangerous ground again, Victoria deftly turn the

conversation.



"I suppose we'll see you and Mr. Gartney at Rome?"



"oh, yes. will you be glad to see we?"



"perhaps. I don't like Mr. Gartney; I've tell you so a dozen time."



"then will you be glad to see I?" demand Otterburn, boldly.



Victoria look at he mischievously, with a dangerous gleam in her

dark eye, then lower her sunshade with a laugh, she turn

abruptly away.



"I shall be glad when we arrive at the Villa Medici," she say,

lightly; "I'm so hungry."



how on earth be a young man to make love to such a capricious girl?









CHAPTER VII.

LADY ERRINGTON'S LITTLE DINNER.



     "an alien race beneath an alien sky,

      amid strange tongue, and face strange alone,

      Stout English heart who for the moment try

      to form a little England of their own."





after the constant sight of dark italian face, and the everlasting

clatter of restless italian tongue, the guest at the Villa Tagni

find it pleasant to form part of an English circle once more, to eat

an English dinner, to discuss english subject and compare everything

british to the disadvantage of all thing Continental. so great a

delight do these six people take in meet one another at a

hospitable dinner-table that one would have think they have be for

year exile in the centre of Africa, and far remove from all

civilizing influence. Heaven only know there be no lack of English

tourist on the continent, but then to a great extent they preserve

their insular stiffness towards one another; consequently when people

meet in foreign part, who have a slight acquaintance at home, they

rush into one another's arm with tender affection, though they would

mutually consider one another insufferable bore during the London

season.



this, however, be not the case with Lady Errington's guest, who be

all genuinely delighted with one another, and chat gaily on

different kind of subject as if they have be bosom friend all

their life. the Hon. Henry have be invite on account of his wife,

who in her turn have be invite on account of Victoria, but having

go to Milan to see an italian Count who have all the complication of

european politic at his finger' end, he telegraph the sad news

that he would not be able to be present, at which Lady Errington be

secretly very glad, as an extra man would have quite upset the balance

of the party.



as it be, Sir Guy take in the portly Mrs. Trubbles to dinner, his

wife be escort by Eustace, and the Master of Otterburn realise the

wish of his heart by act as cavalier to Miss Sheldon. so thing

be thus pleasantly arrange, they all sit round the well spread

table as merry a party as it would be possible to find.



in some mysterious manner Lady Errington have manage to provide a

series of english dish, to which all present do ample justice, not

that anyone be particularly a gourmand, but italian cookery be a

trifle monotonous and a real english dinner in Italy be something to

be appreciate. at all event, what with the food, the wine, and the

continuous strain of light badinage, all the guest be in a state of

the high good humour, and even the pessimistic Gartney deign to

take a moderately charitable view of thing.



"this be jolly and no mistake," say Otterburn, as the servant fill

his glass with champagne, "you need to go abroad to appreciate home

comfort."



"I think you would appreciate they anywhere," remark Eustace the

cynic.



"and quite right too," chime in Miss Sheldon, with a gay laugh,

"everybody do, only they don't like to confess it."



"why not?" demand Sir Guy.



Victoria look rather nonplussed for the moment, having make an idle

statement without think she would be call upon to give she

reason.



"oh, I don't know," she reply, after some hesitation. "I suppose

people like to be think romantic, and think about what you eat

and drink ben't romantic."



"it's very sensible at all event," say Lady Errington; "do you not

agree with I Mrs. Trubbles?"



"I do," reply the matron ponderously, nod her head, upon which

be perch a cheerful-look cap of black lace and glitter

bugle, "people should always eat and drink well at meal time,

but no nibblin's in between. it ben't nature to despise good food

well-cook. I've no patience with those gell who starve themselves

and pinch their waist to look pretty. wasp I call they."



"without the sting," suggest Sir Guy.



"that depend on their temper, and their temper," continue Mrs.

Trubbles impressively, "depend on their eating. give they good meal

and plenty of exercise, and there's the makin' of good wife about

they. let they starve themselves and lace tight, and it make their

nose red and their temper cross."



"the whole duty of woman then," murmur Eustace demurely, "be to

appreciate her cook and disobey her dressmaker. they might do the

first, but never the second."



Mrs. Trubbles, not understand irony, look doubtfully at Eustace

to see if he be smile, but so grave be the expression of his face

that she do not know whether he speak in jest or earnest, so without

make any reply, she continue her meal while the conversation become

frivolous and general.



"I think Italy a very over-rate place."



"really! in what respect--moral, scenery, manner?"



"no, as regard music. it's a very barrel-organy country."



"not more so than the London street. and after all, `ah che la

Morte,' be more musical than 'tommy make room for your uncle."



"both out of date."



"well, say Gounod's 'Romeo and Juliet' and the 'Boulanger March."



"yes, it's much jolly than the Op. 42 _andante adagio con fuoco

prestissimo_ sort of thing they give you at the Richter Concerts."



"Maclean," observed Eustace, gravely regard his glass, "you be a

Philistine, and classical music of the advanced school be throw away

on your uncultivated ear."



"no doubt! I prefer 'Auld Lang Syne' to Beethoven."



"naturally, be a Scotchman. you're like the man who know two tune.

one be 'God save the queen,' the other--wasn't."



"I remember," observe Mrs. Trubbles, whose idea of music be

primitive in the extreme, "that I go to a concert at St. James'

Hall, where they play something call a fuggy."



"a fugue," translate Victoria for the benefit of the company. "I

know! one tune start, a second catch it up. then a third join in,

and just as it successfully muddle up the other two, a fourth and a

fifth have their say in the matter."



"sound dreadfully mixed."



"then it sound exactly what it be," say Miss Sheldon promptly. "but

what about this particular fugue, Mrs. Trubbles?"



"the fugue, dear--yes, of course. there be a young man in front of I

wriggle dreadfully. I think he be uneasy about a pin, but he be

only show how pleased he be with the music, and keep call out

'oh this be food!'"



"want the bottle, I expect," say Eustace sweetly, "such musical

baby shouldn't be allow to go to classical concert. it's too much

for their nerve."



"it's too much for mine," remark otterburn grimly. "after dinner,"

say Gartney, look thoughtfully at he, "I shall play the

'Moonlight Sonata.'"



"in that case, Lady Errington, may I stay out on the terrace? such a

suggestion be inhuman."



Lady Errington laugh and give the signal to the lady, whereupon

they all arise to their foot.



"I'm afraid you're talk dreadful nonsense," she say, shake she

head.



"it's a poor heart that never rejoiceth," reply otterburn

impudently, as he open the door for the lady to depart.



follow the continental fashion, Sir Guy and his guest do not

linger long over their wine, but, after a few minute, go into the

drawing-room, whence they stroll on to the terrace for cigarette

and coffee.



Mrs. Trubbles, feel sleepy after her dinner, find a comfortable

chair in a distant corner of the room, and go placidly to sleep,

while the remain guest establish themselves on the terrace, the

gentleman with cigarette and the lady with coffee.



such a perfect night as it be. away in the distance, dense and black

against the cold, clear sky, frown the sombre masse of mountain,

above which hang in a cloudless firmament the silver shield of the

moon. here and there a liquid star throb in the deep heart of the

heaven, and overhead shine the misty splendour of the Milky Way; not

a breath of wind ruffle the still surface of the lake, which

reflect the serene beauty of the sky, but at interval across the

star-smite surface would move the dark, slim form of a boat, the

oar break the water into thousand of flash diamond.



far beyond glimmer the orange-colour light of Blevio, and the

sudden whiteness of some tall campanile shoot up in slender beauty

from amid its dark mass of surround house. a sense of perfect

fragrance in the still air, a charmed silence all around, and a

wondrous restful feeling under the cool magic of the night. then,

mellow by distance, faint and far like aerial music, the silver

tone of a peal of bell sound at interval through the clear

atmosphere, until the whole night seem full of sweet sound.



"this be the night when Diana kiss endymion," say Eustace dreamily,

"the antique deity which we all deny be still on earth in Italy.

they be not visible, nor will they ever be so save to the eye of

faith alone. even then they be doubtful of reveal themselves to a

generation who would put they under the microscope and on the

dissect table. but although we try hard to disbelieve in their

existence, the spell of their beauty be sometimes too strong, and I

never go anywhere among these hill without a secret hope of find

Pan asleep at noontide in the ilex shade, or of see the laugh

face of a Dryad frame in tamarisk leave."



"and your hope be never realise," say Lady Errington sadly; "that be

so true of our modern desire."



"because we always desire the impossible," reply Eustace, clasp

his hand over his knee while the chill moonlight fall on his massive

face, "and expect to find it in crowded city under the glare of

gaslight, instead of in these magic solitude where the moon shine on

haunt ground."



"but be it possible to reconcile man and nature?"



"accord to Matthew Arnold, yes."



"what a romantic way you have of look at thing, Mr. Gartney,"

remark Victoria with some impatience. "if everyone take your view of

life, I'm afraid the world would not get on."



"it's all humbug," cry Otterburn, who agree in every way with Miss

Sheldon, "that be, you know, not quite sensible."



"I daresay it be not--in a worldly sense," say Eustace bitterly, "but

then you see I don't look at everything from a purely utilitarian

point of view."



"I do" interpose Guy in his hearty british voice, "it's the only way

to get one's comfort in life. and one's comfort suggest smoking."



Otterburn assent with avidity, for they have be sit with

cigarette for some time, but never light up, and even Eustace

depart so much from his poetic dreaming as to accept the soothe

weed.



"you don't practise what you preach, Mr. Gurney," say Lady Errington,

smile.



"how many of we do?" ask Gartney complacently. "I'm afraid we talk a

lot and do nothing, now-a-day. it's the disease of the latter end of

the nineteenth century."



"oh, everything's very jolly," say Otterburn, who resemble Mark

Tapley in his disposition. "who be it say that this be the good of

all possible world?"



"Voltaire! but by that it be not his intention to infer he don't

yearn after some well world."



"Heaven!"



"I don't think that be in M. Arouet's line."



"I'm afraid it ben't in any of our line."



"what a rude remark," say Lady Errington severely. "this conversation

be become so atheistical that I must ask Mr. Gartney to carry out

his promise and play the Moonlight Sonata. it may inspire we with

high thought."



"the Como Moonlight Sonata--it will be a local hit."



"what nonsense you do talk, Macjean," say eustace rise to his foot

and throw his cigarette into the water, "you're like that man in

the Merchant of Venice."



"what man in the Merchant of Venice?"



"oh, if you don't know your shakespeare, I'm afraid I cann't teach it

to you," retort Eustace, and step lightly across the terrace, he

sit down at the piano, which be place near the window of the

drawing-room, and run his finger lightly over the ivory key. within

the party on the terrace could see the gleam of the marble floor, the

dull glitter of heavily embroider curtain, the faint reflection of

a mirror, and over all the rosy light of a red-shaded lamp the glare

of which stream out into the pale moonlight.



everyone sit silently in the wonderful mystic world create by the

magic of the moon, and from the piano a stream of melody, sad and

melancholy, in a minor key, break forth on the still night. the spell

of the shadow, the weirdness of the hour, and the presence of Lady

Errington, to whom he felt strangely draw, all have their influence on

Gartney's wonderfully impressionable nature, and he begin to improvise

delicate melody on a story suggest to he by the calm lake

gleam without.



"in the crystal depth of the blue lake," he chant in a dreamy

monotone, while the subtle harmony wove themselves under his long

lithe finger, "there dwell a beautiful fairy, in a wondrous palace.

she be in love with the nightingale who sing so sweetly from the

laurel that hang their green leave over the still water. the voice

of the hidden singer have strange power and tell she of the cool green

depth of the forest; of the rich perfume shake from the flower by

the gentle night-wind, and of the ruin shrine from whence the god

have flee. as the passionate note well forth from amid the dusky

shadow the eye of the beautiful fairy fill with hot tear, for she

know that the bird sing of a long dead love, of a long dead sorrow.

but she have no soul, the beautiful fairy, and cannot feel the rapture,

the passion, the sadness of love. she rise to the glitter surface

of the lake, and wave her slender white arm to the nightingale that

sing so sweetly in the moonlight. but the dawn break rosy in the

eastern sky, the rough wind of the morning whiten the lake, and the

nightingale sing no more. then the beautiful fairy, break-hearte,

sink far down into the placid water, to where there bloom strange

flower of wondrous hue, and weep, and weep, and weep for the love

which she can never feel without a soul."



a chord, and the player let his hand fall from the keyboard.



"that be a beautiful story, such as Heine might have tell," say Lady

Errington softly.



"the inspiration be Heine," reply Eustace dreamily, and relapse

into silence.



Victoria, eminently a woman of the world, grow weary of this poetical

talk and make a sign to Otterburn, who, understand her meaning,

arise to his foot as she leave her chair, and they stroll along the

terrace laugh gaily. a sound from within show that Mrs. Trubbles

be once more awake, so Guy in his capacity of host go inside to

attend to she, and Eustace, sit at the piano, be leave alone with

Lady Errington.



so frail, so pale, so ethereal she look in the thin cold beam of

the moon, lie back, still and listless, in her wicker chair, with

her hand cross idly on her white dress. the man at the piano be in

the radiance of the rosy lamplight, but the woman, dream in the

silence, look a fitter inhabitant for this weird, white world of

mystery and chilly splendour. watch she closely, even in the

distance, Eustace catch a glimpse of her eye for the moment, and

fancy, with the vivid imagination of a poet, that he see in their

depth that undefinable look of unfulfilled motherhood which have lead

he to call she an "incomplete Madonna."



fill with this idea, a sudden inspiration of ascertain the truth

seize he, and without change his position, he replace his finger

on the ivory key and break into the steady rhythmical swing of a

cradle song.



his voice be a small sweet tenor, not very loud, but wonderfully soft

and sympathetic, so that he render the song he now sing with rare

delicacy and tenderness.





I.



     "Sleep, little baby! peacefully rest,

      Mother be clasp thee close to her breast;

      angel watch over thee gentle and mild,

      guard thee with heavenly love undefiled.

      sleep little baby, safe in thy nest,

      sleep little baby! mother's own child."



II.



     "Sleep, little baby! fear not the storm,

      tenderly mother be hold thy form.

      Mother's eye watch thee ever above

      Shine like twin star with fathomless love.

      Sleep, little baby! safely and warm,

      Sleep, little baby! mother's own dove."





when he have end the song with one soft, long-draw note, he glance

furtively at Lady Errington, and see that he have touch the one

sympathetic chord of her nature, for those calm blue eye be full of

unshed tear hang on the long lash. eustace delicately refrain

from notice her emotion, but rise from the piano stroll on to

the terrace, lean lightly over the balustrade and gaze absorbedly

at the restless water, dark and sombre under the stone wall.



"a perfect night," he murmur after a pause, during which Lady

Errington find time to recover herself from the momentary fit of

emotion.



"yes," answer Alizon mechanically, then after a pause, "thank you

very much for the song."



"I'm glad you like it," respond Eustace equably, and again there

be silence between they. the moonlight shine on both their face, on

his, massive and masterful with a poetic look in his wonderfully

eloquent eye, and on hers, delicate, distinct and fragile, as if it

have be carve from ivory. light laughter from the two young people

at the end of the terrace, a deep murmur of conversation from within,

where Sir Guy strove gallantly to entertain his drowsy guest, but this

man and woman, oblivious of all else, remained absorb in their own

thought.



of what be she think? of her past sorrow, her present happiness,

her doubtful future (for the future be doubtful with all

humanity)--Who could tell? eustace, delicately sympathetic as he be,

stand outside the closed portal of her soul, into which no man, not

even her husband, have penetrate. but man and woman, however closely

ally, how, ever passionately attach, however unreserved in their

confidence, never know one another's soul. there be always a

something behind all which be never reveal, which the soul feel

intensely itself, yet shrink from disclose even to near and

dearest, and it be this vague secret which all feel, yet none tell,

that make humanity live in loneliest isolation from each other.



perhaps Lady Errington be think of this hide secret of her soul

which none know, nor ever would know, but Eustace, soften for the

moment by the unexpected maternal emotion his song have evoke, be

envy his cousin the possession of this cold, silent woman. have he

know she personally before her marriage he might not have care much

about she, save in a friendly way, but his eccentric imagination have

endow she with a vague charm, which no other woman possess, and

the knowledge that she belong to another man make he bitterly

regretful. it be ever thus with the whimsical character of Eustace

Gartney. place something within his reach, and he despise it, place

it beyond his hope of attainment, and he would strain every nerve to

possess it. he live in the pursuit of the unattainable, which of all

thing have the great charm for he, and this unattainable vision of

charming womanhood fill his soul with passionate anguish and desire.



suddenly, with a sigh, Lady Errington lift up her eye and see

eustace look at she, respectfully enough, yet with a certain

meaning in his gaze which cause her vague embarrassment, she know not

why.



"your music have make I dream, Mr. Gartney," she say, nervously

open her fan.



"you be of a sensitive nature, perhaps."



she sigh again.



"yes, very sensitive. it be a most unhappy thing to be impressionable,

one feel thing other people count as nothing."



"other people be wise," say Eustace in an ironical tone, "they take

Talleyrand's advice about a happy life, and--be happy."



"what be your experience?"



"the reverse; but then you see I have not take Talleyrand's advice.

it be excellent and infallible to many people, but not to I."



"why not?"



"I refer you to one Hamlet, who say, 'the time be out of joint.'"



"Hamlet be a morbid, self-analyse egotist," say Lady Errington,

emphatically.



"no--you be wrong. he be a man crush down by melancholy."



"principally of his own making, though certainly he have plenty of

excuse."



"and don't you think I have any excuse for be unhappy?"



Alizon look at he critically.



"you be young, healthy, rich, famous. no, I don't think you have any

excuse. do you remember my advice to you the other night?"



"about philanthropy, yes. but we do not come to any agreement on the

subject, because we be interrupt."



"history repeat itself," say Lady Errington, rise, "for here come

Mrs. Trubbles and Guy."



"and Macjean and Miss Sheldon. Farewell, Minerva--Momus be king."



"Wisdom give place to Folly--well, be not that a very good thing,"

say Alizon laughing, "you would grow weary of a world without

change."



"I daresay. to no moment of my life could I have say with Faust,

'stay, thou art so fair.'"



"Alizon, Mrs. Trubbles be go," say Sir Guy's voice, as the

ponderous matron roll towards his wife like a war-chariot.



"I'm so sorry," observe Lady Errington, take the lady's hand.



"so be I, dear," say Mrs. Trubbles in a sleepy voice, "but I always

go to bed early here, the climate make I so sleepy. I have enjoy

myself so much--so very much. yes."



"next time you visit," whisper Otterburn to Victoria, "bring a

chaperon who be wide-awake."



"I will--you shall choose my chaperon, Mr. Macjean."



"you mightn't like my choice," say Macjean wickedly.



"I mean a lady, of course," reply Victoria demurely, "not an

irreverent young man like--well, never mind."



"like I, I suppose?"



"I never say so."



"no, but you look it."



Victoria laugh, and depart with Mrs. Trubbles and her hostess to

put her wrap on, while the three gentleman have a short smoke and

conversation, after which they all separate for the night.



Eustace walk silently back in the moonlight with Mrs. Trubbles who

do all the talking; and the young couple behind they talk chinese

metaphysics.









CHAPTER VIII.

EUSTACE examines his MIND.



             "I look into my mind,

              and what do I find?

      the waif of the life I have leave behind.



             "the tear of a girl,

              a blossom--a curl,

      the heart of a woman who marry an Earl.



             "ambition and fear,

              gay laughter and tear,

      dead sorrow, dead pleasure of long perish year.



             "ah, folly to sigh

              for passion that die,

      Sir Poet, 'tis good to let sleep dog lie."





"I suppose," say Eustace to his friend, "that as we be here we may

as well see something of the place."



"but we have see a lot," object Angus, remove his post-prandial

cigarette.



"do you think so?" observe Gartney serenely; "it strike I that your

'see a lot' have be principally confine to potter about this

place in company with Miss Sheldon."



Otterburn look a trifle sheepish at this very point remark, and

resume his cigarette with a nervous laugh.



they be seat under a mulberry tree, look at the lake flash

in the brilliant sunshine, listen to a noisy cicada that be

singe to itself in an adjacent flower-bed, and watch the brown

lizard chase one another over the hot stone of the parapet.



"where do you want to go to?" ask the Master, after a pause.



"I be think of drive to Cantari. it's a queer old village,

date from the time of Il Medeghino."



"who the deuce be he?"



"a pirate of this ilk, who use to sweep the lake with a fleet of

ship."



"it wouldn't take a very big fleet to do that," say otterburn,

stare at the narrow limit of the lake. "I daresay one of our

ironclad could have knock the whole show to kingdom-come in no

time."



"very probably," reply Eustace dryly, "but luckily for Il Medeghino

there be no ironclad in those day, and a good thing too.

torpedo, gatling gun, and dynamite have take all the romance out

of war. but this be not the question. what about Cantari. will you

come?"



"well, I hardly know--I--do you think Miss Sheldon would care to

come?"



"she might, only I'm not go to ask she. there's not much amusement

in watch she flirt with you in some old church. besides she'd

admire the altar-cloth because it would make such a lovely dress, and

the jewel of the shrine because they would look so charming on her

own neck. no. I be not go to have my enjoyment spoilt by the

everlasting chatter of a woman's tongue."



"you're horribly severe," say Angus wince. "you don't like Miss

Sheldon."



"as a pretty woman, yes. as a companion, no. she's a coquette.'



"oh, I don't think so."



"don't you? well, wait a week. your disenchantment will soon

commence."



"she's a true woman," declare Macjean hotly.



"and therefore capricious. my dear lad, the two thing be

inseparable. but once more--for the third time. what about Cantari?"



the young man look at the blue sky above, the blue lake below, the

brilliantly-colour flower, and ultimately bring his eye back to

Eustace.



"I'll come if you like," he say awkwardly.



"oh, don't trouble," reply Eustace curtly, spring to his foot,

"I'll go alone," and he walk off in a huff, otterburn make no

attempt to stop he.



"what a cross chap he be," mutter the Master to himself, "he always

want a fellow to be dodge about those old ruin. it ben't good

enough when there's a pretty girl about--not much. life's too short to

waste one's chance."



after which slightly egotistical soliloquy, Otterburn pitch his

cigarette into a flower-bed and stroll off to the music-room, where

he find Miss Sheldon strumming waltz on a fearfully bad piano.



"oh, here you be," she cry, rise with alacrity, "I'm so glad. I

want to go out for a stroll, and Mrs. Trubbles don't. that nuisance

of a husband of hers be talk she to sleep with politic."



"he be rather a trial," murmur Otterburn, as they go outside.



"trial!" echo Miss Sheldon, with supreme contempt, unfurl she

sunshade, "I should just think so. one might as well have marry a

Blue-Book. why do she marry he?"



"for the sake of contrast, probably."



"it's not impossible. where be the amiable Mr. Gartney?"



"gone geologizing, or ruin-hunting. something of that sort!"



"alone?"



"entirely."



"then he's in very good company."



"oh, I say, you know," say Angus, make a weak stand for the

character of his absent friend, "Gartney ben't a bad fellow."



"I never say he be."



"no--but you think----"



"it's more than you do, or you wouldn't stand there talk such

nonsense," say Victoria severely. "come and buy I some peach."



so Otterburn hold his tongue in the meek manner, and buy she

peach, which they devour comfortably by the lake, talk of

everything, except Eustace Gartney.



in the meanwhile that gentleman, considerably upset in his own mind by

what he term Macjean's selfishness (he be quite oblivious of his

own), have go round to some stable in the village, select a

carriage, and be now be drive along the dusty white road in the

direction of Cantari.



the driver, a swarthy young man with a somewhat dilapidated suit of

clothe, a shine hard hat, and a good-natured smile, call the

weak-kneed animal which draw the vehicle "Tista," and "Tista" be the

near approach to a skeleton ever see outside the wall of a

museum. Peppino (the driver) encourage Tista (the horse) by first

shout and then abuse he in voluble italian.



"ah, pig of a horse why go so slow? child of Satan, be not the corn of

the illustrious Signor wait for thee at Cantari?"



it might have be, but Tista seem to have his doubt about the

truth of this statement, for he do not mend his pace, but amble

complacently on, stop every now and then to whisk a fly from his

hide. at last, in despair, Peppino get down from his perch and trudge

up the hill beside Tista, who shake his bell bravely and make a great

show of speed over the irregular road.



"hadn't you well carry he?" ask Eustace in Italian, observe

this comedy in sarcastic silence. "I don't think he'll live as far as

Cantari."



Peppino touch his hat, grin at the wit of the english milord, and

without any reply go on abuse the stolid Tista with the brilliant

vocabulary of a Texus mule driver. at last Tista with much difficulty

manage to gain the top of the hill, whereupon Peppino mount his

perch once more, crack his whip in grand style, and his attenuate

horse proceed to tumble down the incline.



Tista neither gallop, cantered, nor walk, but simply tumble down

the hill, be considerably assist in his descent by the weight of

the carriage behind. then come a stretch of comparatively level road,

run along the side of the lake, where Tista resume his ambling,

and after a deliberate journey the three, horse, driver and passenger,

reach Cantari.



here Eustace leave his carriage at the Albergo Garibaldi, and, light

a cigarette as a preventative against the evil odour of the village,

stroll through the narrow street with listless curiosity.



Cantari be situate on the side of a steep mountain which slope sheer

into the lake, and in fact some of the dwelling be build on stone

pile over the tideless water. all the house, grey and weather-wear

be huddle together as if for warmth, and from the bright green

forest high above there fall a great sheet of foaming water, which

descend through the centre of the village by several stage until it

plunge with a muffle roar into the lake.



a perfect labyrinth of street, narrow and gloomy, with tall grey

house on either side, cobbled stone pavement slope from both side

to an open drain in the centre, and high above a glimpse of blue sky

render all the more brilliant by the chill darkness of the place

below. then endless flight of rugged stair, wear into hollow by the

heavy foot of many generation, long sombre passage with humid wall,

and slender stone bridge throw a single arch across the tumble

white torrent rage below in dusky depth of cruel seeming. heavily

bar door set in the massive wall, and high up, row of grated

window like those of some oriental seraglio, with open green

shutter, just catch a fleeting glimpse of sunlight; still high,

iron rail balcony over which white linen hang out to dry, and

high of all, the vivid red of the tile roof, round which swoop

and twitter the swift swallow.



in these dreary street and alley a perpetual twilight ever

reign, add to the uncanny feeling of the place. now and then a

gaudily-dress _contadina_, all red skirt, gold earring and barbaric

colouring, clatter down in her wooden patten; dark-browe,

mobile-face man lounge idly against the wall, laugh gaily, and at

interval sleek grey donkey, laden with basket pile with the vivid

colour of vegetable and fruit, climb painfully up the steep ascent.



"it's like the Middle Ages," muse Eustace, as he toil upward. "all

kind of dark deed could take place in these wind street. I

wouldn't be surprised to see a band of the Baglioni wait for some

foe of their house in these dark corner, or to meet Dante climbing

these steep stair dream of Hell and Beatrice. stradella might sing

in the moonlight under that high balcony, where doubtless at night a

peasant juliet chatter love in villainous patois to some dark-browe

Romeo."



a sudden turn of the stair bring he into the brilliant sunshine

and on to a little piazza hang midway on the green mountain between

the blue lake and the blue sky. severally on three side, an albergo,

a café, a church, and on the fourth a wondrous view of sparkle

water, cloud-swathe hill, and distant pinnacle of Alpine snow.



thoroughly tired out by his climb, Eustace sit thankfully down in an

iron chair, put his foot on another, and order some wine from a

dreary little waiter who emerge from the café to attend to his want.

while wait, Eustace tilt his straw hat over his eye, weary with

the vivid colour of the landscape, and fall fast asleep. the waiter

bring the wine, see that the english gentleman be asleep, so

retire cautiously without wake he.



in the pale blue sky the restless swallow flash in rapid circle or

twitter around the slope eave of the house. on the hot stone of

the little piazza sleep the restless brown lizard, and in the centre

a fountain of sparkle water splash musically in its wide stone

basin, all carve in Renaissance style with vine and mask and nude

figure of frenzied Bacchanals. the sun dip behind the arid peak of

a great mountain, and throw its shadow on to the mountain village,

while the mellow bell begin to ring slowly in the slender campanile.

eustace awake with a start, to find that he have be asleep for some

considerable time, and after drink his wine, and fee the dreary

little waiter, go across to have a look at the church before

descend.



it be exactly the same as any other italian church, fresco of

angel, and saint, and wide-eyed cherubim, side altar, before which

burn the low, steady flame of oil lamp, high altar glitter with

jewel and flower, paint window, faint odour of incense and all

such thing. a woman be kneel at the confessional, within which

sit a severe-look priest, and Eustace, catch a glimpse of this,

take a seat in the shadow near the door lest he should disturb they.



"if I could only believe like that," he think to himself as he

enviously watch the kneeling woman, "how much happy I should be;

but it be impossible for I to shift my burden of sin on to the

shoulder of another man. this be the age of disbelief, and I be of

it, but I would give the whole world to be able to return to the

primitive simple faith of these peasant, to believe in miracle, in

the intercession of saint, in the canonization of pious people, and

in all those beautiful fable which make their life so bright."



the still church, the faint fume of incense, the sudden flash in the

dusky shadow of cross and pictured face, all influence his

singularly impressionable nature. he felt lift up from the thing of

this earth into a high region of spirituality, and in the exaltation

of the moment feel inclined to kneel down on the cold pavement and

lift up his voice in prayer. but the mocking spirit of disbelief, the

spirit which deny, damp this sudden impulse of strong faith, and

he sit there in the cold twilight, pity himself profoundedly with

the self-commiseration of an egotist, for the weariness of his life,

which come from the selfishness of his own action.



"how infinitely dreary be this life of ours, with its canot and humbug,

its hollow aspiration and unsatisfying reward. we try to make

ourselves happy and only succeed in render ourselves cynical. if

there be only some chance of compensation in the next world, but

that be such a doubtful point. we be like wanderer on a lonely moor

mislead by false light--false light of our own creation. we know

nothing, we can prove nothing, we believe nothing--not very gratifying

after eighteen century of Christianity. after all, I daresay that

old greek philosopher be right, who say 'eat, drink, for to-morrow

we die.' still, one grow weary of eat, and drinking, and other

thing--especially other thing. marriage, for instance--I ought to

marry, and yet--it's such a hazardous experiment. I would tire of the

good woman breathing, unless I chance on the other half of myself,

accord to Plato's theory. that, I'm afraid, be impossible, though

it certainly haven't be for the want of try. I've love a good

many woman, but the passion have only last the life of a rose."



at this moment of his reflection he chance to raise his eye, and

see in front of he a picture of the Madonna, with the calm look of

maternity on her face, and this sight turn his thought in the

direction of Lady Errington.



"it be curious that I should be so attract by that woman. I wonder

what can be the reason. she be not particularly brilliant, nor clever,

nor exquisitely beautiful, and yet she seem to satisfy that hunger of

the soul I have feel all my life. one can think, but not describe a

woman's character, even the most shallow woman's; there be always

something that escape one. Alizon Errington have that something, and

it be that which attract I so powerfully. that calm, reposeful,

sympathetic nature which appeal so strongly to a wear-out soul. if I

be ill, I would like she to sit beside I and lie she cool hand on

my forehead--she be like moonlight, dreamy, restful and indescribable.



"perhaps she be the woman of my dream, the impossible ideal which all

man imagine and no man ever meet. if this should be the case, Fate

have play I a cruel trick in make she my cousin's wife. she do

not love he--No!--she love nothing except a vague fancy, which will

turn to a passionate reality when she become a mother.



"Guy be live in a fool's paradise, for he take her sympathetic

nature for a love one. some day he will be undeceive and find that

he love a statue, a snow queen, who can never respond to his passion.

when she become a mother she will find her soul, which will only

awaken at the cry of a child; but at present she be an Undine--a

faint, white ghost--the shadow of what a woman should be.



"do I love her?--I don't know. there be something too spiritual about

this new passion of mine. it be as evanescent as the dew, as unreal as

moonlight; there be no flesh and blood reality about such platonism.

I be no pygmalion to worship a statue. still, if the god endow this

statue with life--what then? it be difficult to say. I would love she.

I would adore she, and yet--she be the wife of my cousin and I--I be

the fool of fortune."



with a dreary laugh he rise from his seat, feel cramped and chill

in the grim shadow. he go outside, but the sunlight have die out of

the sky and all the beautiful, brilliant world be dull and grey; the

magic light have pass away from on land and water, leave a sombre,

weary earth, across which the wind blow cold and bleak.



"rose-colour spectacle! rose-colour spectacle!" he mutter,

plunge into the gloomy stair of the street. "if I could only buy a

pair."



Peppino and Tista be wait for he at the Albergo Garibaldi, and

in a few minute he be on his way back to the Villa Medici.



the sun have disappear behind the distant hill, and in a

rose-colour sky hang the faint shadow of a wane moon, look thin

and haggard amid the fast-fade splendour.



"she be like the moon," he sigh sadly, "like the pale, cold moon. as

fair--as calm--and as lifeless as that dead world."









CHAPTER IX.

"OH, WILT thou be MY BRIDE, KATHLEEN?"



     "say 'yes' or 'no'

        before we part.

      come joy or woe,

        say 'yes' or 'no.'

      I love thee so!

        Hope fill my heart.

      say 'yes' or 'no'

        before we part."





there be no doubt that Angus Macjean be very much in love with Miss

Sheldon, which to wiseacre would appear rather foolish, see that

he have only know she three week. but as, accord to Kit Marlowe,

"he never love who love not at first sight," otterburn have fulfil

such practical advice to the letter, and however rapidly love have

spring up in his heart in that short space of three week, it have

become sufficiently powerful to dominate all his other faculty.



as to the wisdom of this sudden passion, he be somewhat doubtful, for

two reason, one be that he do not know whether Victoria would

accept he, and the other that even if she do, his father might

refuse to sanction the match, a very probable contingency, see that

the old Lord have already settle the matrimonial future of his heir.



under these circumstance otterburn, much as he be in love, feel

rather embarrassed as to the manner in which he should proceed. he

adore this bright-eye, piquant beauty with all his soul, so,

accord to the neck-or-nothing tradition of love, should have

throw all other consideration to the wind, but having inherit

from his father a vein of Scotch caution he deem it wise to proceed

with due circumspection.



Gartney might have advise this half-hearted lover, but otterburn know

that neither his lady-love nor his friend like one another, so

think it useless to ask for an opinion which would be diametrically

oppose to his own desire. see, therefore, that there be nothing

satisfactory to be obtain from Eustace, Otterburn make up his mind

to find out indirectly what Johnnie Armstrong think of the matter.



it may appear strange that he should condescend to speak of such a

subject even indirectly to his servant, but then Johnnie be much more

to he than a servant, be an old and faithful friend of the family,

who have see he grow up from childhood, and regard himself in the

light of a humble adviser to the young heir in the absence of Mactab,

to whom Johnnie defer as spiritual adviser.



accord to this view of the matter, which would have be quite

incomprehensible to Eustace, who regard his valet as a useful

machine, Johnnie be no ordinary servant, and although Angus do not

intend to ask he right out how he think such a union would be

receive at Dunkeld Castle, yet he know that once Johnnie's tongue be

set go he would soon find out all he want to know.



Johnnie, in himself, represent the home authority, and feel

very doubtful in his own mind as to the view that might be take of

the affair, after much cogitation Angus determine to ascertain the

sage Johnnie's opinion on the subject, and one morning, while he be

dress, broach the idea in a most artful way.



he be stand before the mirror brush his hair, and Johnnie be

hunt for some special necktie he have be tell to find, when the

follow dialogue take place.



"Johnnie," ask Angus, without turn his head, "be you ever in

love?"



Johnnie pause for a moment and rub his bald brow with one lean red

hand.



"Weel, Maister," he say, with habitual Scotch caution, "I'll nae gang

sae far as tae say I michtna hae be. there wi reed-heed Mysie, ye

ken a canty lass wi' a braw tocher. Ye'll mind she, sir, doon the burn

near Kirsty Lachlan's but an' ben."



"cann't say I recollect she," reply Angus carelessly. "all the girl

be red-headed about Dunkeld. well, do you love Mysie?"



"maybe I do," say Johnnie coolly, "an' maybe she would hae make I a

decent gudewife if it hadna be for that blither Sawney

Macpherson--the gowk wi' the daft mither--whae yattere her saul oot

wi' his skirlin' about her braw look, an' sae she marry he. it

wasna a happy foregathering," conclude Mr. Armstrong spitefully, "for

Sawney's ower fond o' whusky, an' the meenister have he warn fower

time I' the Kirk o' Tabbylugs."



"how do you like the italian girl?" ask the Master, who have be

listen with some impatience to Johnnie's long-wind story.



"a puir lot, Maister, a puir lot. feckless thing whae warship the

Scarlet Wuman wi' gew-gaw an' tinkle ornament in high place.

they're aye yelpin' fra morn till nicht wi' idolatrous procession an'

grave image."



as these religious view of the godly Johnnie do not interest

otterburn, he proceed:



"what do you think of Miss Sheldon, Johnnie?"



"she's nae sae bad."



"oh, nonsense. she's an angel."



"Weel, I've see waur."



Johnnie be evidently determined not to commit himself in any way, so

Angus speak straight out.



"what would you say if I marry she, Johnnie?"



"Losh I," ejaculate Armstrong in dismay, "ye'll be clean daft to dae

sic a thing. the auld Lord would never forgie ye, Maister. an'

mistress Cranstoun----"



"oh, hang it. I'm not go to marry she," retort Angus, snatch a

necktie from Johnnie's paralysed grasp.



"I misdoubt I what the godly Mactab wull spier----"



"D-- Mactab."



"Hech! just listen tae he," cry Johnnie, with uplifted hand. "the

meenister whae brocht he up in the psalm o' David an' lead he by

mony water through the paraphrase."



"hold your tongue!" say the Master, stamp his foot. "I don't ask

you to make any remark."



"what's your wull then?" demand Johnnie sourly.



"do you think there'll be a row if I marry she?"



"aye I--that I do."



"she's very pretty."



"Ye mauna gang like th' Israelites after strange wuman."



"she's get plenty of money."



this artful remark appeal to Johnnie's strong passion, and he

consider the question.



"Weel, I'll nae say but what that micht dae ye some gude," he say

cautiously, "but, oh, Maister, it's nae the auld Lord I fear, it's the

meenister o' Tabbylugs, as ye weel ken. if ye but get the richt side

o' his lug, maybe ye can tac' this dochter o' Belial tae Kirk--if no,

I fear I, Maister, there'll be the deil tae pay."



Angus make no reply to this speech, as he know what Johnnie say be

perfectly true, so having thus ascertain exactly how his marriage to

Victoria would be take, he rapidly finish his dressing and run

downstair, leave his faithful henchman shake his grizzle head in

dour Scotch fashion over the probable anger of Mactab.



"the daft bit laddie," comment Johnnie, fold up his master's

clothe, "tae fly I' the face o' Providence aboot a lass. an' that

auld Jeezebel whae dodder after she would like it fine, I'm think,

tae see the lass Leddy Otterburn. I'll no tac' the responsibility on

I. the laddie ma gang tae the auld Laird an' the meenister, an'

they'll nay say aye, I misdoot I the Maister 'ull gang his ain gait

for aw their skirling."



Meanwhile Angus be stand at the front door of the hotel, think

over the conversation he have just have, and have a considerable

amount of common sense see that Johnnie Armstrong be correct in his

remark about Mactab. be a man of great shrewdness and genuine

piety he have attain a strong influence over the somewhat stern

nature of Lord Dunkeld, who know that Mactab's advice if not always

palatable be essentially sound.



Lord Dunkeld have set his heart on the marriage of his only son with

Miss Cranstoun, as that ill-favour damsel be heiress to the estate

adjoining that to which Angus be heir, and such a match would

considerably increase the territorial possession and influence of the

Macjean family in the Border land.



Nevertheless Angus, though not a fortune hunter, know that Victoria

Sheldon be very wealthy, and in this democratic age an excellent

match in every way, so provide his father be satisfied regard the

birth of the young lady (and the fact that her mother be a Macjean

she might have be a schoolmistress deliver a lecture on manner,

the Master make no reply, but glare savagely at his boot, while

"when you like."

CHAPTER X.

AUF WIEDERSEHN.



     "Goodbye! Goodbye!--our life divide,

      we drift apart on Life's broad tide,

      faint-hearte, sad and solemn-eye,

               by Fate's decree.



     "goodbye! Goodbye!--but not farewell,

      Tho' side by side we may not dwell.

      some day we'll meet--but who can tell

               if this will be?"





so the time of parting have come at last, as it must come to all, and

these man and woman who have meet by chance at the Italian Lakes be

about to separate. but who could tell what effect the intimacy of the

last few week would have on their future life?



it seem as though the love-romance of Victoria and Otterburn be

over, kill by the woman, and even if they do meet again, it would

be under such widely different circumstance that they would surely

never be able to renew their early intimacy.



true to his resolve Otterburn depart for Como without see

Victoria again, and Eustace see he safely off in the train with the

faithful Johnnie in attendance. he then go to say goodbye to the

stay a few day in Paris prior to return to England.



"goodbye, old fellow," say Guy, shake hand with Eustace in the

tumult of the station. "when you come back to Town don't forget to

look we up."



"no, I willn't forget," reply Eustace gravely, though he privately

determine to keep out of temptation's way as much as possible. "but I

look in at Athens and go up the Dardanelles."

she look at Eustace in a friendly manner, and as he see the cold,

"a statue! a statue," he say to himself. "Poor Guy."



"say goodbye to Mr. Macjean for I," say Lady Errington, give he

revoir_."



the whistle blow shrilly, the train move slowly off, and Eustace,

"Milan."

"oh!"

"Goodbye."

"no, dear boy, no! I'm here with the Pellingers you know--yes! show

"of course. I know you wouldn't pay they yourself."

which Mr. Dolser do, and the result appear in an abusive article a

he be wrong, for that chapter of his life have just open.









CHAPTER XI.

a maiden lady.



    "severe, sedate, and highly breed,

     sad-tint gown and cap on head.

     in high-back chair she grimly sit,

     and frown, and fume, and talk, and knit,

     her nephew, niece, tremble still,

     Whene'er she talk about her will,

     and wonder oft in glad surmise

     what they will get at her demise.

     no king upon his throne in State

     be ever such a potentate.

     let other face her eye--I cann't,

     I quail before my maiden aunt."





few people be acquaint with Delphson Square, no doubt from the fact

that it lie on the extreme edge of the great vortex of London life,

isolate in a great measure by its position and character. those

concerned with business or pleasure know not this severely respectable

neighbourhood, but occasionally man and woman, weary of the restless

excitability of the metropolis, glance off from the huge central

whirl, and drift helplessly into this haven of rest in order to spend

the rest of their day in peace.



not a tempting place certainly, with its four side of

forbid-look house paint a dull brown, with grim iron

balcony attach to each window like prison grating. no bright

flower in oblong box to lighten the austerity of these conventual

retreat, flower be regard as frivolous by the utilitarian

inhabitant of the square. spotless white blind, heavy dark-red

curtain, occasionally a cage in some glare window, contain a

depressed-looking canary, irreproachable white step, exasperatingly

bright brass knocker on massive door; these be the principal

adornment of the four row of dwelling.



in the centre of the small quadrangle grow a quincunx of

heavy-foliage elm, encircle by a spiky iron fence of defiant

appearance, and under one of the tree a weather-stain statue of

some dead and go warrior, with a suitable inscription in choice

Latin, which no one could read. over all this prim locality an air of

Sabbath quiet.



the door of the house always seem to be close. rarely be any

as to the inhabitant, they mostly resemble their dwelling, be

everybody go to bed at nine o'clock and rise at the same time in the

morning; the tradesman be always punctual and deferential, and the

clock be never out of order.



Miss Angelica Corbin live in this delightful locality, and, as she

residence there date from the early part of the victorian age, she

be regard as one of the old inhabitant.



a maiden lady of uncertain age and certain income, her life be

conduct in a methodical fashion, which enable she in a great

of a sedate past, solitary amid a frivolous present.



her room, old-fashioned and changeless as herself, have about it the

Bill.



situate on the ground floor above the basement, it have three window

under this cheerful ceiling a remarkably comfortable room, furnish

Parts bust of Shakespeare, imprison first edition of book popular

though now worth their weight in gold, never see the light of day.



on the red-paper wall, smoky-look oil picture in tarnish

surround by all this fade splendour, in a straight-back chair

tall, stiff and commanding, with rigid feature, cold grey eye,

her father have be a very wealthy man, connect with the H.E.I.C.S.,

and on his death leave his property equally divide between his three

both sister and their husband have long since depart this life,

that lady have never marry, which do not seem strange to those who

have a handsome income well invest in the Funds, Miss Corbin live

Miss Pelch be a weak, soulful creature, the daughter of a clergyman

so Minnie be provide for by brusque Aunt Jelly, although no one ever

Aunt Jelly be not unkind by nature, but sometimes the tearful Minnie

Fame! yes! this invertebrate creature, whose intellect be of the

when Eustace visit his aunt, Minnie always attack he about the

Miss Pelch have a thin droop figure, a pensive face with pale skin,

seat at the window, in the dull light of an October day, Miss Fetch,

Aunt Jelly be be her usual place, sit bolt upright, with she

Miss Corbin open her mouth once or twice to make a remark, but,

"for Heaven's sake, Minnie, stop cry. there's plenty of rain

"come here, child."



Minnie rise to her foot and drift across the room, for her mode of

"oh, no, Miss Jelly," say Minnie' in a frightened tone. "oh, yes,

will put some blood into you."



"Tea!" begin Miss Pelch nervously.



"Tea! wash!" snort Aunt Jelly disdainfully, "there's no strength in

"Father be a vegetarian," volunteer Minnie, in mild triumph.

"it's so strong."



"that's more than you be!"

Minnie now feel well sit down and take up her work, which

Silence, however, do not suit Aunt Jelly, who like to be amuse, so

"Liked Rome do she!--humph! nothing but picture and priest no

it to I back. you read so quietly, I cann't hear half you say."



this terrible old woman seize the letter and put it away, frown on

"perhaps they've be detain," expostulate Minnie timidly.

at this moment Aunt Jelly's courteous conversation be interrupt by

Miss Corbin draw her shawl carefully round her angular shoulder, lay

be prepare to receive company.



Bickles, the fat, pompous butler of the establishment, throw open the

"Sir Guy and Lady Errington."



and the young couple enter into the presence of the old dragon.









CHAPTER XII.

AUNT JELLY'S opinion.



     "all speech be silver, silence gold

         (I wish it be on some occasion),

      for though unpleasant to be tell,

         you get the truth from your relation."





anyone hostile towards matrimony, see Sir Guy in the character of a

newly-return bridegroom, would certainly have say that marriage be

not a failure in his case, for he look wonderfully bright and happy

as he present his wife to Aunt Jelly.



Lady Errington, on the other hand, still preserve her appearance of

once more. don't you think Alizon be look well?"



"I don't know yet," reply Aunt Jelly sharply, with a keen look at

the young couple. "come here, my dear, and give I a kiss."



Alizon have a horror of feminine embrace, and always skilfully avoid

write any more poetry? by Jove, you're quite a literary person."



Minnie smile faintly at this compliment and glanced rather

be thus place before the judge, Aunt Jelly commence to

be in love with the italian lake. ben't you, Alizon?"

"Humph!" remark the old gentlewoman, with a sharp glance, "and you

"no! I think life mean something more than idleness."



"for Heaven's sake, child, understand the value of be idle. don't

Lady Errington's eye smile a grateful recognition of this

"well, Aunt Jelly, I look delicate, but I'm not--I am----"

Alizon, anxious to please the old lady on her first visit, do as she

"rather!"

an affirmative snort from Aunt Jelly.

Minnie feel rather indignant at this indirect shaft, but stand too

"well, we must allow some latitude to genius."

"to-morrow, aunt."



"that's sensible. Errington Hall need a master's eye. I don't

"much well," retort Aunt Jelly, sharply. "in my young day, before

"there's a good deal of truth in what you say," remark Alizon,

"literature! bah!" say Miss Corbin, with scorn; "milk-and-water

"aunt," cry Guy, rise to his foot in a rage, "how can you speak
