"Pooh! nonsense! don't you get angry," say the old lady, quite

pleased at upset her good-temper nephew, "what's the good of

be an old woman if you cann't say what you like? well, go down home

at once, and perhaps next year I'll pay you a visit."



"I wonder you're not afraid of die in such an unhealthy place," say

Guy, scornfully.



"don't you be afraid. I shalln't afford you that gratification for some

time yet," answer Aunt Jelly malignantly. "I'm a creak door. they

hang long, you know."



"Goodbye, Aunt Jelly," say Alizon, hold out her hand to Miss

Corbin, for she feel she could not stand this terrible old woman any

long. "I'll come and see you when I'm next in town."



"Humph! that mean if you've get ten minute to spare," growl the

old lady, kiss Lady Errington's soft cheek. "well! well! go on. the

old be always neglect."



"they wouldn't be if they be a little more pleasant," say Guy,

still indignant, as he say goodbye.



"ah! you young folk expect to find life all honey, but there's a good

deal of vinegar in it. I dare say you'll grow tired of one another."



Guy, who be at the door with his wife, turn round at this, and

call out in a rage:



"no, we willn't!"



"I've hear well man than you say the same thing, but it always come

to pass."



"it willn't in this case, so your kind heart will be disappoint for

once."



by this time Minnie Pelch have escort Lady Errington to the hall

door, and Sir Guy be about to follow after his parting shoot, but the

redoubtable Aunt Jelly be not one to give in without a struggle, and

would have the last word.



"go away! go away!" she say, furiously--"go away and learn manner."



"I certainly willn't come to you for the teaching," retort Guy, in

great heat. "Goodbye, Aunt Jelly, and I hope you'll be in a more

christian spirit next time we come."



he close the door after he so as to give she no opportunity of

reply, and Aunt Jelly thus be beat, feel in an exceedingly bad

temper. she fight with every one who come to the house, and crush

all except Eustace, whose cool sarcasm be too much for she, but this

unexpected resistance of the dutiful Guy surprise she, and she be

not ill-pleased.



"I don't think he have so much spirit," she chuckle, as she resume

her knitting. "it come from his mother, I'll be bind. Jane always

have a fine temper of her own and, be twice the man her milksop of a

husband be. well, well! I'm glad Guy can speak his mind. he haven't

much to speak, poor fool; still it's well than nothing."



in fact, the old lady be so pleased with Guy's rebellion on behalf of

his wife that she become quite good-temper, and Minnie, on her

return, find her patroness for once in her life an amiable companion.



as for Guy and his wife, when they be both snugly ensconce in their

carriage and drive back to the hotel, both of they laugh heartily

over the visit.



"ben't she an old cat?" say Guy, wipe the tear from his eye; "she

fight like the devil! it's the first time I've have a row with she."



"I'm sorry it be on my account, Guy," observe Alizon, anxiously.



"don't you bother your head, my dear," he reply coolly, pat she

hand; "if it haven't be you it would have be someone else. if Aunt

Jelly haven't a row every now and then she'd die. I wish to Heaven she

would, and then I'd get her money!"



"oh, Guy, how can you speak so?"



"why not? we need the money badly enough, I'm sure. she only waste it

on church and orphan' home. I wish to Heaven I be an orphan; Aunt

Jelly might take some interest in I."



"well, you be an orphan."



"yes, but that's not the genuine article. Aunt Jelly love a

snivelling, alone-in-the-world brat who need reform. a title

orphan like myself be no fun. I cann't harrow her soul."



"you do your good to do so just now," say Alizon, laugh. Sir Guy

echo her laughter, and when they arrive at their hotel both of they

come to the conclusion that they have pass a very pleasant afternoon.









CHAPTER XIII.

bring home the BRIDE.



     "'oh, mither! mither I've brocht hame

        a bonnie bride upon my steed,

      Sae lift she o'er the lintel stane,

        An'brake a bannock o'er her heid.'



     "'oh, bairnie, syn the wand begin

        Nane see sic sicht o' muckle wae,

      where gat ye, son, this witch wuman,

        Wi gowden hair an' skin o' snaw?'



     "'oh, mither, she's a Chrisom lass

        Wha by the Kelpie's burn do stray,

      Wi buke an' bell an' holy mass

        I wed she at break o' day.'



     "'oh, bairnie, she's nae Chrisom child,

        Sae evil glower her een tae see,

      she be a speerit fra the wild,

        an bring but dule tae you an' I.'"





Sir Guy be hum this gruesome ballad as the train near Denfield

Station, where news of their arrival have already precede they, and

the Errington tenantry, in a state of high excitement, be wait to

welcome the young couple home.



Blithe and happy, with a faint roseate tinge in her pale cheek,

arise from a natural feeling of anticipation, Alizon sit opposite to

her husband, who be gaze fondly at she, and the glint of her golden

hair and the whiteness of her skin set he think of that weird old

ballad, sing to he in childish day by an old Scotch nurse full of

the haunt superstition of the North.



"what on earth be you mutter about, Guy?" ask Alizon, in a

puzzled tone, as she hear he croon this melancholy strain.



"only an old song about a bride's home-come," he reply gaily, and

thereupon repeat to she all he remember of the legend, the

foreboding strain of which make his wife, sensitive in a great measure

to supernatural hinting, shudder nervously.



"don't, Guy, don't tell I any more," she say apprehensively, put

her gloved hand over his mouth. "it's a bad oman."



"what, be you so superstitious as that?" he reply, kiss she

hand. "do you think you be the witch-woman of the ballad, destine to

bring woe to Errington?"



"no! no! I hope not! I trust not!" cry Lady Errington, shrink

back with a vague dread in her eye, "but I be a little superstitious.

I think everyone be more or less, and my family have be so terribly

unfortunate that I be afraid of bring you bad luck."



"Nonsense! I don't mind the bad luck a bit, as long as you come along

with it," say her husband, soothingly. "I wish I haven't put these

idea into your foolish little head. you must have nothing but bright

thought to-day, my dearest, for this be your home-come, and I hear

we be to have a great reception."



"tell I all about it," ask Lady Errington, recover herself with

an effort.



"oh, that would take too long, besides I'm as ignorant as you be; but

there be to be banner and flower and music and all that sort of

thing, you know, and I expect old Welstarler the Rector will read we

an address. then, of course, everyone will have a tuck-out at the

Hall, and there be to be a dance in the evening down the village. all

Denfield's go to have a high old time, so, for once in your life

you'll be receive like a royal personage."



"don't make I nervous."



"Pooh! there's nothing to be nervous about. just smile and look sweet,

I'll do all the patter."



"the what?"



"Patter! talk you know. I'm afraid it be slangy, but very expressive

all the same. by Jove, the train's slow down, we'll soon be

home now. there's the square tower of Denfield Church, and yonder,

Alizon!--here, quick--on the right--that white wing of a house. that's

our place."



Sir Guy be quite excited, and chatter like a schoolboy home for

his holiday, whilst Alizon, for once arouse from her coldness, stand

near he, lean her head on his shoulder, and look out of the

window at the various object of interest, as the train steam slowly

onward.



at last they arrive at Denfield.



the little railway station be gaudy with bunting, much to the

astonishment of the prosaic folk in the train, who could not

understand the reason of such unusual decorative splendour, and as the

train go on immediately Sir Guy and his wife alight, they have no

time to find out what the excitement be all about, therefore depart

more in the dark than ever.



the station-master, who have know Sir Guy from boyhood, be much

flatter at be shake hand with, and present to Lady Errington,

to whom some child offer a charming bouquet of wild flower.



outside the station their carriage with four horse be wait, and

they get in amid the cheer of the villager, who muster here in

strong force. sturdy farmer, mount in good style, labourer,

look forward to unlimited beer, woman, in the bright of

dress, talk shrilly among themselves of the beauty of the bride,

school-child, jubilant at an unexpected holiday, all these be

present, with banner, flag, and flower unlimite. a proud man that

day be the old coachman, as he guide the prance horse through the

long lane of happy face, with his master and mistress sit in the

carriage behind, respond to the acclamation resound on all

side, while from the grey, old church tower ring a peal of joy-bell.



after all, let people pretend to despise it as they may, popularity be

a very pleasant thing, and it make life appear very bright to this

young couple, receive such an uproarious welcome, instead of

steal homeward amid indifferent face. despite the howling of

radical, the spread of socialism, the groaning about agricultural

depression, the bond between landlord and tenant be too kindly, and

too deeply ingrained to yield readily to the mob-shrieking for

equality and equal division of land. Sir Guy be a great favourite in

the county, and the Erringtons have be gentry at the Hall for many

century, so the sturdy british yeoman and kindly neighbour of the

young pair determined to make their home-come as pleasant as

possible--and succeed.



drive through the quaint, narrow street of the village, follow by

a long train of horseman, all the house on either side be gay with

flag and flower and handkerchief wave from the narrow casement.

flower strew the dusty road under the foot of the horse, the

village band, in bright uniform, play "Home, Sweet Home," on their

brass instrument, with mighty strength of lung, hearty cheer from

hundred of willing throat, loud clashing from the bell overhead,

mad with joy, and at the entrance to the Park a triumphal arch of

evergreen, with the word "welcome" inscribed thereon.



under this arch wait a gallant company of horsemen in pink, for Sir

Guy be a prominent member of the Hunt, and his brother Nimrods give

he a hearty greeting to his paternal acre. then, when the crowd

have cheer themselves hoarse, the old Rector, silver-haired and

kindly-faced, read an address to the happy pair wish they long life

and happiness, to which Sir Guy respond in suitable term, stand

up in the carriage, his hat off, and his bright, young face flush

with excitement.



up the long avenue, still follow by the huntsman, the farmer, and

the villager more flag overhead among the green bough of the

beech-tree, more flower on the dusty road below, and at length the

wide space before the house and the long façade of Errington Hall,

with its tall gable, its innumerable diamond-pan window, its

slender turret and weather-stain stack of chimney.



cheer from the servant, wait in two long line to welcome their

new mistress, with whose sweet face they fall in love at once. Sir Guy

then help his wife to alight, and they both stand on the threshold

of their new home, whilst a speech of welcome be make by the old

inhabitant, prompt by the village schoolmaster, to which the young

baronet respond with a few manly and straightforward word.



the band then play a noisy quick step, which inspire the villager

to far cheer, and the gentry, having see the Erringtons safely

home, ride off to their different residence, while the tenantry and

villager all rejoice and make merry on the lawn in front of the

terrace.



a blue sky above, a green earth below, happy face all around, kindly

voice sound in her ear, and her husband by her side, it be no

wonder that Alizon Errington, daughter of a social pariah, feel she

heart swell with gratitude towards God, who have guide she safely to

such a pleasant haven of joy and kindliness.



but it all come to an end at last, and after the tenantry have eat

and drink as much as they possibly could at Sir Guy's expense, they

all go down to the village to finish up the evening with dancing and

firework. the Erringtons, quite tired out, be leave alone stand

on the terrace watch the crowd as it melt away in the come

shadow, and the husband, put a kindly arm round his wife, feel

that this be the bright period of his life.



suddenly Alizon, who look pale and wear out with excitement, burst

into a passionate flood of tear, as she lean against her husband's

breast.



"my dearest," cry Guy, in alarm, "what be the matter?"



"nothing," she sob, put her arm round his neck, "only--only I

be so happy."



"you've get a curious way of show it," say Guy, cheerfully,

although his own eye be now rather wet.



"come, come, Alizon, you must not give way like this. you be tired

after your journey and all this excitement. if Aunt Jelly be here,

I'm afraid she would prescribe her favourite port wine," he add

jestingly.



Alizon laugh at this, dry her eye, and they both go inside to

dress for dinner.



a very pleasant little meal they have, in the old-fashion

dining-room, with the staid face of the family portrait stare down

at their frivolous descendant. Guy make his wife drink some famous

champagne, which be the special pride of the Errington cellar.



"I believe in fizz myself," he say sagely, hold his glass up to

the light. "Aunt Jelly pin her faith to port, but this be quite as

good and not so heavy. look at all those ancestor of mine frown

down on we, Alizon. no doubt if they could speak they would denounce

our conduct as frivolous."



"I'm very glad they cann't speak then," reply Lady Errington gaily.

"perhaps, however, they appear at midnight. do they? this place look

like a haunted house."



Guy shrug his shoulder.



"no! we haven't get a family ghost. it's a great pity, ben't it?

ghost generally run in family who have be bad lot, but the

Erringtons have always be a steady-go set, so we haven't get even

a haunted room, or a gruesome Johnnie with a clanking chain."



"I don't know if that's to be regret," answer his wife, as she

arise from the table; "besides, no one believe in ghost now-a-day."



"a good many people do not, but I firmly believe you do."



Lady Errington laugh a little nervously.



"no! I certainly believe in presentiment, but not in ghost--there's

a great difference between the two. be you come with I now?"



"yes! you surely do not want I to sit in solitary state over my

wine?"



"certainly not, and as it be such a pleasant evening, let we go

outside on the terrace."



"you must wrap yourself up, Alizon," say Guy, anxiously, "the air be

very keen here."



he send a servant for her shawl, and in a few minute they be

stroll up and down the terrace, arm in arm, not talk much, but

enjoy each other's company and the reposeful silence of the hour.



it be an exceptional night for November, in England, be still and

restful with a moist, warm feeling in the air, and a gentle wind

stir the distant tree. no moon, no star be visible, as the sky

be hide by heavy masse of cloud which seem to press down on the

weary earth, and a kind of luminous twilight be spread around, which

make everything loom strange and spectral in its half-light.



the warm, yellow light from the drawing-room pour out through the

open window on to the terrace, and away beyond the lawn, the flower

bed, and the great masse of beech, elm, and oak lie swallow up in

the dusky shadow. the wind rustle the dry leave from the tree, and

make the great bough shiver with complain sigh, as though they

dread the coming of winter, while there be a salt feeling in the

air, come from the distant sea, and, at interval, the dull, muffle

roar of the surf, beat on the lonely coast.



"this be not like Italy," say Alizon to her husband, as they stand

arm in arm, peer into the shadow, "and yet there be a kind of

similarity. this be the terrace of Villa Tagni, beyond the tree be

the distant mountain and that strip of luminous ground be the lake."



"I'm afraid I haven't your imagination, my dear," he answer

comically, "or, perhaps, I know the place too well, but I've get a

strong feeling that I'm not in Italy, but in England, and, moreover,

that I be at home."



"it's a very pleasant feeling."



"yes! I think even the most inveterate Bohemian, Eustace, for

instance, must experience a home-sickness sometimes."



"have your cousin any home?"



"oh, yes! at least, he own a kind of tumble-down old ruin about four

mile from here. it overlook the sea, and be a most dismal place.

eustace visit it about once in a blue moon, but I don't think he

like it. it's a haunted place, if you like."



"haunted by what?"



"oh, I don't know. there's some sort of a ghost, who make himself

objectionable--by-the-way, I'm not sure that it ben't a lady ghost,

with a rustling of silken skirt. but then ghost have no sex."



"you seem to be well up in the subject," say his wife, a little

drily, as they re-enter the house.



"not at all. I only know folk lore in a desultory sort of manner. but

when you get to know all the people round about here, you'll be tell

the most gruesome story."



"I suppose for the next few week we willn't have a moment of peace."



"it's very probable," reply Guy coolly, "and then we'll have to

return all the visit. it's a deuce of a nuisance, but one must do it.

we owe it to our position.



"I never hear that last phrase till I marry you," say Lady

Errington, a little sadly.



"why do not your father----?"



"my father! you forget, Guy. I be the daughter of a pariah."



he take she in his strong, young arm, and kiss she fondly.



"you be my wife, and the mistress of Errington Hall."









CHAPTER XIV.

an UNDESIRABLE ACQUAINTANCE.



     "this ghost from the past

        I tremble to see

      behind I I cast

        this ghost from the past,

      Life's pleasant at last,

        so let there not be

      this ghost from the past

        I tremble to see."





Errington Hall, hide in the green heart of its noble wood, be a

building of very mixed architecture, display in its incongruity

the various disposition and taste of the different owner who have

live therein. the original structure be evidently the large hall

(from whence the building take its name) which have be erect by the

first Errington after the Battle of Bosworth Field, when England be

once more settle down to domesticity, after the tumult and strife of

the Wars of the Roses. to this noble room, lofty, majestic, and

sombre, the various master of the Hall have add other and small

room, long, winding corridor, and innumerable outhouse, as the

fancy take they, or as their need require they, so that the centre

apartment be quite lose amid the huge wing and gable which

surround it on all side. the result be a bizarre combination which

make the old mansion wonderfully attractive to architect and

archeologist, while the lapse of century have mellow the whole

mass into one delicate tone of warm-hue loveliness.



from the central hall, with its carven roof, its long narrow window,

and quaint oaken gallery, run many crooked corridor, full of

unexpected angle, queer corner, sudden depression, and shallow

flight of step, lead to long range of bedroom, to the kitchen

and the servant' wing. this portion be Elizabethan and the outside

present the usual tudoresque aspect of battlement, venerable wall

of grey stone, cover with ivy, diamond-pan window, and grotesque

gargoyle. after the building of this, the Erringtons be evidently

too busy with the Parliamentary Wars to attend to their home, for the

next portion add to the original fabric be of Queen Anne date, of

dark-hue red brick, wide casement and heavy door. again there be

an architectural interval, as the Hanoverian Erringtons be engage

in make their peace with the new german sovereign of England for

suspect Jacobite practice, and the last notable addition take place

in the reign of the third George, when the front wing be add to the

house, a vast façade of dull white stone with innumerable window,

range of heavy balustrade, and confused decoration in the

Renaissance style, of nude figure, fantastic flower, bird, scroll

and such-like dainty device. a balustrade run along the front of the

roof, hide the lead, and in the centre an elaborate carving of the

Errington coat-of-arm, support by two greyhound, with the motto,

"Curro, Capio, Teneo." a broad terrace, with statue and urn thereon,

stretch from end to end, and a double flight of marble step lead

downward to the smooth, green lawn, from whence the great white pile

standing on its hill present a noble appearance. the Victorian

Erringtons add but little to the house, for the simple reason, that

the builder of the Renaissance wing have not only exhaust the family

resource in do so, but have encumber the estate with heavy

mortgage, which his descendant have not yet pay off. Sir Frederick

Errington have a turn for amateur gardening, and add long line of

hot-house to the side of the house, and also a kind of winter garden,

while Sir Guy have do his share in the adornment of the place, by

build a handsome range of stable. altogether it be a wonderfully

fine place, but far too expensive and costly for the Errington

rent-roll, which be not particularly large. so there it stand, a

monument of vanity and folly, which often make its present possessor

curse his bad luck in own such a white elephant.



the interior be quite in keep with the palatial exterior, for the

state apartment, situate in the front wing, be of enormous size,

splendidly furnish, but which look lonely in the extreme unless

full of company, a gaily-dress crowd be need to set they off to

advantage. the Errington family be proud of these state-room, which

be really wonderfully impose, but, except on grand occasion, when

they be throw open to the county gentry, prefer to inhabit a

small range of room on the western side, which be more

comfortable, both as regard size and furniture, than the chilly

splendour of the great apartment.



one of these room have be especially fit up for Alizon by her

husband, a charming octagon-shape apartment with window look on

to a quaint garden set forth in the dutch fashion, with trim

symmetrical line of box and sombre yew tree clip into fantastic

shape, know by the name of "my Lady's Pleasaunce."



"I think this be delightful, Guy," say Alizon, as she stand in the

garden with her husband; "it be so shut out from the world."



they be amuse themselves by explore the great house, and Alizon

be quite overwhelmed by the size and magnificence of everything.

range after range of splendidly furnish room shut up and leave to

the dust and spider, lofty wide passage with figure in armour on

either side, stain glass window here and there in which blush the

Errington escutcheon. it be all angle, and turret, and gable, and

crooked winding, so that Alizon clung closely to Guy as they wander

through the lonely room, feel quite afraid of the vastness of the

building.



"it put I in mind of Mrs. Radcliffe's story," she say with a

shudder, "there's something quite awesome about the place."



"Awesome? not a bit of it," reply Guy cheerfully, open a shutter

and let a flood of sunlight into a room, "it require live in,

that's all. you see, dear, my parent die age ago, and I've be

live here very little, so the whole place have get a little musty.

but now we're here we'll have more servant, and a lot of people to

come and see we. that will wake the place up a bit."



"but it's so large, Guy. why be it build so large?"



"I'm sure I don't know," say the young man somewhat ruefully, "it's a

deuce of a barn, ben't it? the errington always have a mania for

building, and whenever they'd nothing else to do they add wing.

More fool they, as it run away with all the money and put these

confound mortgage on the property. this be a dear old place, and

I'm very fond of it, but it's mile too big for we, and be a regular

white elephant."



"it must take a lot of money to keep it up."



"it do! so much that there's none leave for anything else. I wish to

heaven I ben't sentimental, or I'd pull down a lot of it."



"oh, Guy!"



"well, what be the use of all these empty room? it take an army of

servant to keep they clean, and for no purpose. we haven't get enough

money to keep open house, or I could fill all these room with people

I know, but what with this place, and the mortgage, and bad tenant,

it's a deuce of a nuisance altogether. I wish someone would take the

Hall off my hand as a museum, or an almshouse, after the style of

Hampton Court."



"you wouldn't sell it?"



"no, I daresay I wouldn't. I cann't do with it, and I cann't do without

it. it's a dead lock. but, if Aunt Jelly would only give up the ghost

and leave we her tin, we could keep the whole shop go beautifully."



"I'm afraid there's no chance of that."



"no, there ben't. Aunt Jelly be one of those aggravate old woman

who'll see the end of the present century."



"well, that's not far off," say Alizon mischievously.



"too far off for we to get her money, my dear," reply Guy candidly.

"I believe she's immortal."



they leave the room in which they be stand and resume their walk

through the house, stop in the picture gallery which contain the

Errington portrait, and also a number of celebrated picture, all of

which Guy contemplate ruefully.



"cann't even sell these," he say with a groan. "Fancy, what

humbug--they're all heirloom, and I'd have to apply to Chancery to

get permission, which I daresay they'd refuse. it take I all my time

to keep up this place and live decently, yet all this money be hang

on the wall in the shape of these picture. it's awful bosh, just like

make a child the present of a shilling on condition he don't spend

it. Humbug!"



"what! would you sell your ancestor, like Charles Surface?"



"no, I wouldn't go so far as that. but these picture be waste

their sweetness on the desert air in be shut up here, and, as I

need money more than picture, I would sell they if I could. I don't

see much chance of do so, however, for the Errington cousin--and

I've get about a hundred--would come down on I as a lunatic if I do

so. hang they! I wish they'd this place to keep up on a small income,

they wouldn't be so anxious to keep these mile of paint canvas. but

never mind, while there's Aunt Jelly there's hope, so come along and

look at the hall from the gallery. it's the good place to see it."



so they go along a narrow passage into the old portion of the

house, and soon find themselves in the wide gallery run round the

hall at a height of about forty foot. a wonderfully impressive place

it be, with its lance-shape window, fill with stain glass,

through which the pale sunlight stream, cast fantastic pattern

on the oaken floor. between every window, shield, spear and battle

axis, with fade banner droop above they, tell of ancient war

and the day of chivalry, when the deserted hall be fill with

man-at-arm and bold knight in steel armour, before the invention of

gunpowder relegate their iron panoply to the obscurity of country

house and museum. at the upper end of the room a raise dais, above

which a royal canopy and the Errington arm flash in gilt splendour

from the dusky shadow, while high above arise the pointed roof with

its great oaken rafter faintly see in the gloom. it be certainly a

fine speciman of the mediæval age and doubtless many stir tale

could be tell of the generation that have feast under its lofty

roof, or depart from thence to harry the land of weak neighbour,

as be the kindly fashion in those misname good old day.



"a wonderful old place, ben't it?" say Guy, as they stand look

from the height of the gallery at the immense space below, "and

genuine too. none of the sham antiquity of Abbotsford here. all this

be the real thing, and just as it be in the old day when the

Erringtons wear those absurd suit of armour, and poke their

neighbour' eye out with those long spear."



"you ought to be very proud of your race, Guy."



"I don't see much to be proud of in they," he reply candidly,

throw his arm round his wife's waist, "they be a humdrum lot at

good the Erringtons. go to church, mind their own business, and

leave other people's wife alone. they always seem to have be on

the safe side in keep their property, however, and if it haven't

be for their building craze, I'd be decently off. accord to their

idea there be no place like home, however, and that be why they

spend such a lot of money over it. I be proud of the dear old Hall,

but I do wish it ben't quite so large."



"do you use this place at all?" ask Alizon as they leave the gallery.



"only for dance, and tenant' dinner," he answer carelessly; "it

look very pretty when it's full, but at present one would think it

be haunt. quite a mistake, as there ben't a single ghost in the

whole place. a pity, ben't it, for this queer old house just look a

fit place for shadowy figure and gruesome legend."



"I suppose there be plenty of story about the Hall."



"oh yes! but very mild story, I'm afraid, not even equal to the

average shilling shocker. Errington Hall have no history which would

delight novelist or antiquary. Queen Elizabeth don't stop here on

a royal progress, Oliver Cromwell's Ironsides don't besiege the

place, and though I think the Hanoverian Erringtons be mix up in

CHAPTER XV.

a WOMAN SCORNED.



     "what! will she place her foot upon my neck,

      and hold I helpless, writhe in the dust?

      Nay, such a thing be folly at the good,

      'tis ill to tamper with the mean worm,

      for, serpent-like, I'll wound she in the heel,

      and when she fall from her magnificence,

      I'll twist my coil around her dainty throat

      and sting!--and sting!--and sting!--until she die."





"who be Mrs. Veilsturm?"



a good many people ask this question, when a woman, black-browe,

south american business."



"if she come to measure sword with I in that way," say Maraquita

with vicious slowness, "I can tell a few story about her late father

which willn't be pleasant for she to hear."



"Pish! what good will that do? you cann't tell story about Mostyn

without inculpate yourself. it willn't harm his memory, which be black

enough. it will only harm you, and through you, I. no, no, Mrs.

Veilsturm, I've too much at stake to risk the world find out what

we want keep quiet, and if Lady Errington do not return your call,

put your cursed pride in your pocket and hold your tongue."



"I've get my wit about I as well as you," say Cleopatra coolly, "so

you needn't lecture I as if I be a school-girl. besides, my

position be too strong in Society to be hurt by Lady Errington or any

other silly fool of a woman."



"your position, my dear," remark Griff with cruel candour, "hang by

a thread, and that thread be Mr. William Dolser, of 'the Pepper Box.'

he put in what I want, and make people shut their mouth, but if he

turn nasty, he could find out quite enough to make they open they

again."



"if he try to, you could promise he a thrashing."



"that wouldn't do much good. he's use to the horsewhip."



"then you could have an action for libel against the paper."



"and very nicely we'd come out of it. whether we win or lose it would

be the death-knell of our campaign in town. no! no, I'll keep the

'Pepper Box' in a good temper by judicious bribe, and you on the

other hand, don't play with fire or you'll have the whole place in a

blaze."



the dexterous arguing of Major Griff evidently impress Mrs.

Veilsturm, for she make no reply, but look down frown at one

dainty foot in a high-heeled slipper that be rest on the green

velvet foot-stool. she know her partner be right in all he say, but

with feminine persistence be about to renew the argument and have the

last word, when a servant enter the room and present a letter to

his mistress, leave it again, close the door noiselessly after he.



Mrs. Veilsturm, lean back languidly in her chair, be about to open

the letter, when Major Griff stop she.



"wait a moment, Maraquita," he say deliberately, with a certain

anxious look on his face. "you know I often have an instinct as to how

thing will go?"



she bow her head, but say nothing.



"I have an instinct that your call on Lady Errington be a mistake,

and that letter be from Lady Errington to tell you so."



Mrs. Veilsturm laugh scornfully as she tear open the envelope, but

the Major, put his hand behind his back, lean against the

mantelpiece, and look steadily at she with a satisfied smile on his

lip.



the woman have wonderful self-command, for as she read Lady Errington's

curt note, no sign of anger escape from her lip, but her dark skin

flush an angry red and a venomous smile curl the corner of her

full mouth. still she give no further sign of be move, but having

read the note through in the most deliberate manner, hand it to the

Major with a low, fierce laugh.



Major Griff adjust his eyeglass carefully, smooth out the sheet

of cream-colour paper, and read as follow in a subdue voice:





"Lady Errington present her compliment to Mrs. Veilsturm, and

return the enclose card, which be evidently leave to-day at the Hall

by some mistake."





"so I be right, you see," observe Griff, leisurely fold up this

short epistle and let his eyeglass drop down. "Mostyn do tell

she about you after all--damn he!"



the Major swear in a tranquil manner, without any sign of anger, but

that he be greatly annoyed could be see by the twitching of his thin

lip under his grizzle moustache. as for Mrs. Veilsturm, her temper

have get the well of her discretion, and having leave her seat, she

be sweep up and down the little room like an angry panther in its

cage.



"well Maraquita," say Griff quietly, after a pause, "you see Lady

Errington have declare war, as I know she would."



"you know no more than I do," hiss Maraquita viciously.



Major Griff smile at she in a pitying manner.



"Instinct, my dear! instinct! I tell you what be in that letter

before you open it."



"I should like to kill she," say Cleopatra, glare at he in a kind

of cold fury.



"I've no doubt you would, but, as you cann't, why waste time in useless

threat?"



"that she, a school-girl--a brainless fool--should dare to put such an

insult on I," rage Mrs. Veilsturm, clench her fan tightly. "how

dare she? how dare she? do she know what I be?"



"she do," reply the Major drily, "her letter show she do."



Maraquita look from left to right in wrathful despair, then throw

all prudence to the wind, snap her fan in two, throw it on the

ground, and stamp on the fragment.



"I wish she be there! I wish she be there! what can I do to punish

she? what can I do?"



"you can do nothing," reply Griff, examine his nail. "to make war

on Lady Errington would be like throw feather at a granite image

in order to hurt it. she have an assured position in Society. you have

not. she have a past that will bear look into--you have not. she have

everything in her favour--you have nothing, so be a philosopher, my

dear Maraquita. Grin and bear it. Vulgar certainly, but sound advice,

very sound advice."



Mrs. Veilsturm turn on her dear friend in a fury, and stamp she

foot on the break fan, look like a demon with her blaze eye and

clench white tooth, which show viciously through her draw lip.



"hold your tongue," she shriek wrathfully, "don't stand sneer

there you fool. tell I what I'm to do."



the Major pour out another glass of sherry from the decanter on the

table and advance towards she.



"have a glass of sherry, and keep your temper," he say soothingly.



Cleopatra glare at he in speechless anger, then strike the glass

from his hand with such violence that it shatter to piece on the

carpet. Griff shrug his shoulder, and walk back to the

fireplace.



"you're act like a fool, Mrs. Veilsturm," he observe, tranquilly;

"first you break a fan, now you break a glass--silly, my dear, very

silly! it don't hurt Lady Errington, but only yourself. by-the-way,"

glance at his watch, "it's seven o'clock. I wonder when dinner will

be ready, I'm dreadfully hungry."



his partner, however, be not listen to he, but a sudden thought

the fan.



"I'm sorry I break this," she say, quietly, advance towards the

Major; "it be such a pretty fan. dolly Thambits give it to I. never

mind, I'll make he give I another."



she speak quite cheerfully, and the Major stare at she in silent

surprise at this sudden change from intense anger to languid

tranquillity.



"upon my word, you puzzle I, Maraquita," he say doubtfully. "a

moment ago you be like a devil, now you be within reasonable

distance of an angel. what be the meaning of this change?"



the beautiful widow put one slender foot on the fender, look in the

glass, touch some ornament in her hair, then reply, in a

wonderfully calm manner:



"simply this, that I see my way to punish Lady Errington."



"the deuce you do."



"yes; she be newly marry, and, no doubt, love her husband--he's a

fool, for I've see he in London, so through her husband I'll punish

she."



"oh, I see," say the Major, grimly; "you intend to make love to the

husband."



"what acute penetration, my dear Griff! that's exactly what I intend

to do."



"no good," answer the man, shake his head. "Errington be newly

married, and can see no beauty in any woman save his wife."



"he's a fool I tell you," retort Mrs. Veilsturm, coolly, "and I

never meet a man yet I couldn't twist round my finger. he may be

difficult to manage in his character of a newly marry man, but I'll

gain my end somehow."



"and then?" query Major Griff.



"and then," echo Cleopatra, viciously, "when I've estrange he from

she and possibly lead to a divorce, I'll have my revenge."



"at the cost of your own position."



"don't you be afraid. I'll look after that! I'll keep my position and

ruin her happiness at the same time."



"you're play with fire."



"fire that will burn they, not myself! come, dinner be ready, give I

your arm."



"one moment! when do you intend to begin the business?"



"that depend on Sir Guy Errington. as a newly marry man, I dare say

I can do nothing with he. newly married man sometimes get tired of

honey. when he do, then I will step in."



"suppose he do not get tired?"



"but he will. I tell you he's a fool."









CHAPTER XVI.

the event of eighteen month.



     "time fly onward with tireless wing.

      diver gift to we all he bring,

        Joy and sorrow

        on every morrow,

      a thousand pleasure, a thousand sting.



     "love he hath bring to a maiden fair,

      Hate hath sunder a love pair,

        Gauds that glitter,

        and memorie bitter,

      each of we bear hath his fated share.



     "life be evil, the wise man saith.

      Joy come but at the last-draw breath,

        Earth's false pleasure

        yield no treasure,

      there be no gift like the gift of death."





perhaps it be due to the way we live now, or possibly to the inherent

restlessness of the present generation, but Time certainly seem to

pass more rapidly with we than it do with our grandfather.



they live in a delightfully leisurely fashion, not without its charm,

and either stay complacently at home, or, if they do travel, go

in a sober-side mode by stage coach and sailing vessel. if they do

make a journey through Europe, it be call a Grand Tour, and seem

to have be somewhat after the style of a royal progress, judge

from the stately manner in which it be conduct. ah, there be, no

doubt, our steady-go ancestor know the value of be idle, an art

which we have quite lose, and take life in a wonderfully sedate way,

saunter, as it be, in an idle fashion, from the cradle to the

grave.



we, alas, have change this somnolent existence, and make the latter

end of this nineteenth century somewhat try to a man whose health

be not of the good, or to he who desire to shine among his fellow

creature. the struggle for existence be keener, the survival of the

fit more certain than ever, and the art of enjoyment have resolve

itself into a series of hurried glance at a multiplicity of thing.



if we want to travel, steam whirl we from one end of the world to the

other, give we no time to examine thing; if we wish to read,

hundred of book, fresh from the press, call for attention; if we

desire to enjoy ourselves, theatre, ball, picture gallery, all

offer their attraction in such profusion, that it be difficult to

know where to begin. we have gain many aid to enjoyment, yet it be

questionable if those very aid have not lose we the faculty itself;

for a breathless scamper after pleasure, with a hurried glance here,

and a momentary pause there, can hardly be call true enjoyment. the

world, and we who live therein, be so busy get thing in order

for the beginning of the next century, that all hand be press into

the service, and no one have a moment to be idle, or to admire the

profusion of good thing spread before he.



therefore, amid all this hurry and bustle, Time fly much more

quickly than formerly; our ancestor yawn through twelve hour of

leisurely work, we scarcely find twenty-four long enough for all we

want to do. we eat, drink, marry, and give in marriage, welcome the

newly bear, and forget the newly dead, with the utmost despatch and

rapidity, and no soon be one year, with all its trouble and

breathless enjoyment, at an end, than we have map out the care of

the next twelve month before they be fairly start.



eighteen month have, therefore, pass very rapidly since the

Erringtons take possession of the Hall, and a good many important

event, both to nation and individual, have happen in the meantime.

it be now the middle of the London season, and those who have part

month before at Como, be now about to meet again under widely

different circumstance.



Victoria Sheldon have duly return home with Mrs. Trubbles, and take

Absence, in this case, make Victoria's heart grow fonder, and she feel

which do not add to the pleasure of life.



at the invitation of Lady Errington, she go down to the Hall at

"and I'll lay two dollar," say this sagacious young man, who have

many event have happen in the Errington household since the young

"next year, Maraquita," say the Major in a satisfied tone, as they

at the end of this eighteen month Eustace Gartney return to Town,

having herald his appearance by a book of travel entitle "Arabian

Knights," in which he describe all his wandering in the native land

of Mahomet. judge from the brilliant description give in this book

with its bizarre title, he seem to have make good use of his time,

and the fascinating page of the volume open an enchanted land to

western reader. the mysterious desert with their romantic

inhabitant, the lonely city far in the interior, whose very name

be suggestive of the fantastic story of the "Thousand and one

Nights," the poetic description of the melancholy waste of sand,

whose sadness seem akin to his own sombre spirit, and the wayward

fierceness of the arab love-song scatter like gem through the book

all make up a charming volume, and even the critic, much as they

dislike Eustace for the contempt and indifference with which he

treat they, be fain to acknowledge that this "Arabian Knights,"

whose pun title they ridicule, be a worthy addition to English

literature.



Eustace himself, in spite of the wide interval of time which have

so Eustace Gartney, blind by self-complacency, prose on to himself

so the puppet be all on the stage, and it only remain for Fate in

CHAPTER XVII.

GOSSIP.



     "if friend be poor and you cann't use 'em,

      'tis always pleasant to abuse 'em,

      although in their turn it be true,

      they're sure to speak the bad of you.

      the pot may call the kettle black,

      but kettle pay the favour back,

      and useless be all indignation,

      for 'tis the law of compensation."





Otterburn's chamber in a pleasant street off Piccadilly be

furnish in a very comfortable fashion, having be the property of

an extravagant young man who come to grief on the turf, and thereupon

dispose of his room and their content to Angus Macjean, who be

look for apartment. as the Master have not much idea of arrange

furniture accord to individual taste, beyond banish some rather

"rapid" picture from the wall and replace they by hunt trophy

from his american trip, he leave the room in their original state,

which be by no mean a bad one.



Johnnie Armstrong indeed have be move to wrath by see such a lot

of money spend on costly trifle, for the charming little statuette

in bronze, the delicate ornament in Dresden china, and the thousand

and one nick-nack suggestive of cultured taste be all so many

object of horror in the eye of Mr. Armstrong, be evidence of

sinful waste on the part of their purchaser. in spite of his love for

the turf, the former proprietor of these room must have have a

cultured mind, rare among the gild youth of to-day, as Angus during

the early day of his occupancy often come across some tiny

water-colour, or some rare edition of a book which show both good

taste and critical judgment.



"what a pity for such a clever fellow as Bamfield to go to the dog

through racing, when he could appreciate all this sort of thing," he

say half aloud one day, on turn over a charming edition of

Villon's poem.



"it's an ill wind that blaw naebody ony guid," observe Johnnie, who

overheard this remark, "an' ye get the hail thing cheap enow."



this view of the situation be quite characteristic of Johnnie. he

despise the costly furnishing of the room as sinful waste, but be

quite content that all this splendour should be pay for by someone

else, see that his master have get it cheap. economy in Johnnie's

eye be the great of virtue, and he delight to make bargain

for thing which he do not want for the mere sake of get the

well of the seller. this be not strictly speak economy at

all, see that the thing buy be superfluous, but it please

Johnnie and amuse Angus, so the dour old man potter on in his own

narrow-minded way without interruption.



the room, therefore, be furnish in a fashion calculate to please

the most fastidious critic, and Angus be very comfortably settle in

Town for the season. he have not yet see Victoria, as he intend to

woo his lady love in a somewhat cautious fashion, but have ask dolly

Thambits to breakfast with a view to find out her movement in

Society.



Mr. Thambits be a good-natured young fool, with the comfortable

income of thirty-thousand a year and not the slight idea how to

spend it. his father having be an inventor, have make a large fortune

by genuine talent and dexterous advertising, and resolve to make his

son a gentleman, in which laudable ambition he succeed fairly well,

for Adolphus Thambits be not a bad sort of fellow on the whole,

although a monstrous fool in many way. not all the tuition of Harrow

and Cambridge could put any sense into his silly head, and his father

having die suddenly, he be leave alone in the world with this large

income and not the slight idea how to guide his life.



for the sake of his money he be ask everywhere, and as he always

conduct himself well, and be very good-natured, people like he

after a fashion, although they despise and profit by his weakness

Griff, be teach he experience of the world in a manner beneficial

unfortunate dolly.



as Angus hear that Thambits be rather smite with Victoria, he

in his own weak way, arrive at that young man's room, accompany by

in his head, who impose upon thambit' good nature by borrow money

from he.



while the three be seat at breakfast, somewhere about eleven

where he have be play the part of the prodigal son. be tired of

the domestic veal, he have look up Angus, to propose another hunting

expedition to the wild of Africa.



Laxton, having have his breakfast, sit in a comfortable arm-chair and

"hardly?" reply Angus drily, "see that I've only have three week

of it. what do you want to do now."



"Try Africa--we'll get some elephant shooting."



"ben't that rather dangerous?" say Thambits mildly.



"dangerous!" echo Laxton with contempt. "Pooh! nonsense--not a bit

of it. jolliest thing out. it's life, my boy--life!"



"yes, and on some occasion it's death, my boy--death," rejoin Angus

"I have always hear," remark Mr. Jiddy, who sit curl up on the

"who say there be," say Laxton, who hate Jiddy as a parasite and a

"nothing," reply Mr. Jiddy, who never take offence, be

about it."



"Sunday-school book, I suppose?" say Laxton with a sneer. "'Little

Henry and his Bearer' be about your style, I think."

"not quite so rapid as Zola," say Otterburn, who be begin to

"I have," say dolly again, "in my schoolday!'



"oh, bosh!" return Laxton with supreme contempt. "we're not talk

of that."



"oh, no," chirrup Mr. Jiddy, delight at know something, "it's

"what on earth be the Arabian Nights with a k?" demand thambit

"it's Gartney's book about Arabia," explain Angus, rise from the

"oh, I've hear of it," say thambit, complacently. "Billy Dolser

tell I he do not think much of it."



"be Billy Dolser a judge?" ask Laxton, with great scorn.



thambit turn on he a look of mild reproach.



"of course! why he's get a paper of his own."



"oh, that settle it!" return Laxton, drily. "I think myself it

be a jolly good book, and write by a man who know what he be

"because he be one," growl Laxton, wrathfully--"a mean little sneak

Mr. Jiddy contribute himself to the "Pepper Box" in an underhand way,

by listen to thing he be not mean to hear, so he take up the

"be they," say Laxton, who have rise to his foot and be look

Mr. Jiddy wriggle, not like the turn the conversation have take,

Laxton say, but could hardly endorse it in his character of host,

"by the way, Gartney's in London."



"just come in time to hear Mr. Dolser's opinion about his book," say

"I don't think that would trouble Gartney much," reply otterburn,

"I'll lay you a level fiver he don't," say Mr. Laxton, remove his

"what's prairie fever?" demand dolly.



"do you know what a prairie be?" say Laxton, answer one question

by ask another.



"a large field, ben't it?" say Mr. Jiddy, complacently. Angus roar.



"yes, a very large field," he reply, "much large than any you'll

point to a huge shaggy head adorn the opposite wall.



"it's a very large head," observe Mr. Jiddy, wisely. "a buffalo--a

kind of cow, ben't it?"



"not exactly," return Laxton, drily; "it's more like an enraged

bull. but to return to prairie fever. it's the feeling a man get when

he once see those undulate grass plain and which haunt he ever

afterwards."



"what haunt he ever afterwards?" ask the intelligent dolly,

light another cigarette.



"oh, damn!" retort Laxton, politely, and turn on his heel, quite

disgusted with the ignorance of the young man. thambit be not in the

Mrs. Veilsturm, while Jiddy try to extract a paragraph out of Laxton

by a series of mild little question about buffalo.



"Mrs. Veilsturm's an awfully jolly woman, Macjean," say Thambits--"real

good sort, you know! I  think you'd like she immensely."



"would I?" reply Angus absently, wonder how he be to ask dolly

about Miss Sheldon.



"yes; she's get awfully jolly Sunday evening, you know. be you fond

of baccarat?"



"not much. be you?" ask Otterburn, fix his keen grey eye on the

weak face of the young man.



"yes, rather. only I always lose. I'm so unlucky."



"oh, you lose at Mrs. Veilsturm's?"



"yes. we play there on Sunday evening. it's awfully jolly!"



"it must be--for Mrs. Veilsturm!" retort Otterburn, doubtfully, at

once form an unfavourable opinion of that lady; "but if you're so

unlucky, you shouldn't play baccarat."



"oh, but I'll win when I get to be a well player."



"will you? I wish you all success. do many people go to Mrs.

"yes, lot. all the jolly people in town. she's quite in the swim

you know. you meet all sort of pretty girl there."



"indeed! not on Sunday evening, I presume?"



"oh, no; on week-day. I meet that pretty australian girl there last

Thursday for the first time this season."



"Eh? Miss Sheldon?"



"yes. awfully jolly girl. do you know she?"



"slightly," reply Angus, carelessly; "I meet she once in Italy. she's

quite the belle of London, I hear."



"yes, rather. and such a nice girl! no humbug about she. lot of

fellow want to marry she."



"you among the number, I suppose?" say Otterburn, with an uneasy

"eh? oh, no! there's not much chance for I. I've get no brain, and

"you're very modest, at all event," say otterburn, feel rather

"oh, no, I'm not," reply Mr. Thambits wisely; "people think I'm a

"I don't know," say Angus, shortly; "perhaps."



"she's go to have a fancy dress ball, soon," ramble on Mr.

"rather. everyone in London be go."



"then I may as well follow the example of everyone in London," say

"so be I," reply Mr. Jiddy, slip off a chair where he have be

"it's more than you be!" growl Laxton, grimly.

"they be fool, ben't they?" reply Otterburn, select a pipe

from his rack; "but the fact be, I ask Thambits to find out

something, and Mr. Jiddy come uninvited."



"like his cheek! why don't you drop he out of the window?"



"because we're in London--not in America," reply Angus, mildly; "my

dear Laxton, do remember that!"



"I never get a chance of forget that," groan Laxton, sit

"it sound tempting," say Angus, wistfully; "but you see, Laxton, I

"it's that girl, I suppose?"



Angus nod.



"yes. I haven't see she yet, but intend to shortly. if she refuse

I, I'll go out to Africa with you, but if she accept me----"



"well?" demand Laxton, grumpily.



"I'll ask you to be good man at the wedding," reply Angus, laugh.



his friend arise to his foot with a resign expression of

countenance, and hold out his hand.



"it's no good arguing with a man in love," he say, in a dismal tone;

"but fancy give up a jolly expedition for the sake of a woman! let

I know soon, as if you don't go I will, for I'm die to get out of

these clothe."



he look down with disgust at his well-fitting frock coat, grey

trouser, and neat patent leather boot, all of which he be willing

to change for a rough hunter's dress and a life of danger, such be the

instinctive leaning of young Englishmen towards the barbaric delight

of their woad-stain ancestor.



"well, you be a queer stick, old fellow!" say Angus, laugh;

"you'll give up all the comfort of life for what?--jungle fever,

Liebig's Extract, and a dangerous existence!"



"don't prose, my boy," retort Laxton good-humouredly, take up his

meantime, just drop a line to the Globe Trotters' Club.'"



when he depart Angus stand for a moment in deep thought, fill his

pipe, with a strange smile on his face.



"I'm in love be I?" he say, strike a match. "well, that's true

enough, but whether it's a wise thing to be in love be quite another

affair! Humph!" light his pipe, "it all depend on Victoria."



he pick up the morning paper, and be about to settle himself down

for a good read, when a knock come to the door.



"confound it!" grumble Otterburn, fold up the paper, as he hear

Johnnie Armstrong go to the door. "I wonder who that be?"



his question be answer in another moment by himself, for suddenly a

massive figure appear at the door of the small sitting-room, and

otterburn spring to his foot with a cry of pleasure:



"Eustace Gartney."









CHAPTER XVIII.

from FOREIGN PARTS.



     "I have come from land fantastic,

      which the desert sand environ,

      where the Koran's law adrastic

      bind the soul in chain of iron.



     "all the land be full of magic,

      Danger 'neath delight repose,

      Love be fierce and dark and tragic.

      Cypress mingle with the rose."





it be Eustace Gartney in the flesh, return to quiet old England

after his perilous wandering in distant land beyond the verge of

civilization, and Otterburn feel most unaccountably glad to see he

once more. why this should be the case seem somewhat strange, see

that they have tired of one another in their former intimacy, and

part with mutual satisfaction, yet in the heart of each there lurk

a kindly feeling which cast a certain glamour round their old

friendship, and make they mutually glad to meet again.



otterburn shake the hand of his former mentor with [* * *] pleasure,

thrust he into the most comfortable chair in the room, and prepare

to ask a series of breathless [* * *] as to all that have take place

since their parting at [* * *] many month ago. Eustace, on his part,

feel a [* * *] this enthusiastic reception, and be glad to think that

at least one friend remember he in a kindly manner.



they have both change in outward appearance since their last meeting,

Gartney be much thin than formerly, but his face, lean and

brown, still retain its dreamy expression, which be, indeed,

deepen by his habit of thoughtful self-communing in solitary

desert. for the rest, he be as badly dress as ever, be now

array in a loose suit of grey home-spin, which would have startle

the accurately dress denizen of St. James' Street on the person of

any one else but Eustace Gartney. but, then, he be a privileged

person, and, as his poetic book of travel have render he more

famous than ever, his former friend greet he heartily, all of

which greeting, although kindly mean, Eustace estimate in a cynical

fashion at their proper value, until genuinely touch by the boyish

and demonstrative affection of Otterburn.



that young man, on his part, have wonderfully improve from the slender

boy of eighteen month before, for, although the space of time seem

short, Macjean be at that age when the change from adolescence to

manhood be very sudden and very marked. the semi-uncivilized life he

have lead have also a great deal to do with the shaping of his physical

characteristic, and he be more manly, more self-reliant, and more

mature in every way than the unformed youth from whom Eustace have

part. a heavy moustache adorn his upper lip, he carry himself in

a dashing, self-confident manner, and the tone of his voice be

deep and more mellow than formerly. still he retain that boyish,

impulsive manner that have so fascinate the cynical man of the world,

and Eustace look upon he approvingly, as he lean forward in his

chair, with eager eye and part lip, anxious to hear all about the

eld man's adventure.



"what a jolly time you've have, Gartney!" say Otterburn, gaily, "but,

by Jove, what a queer fish you be. you start for a month's tour in

Cyprus, and you end up by a year and a half's exploration of Arabia."



"the seductive influence of travel draw I onward," reply Gartney,

cross his leg and fold his hand. "after all you might as well

have come with I that time at Venice, instead of go off to Central

Europe."



"oh, I've be to America since then."



"yes, so I hear. same man you go that carpathian trip with?"



"yes. awfully good sort of fellow, but a mania for wild life. he be

here a few minute ago, want I to start off to Africa on another

expedition."



"and you, be very comfortably settle here, decline."



"rather! I like breathe time you know. will you have a cigarette?"

say Angus, hold out his open case.



"no, thank you. I've contract the vice of pipe-smoking," reply

"you've get very pleasant room here."



"yes, be they not? I buy the whole box and dice just as they stand

from Bamfield. get they at a bargain, much to the delight of Johnnie."



"be Johnnie still with you?"



"of course! he's part and parcel of my life, and circumnavigate the

globe with I, like a Scotch Sir Francis Drake. do you want a light?

here you be."



he strike a match, and hand it to Eustace, who light his pipe, and

then lean contentedly back in his chair, listen to the vivacious

chatter of the young man.



"of course you know your book have be a great success," say

otterburn, point to a copy on the table, "there it be. I get it as

soon as it be publish. some of the critic, however, have be

give it fit, especially the chapter call 'the Puritans of

Islam.'"



"indeed! and what do the critic know about the Wahhabees?" ask

Eustace, with calm surprise.



"accord to their own showing, everything."



"ah, we all know the omniscience of critic! they be truly wonderful

man, before whose vast experience and knowledge I remain dumb. and the

rapidity of their work, their marvellous grasp of every subject in

literature, from a child's Primer to a novel of George Meredith's.

nothing appall they, nothing daunt they. oh, what wonderful man they

be! truly wonderful, so calm, so learn, so kind-hearted. why do you

know, Macjean, I meet a critic once who think nothing of Dickens as

an author! think of that! think of the wonderful mind of that man who

could afford to speak contemptuously about one of the master spirit

of the age."



"do he write book himself?" ask Otterburn, shrewdly, at which

Eustace look at he in grave reproof.



"of course not," he reply quietly, "he be a most self-deny man.

he do write one novel I believe, but it be so far in advance of our

present age that the publisher be afraid to print it--fancy that, a

publisher afraid! well, it be so, and now this critic only review

other people's book--what self-denial. and then his decision. why he

make up his mind about a book, that have take month to write, in

five minute. I've know he analyse a book without cut the leave

to read it. of course it be marvellous, simply marvellous, but our age

be prolific in such clever man. I've meet many such, and always feel

like a whip schoolboy before their calm, clear gaze. if you boil

down twenty of our good author you may make one critic, but then it's

quality not quantity."



"I think you do not like critic?"



"not like critic, my dear fellow?" say Eustace sweetly, "why they

be my dearest friend, my good benefactor. they always read my

book, and give half an hour to each, actually a whole half hour. what

friendship! and then, you know, they be so kind, they point out all

my mistake, and if I copy any idea of foreign author, they always

look they up to see if I have do so correctly, and mention

it--really mention it--in their article. if there be anything naughty

in my chapter, they reprove I, oh, so kindly, and tell the public

where to look for the bad bit. and then they be so modest; they

never tell I they write these article, when I meet they in society.

I always put my name to my book, they never do to their article, and

yet my book be full of mistake which they try to correct for I."



"how kind of they?"



"yes, be it not? I wish I be a critic, Angus, instead of a poor

author. I be always wrong, you know, and they be constantly right,

but then I don't know so much as they do. when I write a book I've to

see thing for myself, but they can sit down and correct I without

go outside the four wall of their study. what a pity Shakespeare

have not critic in his day! they would have point out all the

defect in Hamlet, and good-naturedly correct Lear for he. I

daresay they would have show he how to improve his blank verse. it

do need improve, you know, because I hear a poet say so the other

day. a real poet, much well than Browning or Tennyson, only he

ben't know so well. just twenty-two year of age, and yet could talk

like that--wonderful. but don't speak any more about critic, because

I'm so fond of they that I could praise they for hour. let we talk of

mean thing. tell I all the news of the day, the scandal of the

hour, the gossip of the drawing-room, and story of club."



"Faith, I don't know that I've much to tell you," say otterburn

candidly. "I've be on the war-path as well as yourself, so be just

an ignorant of town as you be."



Gartney smoke on quietly for a few moment, and then suddenly ask

the question nearest his heart:



"what about the Erringtons, Macjean?"



"I haven't the least idea," reply Angus carelessly, "as I have not

see they since you do at Como. I believe they be still live at

their place in the country, and that Lady Errington have present she

husband with a son and heir."



"yes, I hear that," say Gartney, with a slight smile. "I wonder if

my prophecy have come true?"



"Eh!--what prophecy?"



"about the Incomplete Madonna."



"oh, yes, I remember now," respond Otterburn indolently, "you say

"I've  no doubt she's happy," say Eustace significantly; "but what

"I'm sure I don't know! you seem to take a great interest in the

Eustace flush a little under the bronze colour of his skin, and

"do I? a mere whim, I assure you, to see if my prophecy about the

incomplete Madonna turn out correct. but never mind, I'm go to

"no; she be still Miss Sheldon."



"you be rather fond of she, be you not?"



"so fond of she that I ask she to be my wife at Como, and she

refuse I."



"I guess as much," reply Eustace calmly; "however, that be merely

a boyish fancy."



"I beg your pardon. no!"



"indeed! you don't mean to say you be in love with Victoria Sheldon

Otterburn arise to his foot with an angry laugh, and begin to walk

she refuse to marry I, so I try to forget she. well, I haven't

forget she, and I've come back to Town expressly to ask she to be

understand the feeling."



"can I not!" answer Gartney serenely, think of Lady Errington,

"well, I don't know so much about that. have you meet Miss Sheldon

yet?"



"no.



"that don't sound like an eager lover."



"I daresay it don't," retort Angus coolly, "but you see I've

learn sense since my first rebuff, and now gang warily, as the Scotch

his guest to the door.



"No!--by-the-way, I'll come back and tell you my impression of Miss

Sheldon, and you can shape your course accordingly--in love with the

same woman for eighteen month! good Lord! what constancy! ah, Johnnie

and how be you?"



"Brawly! brawly! thank ye for speiring, sir," reply Mr. Armstrong,

who stand hold the door open, "may I tac' the leeberty of

obsairving, sir, that ye look a wee bit brown, it's the weather na

doot."



"not a bit of it, Johnnie--the sun, my man, the sun."



"Hech! Hech! Au thocht it be the dochter," reply Johnnie, laugh

at his own wit.



Eustace do not take offence, as Johnnie's dour way rather amuse

he, so he laugh also and depart, while Angus go back to his

dressing-room to get ready for pay a round of visit.









CHAPTER XIX.

AUNT JELLY discuss FAMILY AFFAIRS.



     "you know the marriage service where it says--

      'whom God hath join let no man put asunder,'

      that answer for an interfere third,

      who sow dissension in a happy home;

      but wife and husband can do just the same,

      unless there's give and take betwixt the pair,

      black look, neglect, hard word, and other ill,

      will put asunder a and b new wed,

      as surely as if C have play the rogue."





Aunt Jelly be a lady whom everyone judge well to leave alone, as

she, be of a tart and aggressive nature, be dispose to be

exceedingly disagreeable when meddle with. Old Father Time appear

to be of the same opinion, for he never seem to come near she in any

way, and though year after year go by, change youth to age,

dim bright eye, whiten head brown and golden, and turn

mellow voice to shrill treble, Miss Corbin still preserve the same

grim appearance, as if she be indeed the granite figure to which so

many of her friend liken she. if Time do add another wrinkle in a

stealthy way, or make her blood course more slowly through her

wither frame, he do it in such a manner that no one, not even the

close observer, could notice it; and Aunt Jelly, straight and

defiant as ever, sit grimly silent in her chair, knitting, knitting,

knitting, as though she be some immortal hag weave the destiny

of short-live humanity.



the old lady have hear of Eustace's return from abroad, and be in a

high state of indignation that he have not call to see she as soon as

he arrive in Town, but having receive a note from he say he

would pay she a visit that afternoon, she be now wait with the

firm determination to give he an unpleasant reception.



Victoria have already go out in the carriage to do some shopping for

the old dame, and no one be with Miss Corbin except Minnie Pelch,

who, more tearful than ever, be seat at the window, like Sister

Anne, watch for the approach of Mr. Gartney.



the room have the same old-fashioned look about it, save that here and

there a bunch of flower or some dainty feminine adornment show that

Victoria Sheldon have strive to make thing somewhat more home-like.

Aunt Jelly sit in her chair with woolly-haired Coriolanus at her foot,

and knit on in severe silence, only open her mouth every now and

then to speak to the tearful Miss Pelch.



that young-old lady be in a state of suppress excitement at the

prospect of see eustace again, as she contemplate make a final

assault on he regard the publication of her poem, but Aunt Jelly

have so harass and worry she, that she be reduce to a state of

extreme limpness, and weep in a stealthy manner, make her eye red,

which by no mean add to the beauty of her appearance.



the port and sherry decanter be on the table with the usual plate

of cake, for though Miss Corbin intend to give Eustace a

disagreeable reception she do not think of neglect the duty of

hospitality; fulfil in her eye by the production of cake and wine.



"well," say Miss Corbin sharply, for the seventh time, "be he

come?"



"not yet," reply Minnie meekly, after the fashion of Sister Anne.



Miss Corbin snort like an old war-horse, toss her head in an

indignant manner, and resume her work.



"in my young day," she observe at length in her usual harsh fashion,

"the junior be always civil to the senior. civility cost nothing

then--now it appear to be unpurchasable. eh! what do you say, Minnie?

Nothing!--it's your sniffling then! how often have I tell you to

correct that habit. look again--be he come?"



"not yet," answer Miss Pelch once more, "it's only three o'clock."



"I don't ask you the time," rejoin Aunt Jelly tartly. "I suppose

you're go to worry he about that poetry of your?"



"I'm go to ask he to get it publish," say Minnie with tearful

dignity, "bind in blue and gold with my portrait at the beginning."



"poor child," say Aunt Jelly, pause a moment, "how you do build

castle in the air. well, I hope my nephew will help you to do what

you wish. nobody will read the book except the critic, and they'll

abuse you. if they do," continue Miss Corbin, shake her finger,

"don't come to I for sympathy, for I've warn you. be he come?"



"yes!" cry Minnie, in a state of excitement, see a hansom rattle

round the corner and pull up before the door, "he's in a cab."



"oh, indeed, couldn't walk I suppose," grumble Miss Corbin grimly,

"well for his pocket and his liver if he do. hand I that last

volume of his rubbish, Minnie, I've get a few word to say about it."



Minnie obediently do as she be tell and Aunt Jelly take the heavy

book on her knee, while the door be fling open by the butler, who

announce in his usual pompous voice:



"Mr. Eustace Gartney."



"how do you do, Aunt Jelly?" say Eustace, walk across to the old

lady as if he have only part with she the day before, "you don't look

a day old."



"Humph! I'm sorry I cann't return the compliment" reply Miss Corbin,

present her withered cheek to be salute. "Arabia haven't do you

much good, at all event."



"you're as candid as ever, I see," say Gartney carelessly, turn to

Minnie. "I hope you be well, Miss Pelch."



"oh quite, thank you, dear Mr. Gartney," answer Minnie, in a state

of flutter excitement. "I'm so delighted to see you back."



"so kind of you," murmur Eustace, take a seat in the chair Minnie

push forward for he. "well, Aunt Jelly, and how have the world be

use you?"



"the same as I've be use it," retort Miss Jelly

epigrammatically. "I keep the world at its distance."



"like oil painting. they always look well at a distance, you know."



"don't talk book to I," say the old lady, "I've have quite enough of

your smart saying in this," touch the volume on her lap.



"so I see! I tell my publisher to send you a copy. I hope you like

it."



"I do very, very much," cry Minnie clasp her hand, "it's simply

too lovely for anything."



"the critic don't think so," say Aunt Jelly spitefully.



"and I suppose you agree with the critic," reply Eustace.



"do you hear I say so?" demand his aunt fiercely.



"No but----"



"then don't cry out till you be hurt. take a glass of wine--Minnie,

the wine."



Miss Pelch pour out the wine with trembling hand, so excited she be

at the presence of the great author, and Eustace, know his aunt's

determination on the subject of port, drink it meekly although it be

a wine he hate.



"the book," say Miss Corbin, after a pause, "be not at all bad. I

daresay there be a good many lie in it, still they're decently tell

lie. you've improve this time, Eustace."



"thank you, my dear aunt, I'm glad to have your good opinion, but the

critics----"



"critic," snort Aunt Jelly scornfully, "do you mean those idiot

that scribble for the paper and who would abuse their parent for two

penny three farthing? Pooh! I don't call those critic. in the palmy

day of the _Quarterly Review_ there be decent reviewer, but

now--rubbish! they write nothing but drivel, though to be sure it's

drivel they criticise. I'm not talk about your book, Eustace, my

dear. it's good!--very good, and I wouldn't say so if I don't think

so."



"no, I'm sure you wouldn't," reply Eustace meekly. "and how be

thing, aunt?"



"what kind of thing, child? be more explicit."



"well, my cousin Errington, be he all right?"



"Humph! right enough."



"and his wife?"



"she's a fool," remark Aunt Jelly politely, at which Eustace feel

quite indignant.



"I don't think so."



"what do you know about it?" retort the old lady sharply. "I tell

you she be a fool. Guy be up to see I the other day."



"well, you can hardly expect I to believe that Guy speak like that to

you about his wife.



"who say he do, you blind bat? don't jump to conclusion, Eustace,

for you're not clever enough to land at they."



"well, tell I why you speak of Lady Errington like this"



"I take my own time and own way of tell thing," reply Miss Jelly

deliberately. "Minnie, my dear, go upstairs and look for your poetry,

I daresay Mr. Gartney will glance at it before he go."



Minnie have her precious manuscript in her pocket, but know from

Miss Corbin's hint that she want to discuss private affair with her

nephew, meekly retreat from the room, close the door quietly after

she.



"I don't know what I've do that you should inflict Minnie's poetry

on I," say Eustace in an injure tone.



"Pooh, nonsense! don't be selfish. it give the poor child pleasure to

have her milk-and-water rubbish look at by you. do a kind action for

once in your life, Eustace. I'm sure it's little enough you do for

your fellow-creature."



"they ben't worth it."



"I daresay, but no doubt they make the same remark about you."



"well, don't bother about my failing, Aunt Jelly," say Eustace

impatiently, "tell I about the Erringtons."



"it's just this," observe Miss Jelly, let her knitting fall on

her lap, "you know how fond Guy be of that wife of his, a piece of ice

with no more feeling in she than that pair of tong. well, since this

child be bear, she have change altogether, nothing but love and

affection, and the Lord know what!"



"all the well for guy, I should say," say Eustace, who know what

be come.



"all the bad you mean," retort his aunt. "bless my soul, I don't

mind the woman melt, no one could go on love such an icicle, but

she's melt the wrong way, and every particle of affection she have be

give to the child."



"well that's only natural."



"it's nothing of the sort, sir," object Aunt Jelly energetically.

"why should a woman love nothing but her child, and take no more

notice of her husband than if he be a sign-post? every woman ought to

love her child, certainly, but they owe something to the father of

the child as well."



"no doubt! but perhaps Guy exaggerate his wife's neglect."



Aunt Jelly shake her head in a doubtful manner.



"I don't think so," she reply, deliberately, "guy ben't the man to

cry out, unless he's hurt. from what he say, it appear Alizon be

always with the child, and the poor lad be leave to wander about by

himself. sometimes, she willn't even come to meal. now, that cann't

possibly be right, can it?"



"no, I suppose not," answer Eustace, after a pause, wonder to

himself at find his prophecy so literally fulfil, "but, perhaps,

the child be ill, and need care."



"the child be as well as you be," retort Aunt jelly, snappishly,

"though that be not say much, for you look as if you be sicken

for some disease, but in plain word Alizon be neglect her husband

in the most silly manner for the child. if this be the case, how will

it end?"



"I'm sure I don't know!"



"you never know anything! then I'll tell you, they'll learn to do

without one another, and that's a bad thing. she'll be all right,

because she's get the child, but Guy's get nothing, and he's not the

man to put-up with such treatment. if she neglect he, he'll find

consolation with some other woman."



"oh, aunt!"



"I've shock you, have I?" say the old lady grimly. "get your nerve

well under control, then. I call a spade a spade, and be tell you

the truth. if Alizon Errington go on like this, the first woman that

come along will snap up her husband, and the consequence will be of

her own making."



"well, what's to be do?" demand Eustace, blankly.



"I'm sure I don't know," say Aunt Jelly, with an air of vexation,

be up the other day, and tell I some long rigmarole about his

feeling, so the good thing you can do be to go down to the Hall, and

see what you can do."



"I!" cry Eustace, jump to his foot in a state of agitation. "I

can do nothing."



"take a glass of wine, my dear, take a glass of wine," say Aunt

Jelly, sharply. "your nerve be all crooked. that come of gad

about the world."



Eustace make no reply to this onslaught, but walk to and fro in

have make up his mind not to see Lady Errington, think his feeling
