husband for the child, it be unlikely she would respond to his

passion in any way, see that she have neither eye nor ear for

anything save her first-bear. Gartney's egotism blind he on this

occasion, as it do on many other, but he feel that he be be

force into a situation, towards the woman he love, from whence there

be no escape. look at it in his narrow-minded fashion, it seem a

struggle between love and honour, and he be undecided how to act. all

his life, however, he have be accustom to deny himself nothing, and

in this case he carry out his rule principle of selfishly

gratify himself, so there and then make up his mind to accept Aunt

Jelly's mission and go down to Errington Hall.



"well, Eustace," say Aunt Jelly sharply, quite unaware of the

struggle go on in her nephew's mind, "what do you say--will you do

a kind action for once in your life?"



eustace having make up his mind, come slowly back to his elderly

relation and resume his chair.



"I'm sorry you've get such a bad opinion of I, Aunt Jelly," he say

coolly, "and I'll have much pleasure in prove you're wrong for once

in your life, by go down to Ellington Hall, and have a talk with

Guy."



"that's right," reply Miss Corbin, much gratified. "and I suppose

you'll have a look at your own place."



"of course!"



"I think so, you never do a thing in your life without a double

motive," say Aunt Jelly, unjustly. "however, I don't care two straw

what you go down for, so long as you try and put thing right between

those two idiot."



"kindly opinion you've get of human nature, Aunt."



"no doubt, I have," retort Miss Jelly, coolly, "but that's human

nature's own fault, not mine."



"do you remember what wise La Rochefoucauld say?" observe Eustace,

thoughtfully. "'many people judge the world as if they be its

judge, and not its denizen.' that be true, I think."



"I don't like your cut and dry wisdom, Mr. Quoter-of-old-saw,"

reply Aunt Jelly, "there's sure to be a flaw in it somewhere."



Eustace laugh and lean back in his chair.



"you've get an answer for everything, Aunt Jelly! well, I'll go down

to Errington, and do my good, but I'm doubtful of success. it's

foolish work meddling between man and wife."



Miss Corbin sniff in a doubtful manner, and be about to make some

bitter reply, when the door open and Victoria, bright and piquant as

ever, enter the room.



"here I be, Aunt Jelly," she cry gaily, "with not one of your order

forget--Mr. Gartney!"



"how do you do, Miss Sheldon?" say that gentleman rise from his

seat, "it's some time since we meet."



the memory of their ill-conceal enmity at Como, and of the

circumstance under which, she have part from Otterburn, all rush

suddenly into Victoria's mind, and she blush deeply, but with her

usual self-command she suppress all other sign of emotion, as she

hold out her hand frankly to Eustace.



"it's eighteen month since we last see one another," she say,

equably, "and since then, judge from your book, you have be

lead a delightfully dangerous life."



"More fool he!" mutter Aunt Jelly disdainfully.



"and you, Miss Sheldon," say Eustace, take no notice of the old

lady's ill-nature, "what kind of a life have you be lead?"



Victoria slip into a chair, and take off her glove carelessly,

though, truth to tell, her heart be beat somewhat rapidly at this

meeting.



"oh, the usual London life!" she reply nonchalantly. "Theatre, Park,

Ball, Church--Church, Ball, Park, Theatre. the only change you can get

be to reverse they."



"you young girl don't know how to enjoy yourself in a rational

way," say Miss Corbin, politely; "you ought to marry and settle

down."



"that's your advice to everyone, Aunt Jelly," retort Victoria, she

cheek grow hot; "but you have not practise what you now preach."



"circumstance alter case, child," return Aunt Jelly, composedly.

"I have my reason--you, no doubt, would call they ridiculous

reason--but they be good enough for I."



Victoria do not know of the old love romance between her father and

this fade beauty, or she would never have speak as she do; but as

Miss Corbin, with a softened look in her eye, bend over her work, she

feel vaguely that this sharp-tongue woman have suffer, and touch

the withered hand with a pretty gesture of penitence.



"I suppose you have quite forget Como, Miss Sheldon?" say Eustace,

remember his promise to otterburn, and artfully try to find out

if she still remember the boy.



"oh, no! I like Como very much! the scenery be delightful."



she speak quietly enough, but Eustace be an acute observer of human

nature, and his keen ear catch an inflection of a tremor in her voice

which considerably guide he in frame his next remark.



"yes, the scenery be charming, be it not?" he remark

significantly; "and the friend we meet there also. what a pleasant

party we be. the Erringtons, Mrs. Trubbles, yourself and--Macjean."



"and what have become of Mr. Macjean?" she ask in a low voice, take

up Aunt Jelly's ball of wool.



"oh, Otterburn be in London."



"in London!" she echo, start violently.



"dear I, Victoria," say Aunt Jelly, snappishly, "how nervous you

be, child! you've upset my wool all over the place."



Victoria, glad of an excuse to hide her face, bend down to pick up the

ball, and Aunt Jelly, having catch Otterburn's name, go on talk.



"Otterburn, eh? I know that name. ben't that the young man you

flirt with at Como, Victoria?"



"I don't flirt with he," cry Victoria, raise her head defiantly.

"at least," she add, catch sight of Gartney's keen eye fix on

she, "at least, not much."



"that's so like you, child," observe Aunt Jelly, disentangle she

yarn, "you will play with fire--some day you'll burn your finger."



"perhaps that catastrophe have happen already," say Eustace quickly.



Miss Sheldon laugh in a somewhat artificial manner at this remark,

and promptly deny it.



"I'm sure it haven't," she say, look straight at Eustace with

crimson cheek. "I take too good care of myself for that. but talk

about Mr. Macjean, how be it I have not see he?"



"I don't know I'm sure," reply Gartney carelessly; "he's only be a

short time in Town, you know. I want he to come here to-day, but he

be engage."



Victoria feel all her old hatred of Eustace revive as he speak the

last word, as she feel sure he be talk sarcastically, and would

have like to reply sharply, but she could hardly do so without

betray an unwonted interest in Otterburn, which might have place

lynx-eyed Aunt Jelly on the _qui vive_, so wisely hold her tongue.



Eustace himself, be satisfied that Victoria still feel an interest

in his young friend, inwardly congratulate himself on the result of

his diplomacy, and arise to go.



"Goodbye, Aunt Jelly," he say, kiss his relative. "I'll go down

home to-morrow and tell you what I've do on my return."



"that's right, Eustace," say Aunt Jelly, much pleased; "have a glass

of wine before you go?"



"no, thank you," reply Gartney, walk to the door, "one glass be

enough for I."



"weak head," mutter Aunt Jelly, "just like your father."



"well than a weak character," retort Eustace, gaily. "_Au revoir_,

Miss Sheldon. I'll tell Mr. Macjean I've see you."



"no, don't," say Victoria hastily, then, feel that she have

commit an error, strove to mend it. "I mean yes, of course I'll be

very pleased to see Mr. Macjean again."



"I've no doubt you will," mutter Eustace to himself, as he get into

his cab; "she's still in love with he, so otterburn have only to ask

and to have."



Mr. Gartney would hardly have be so confident have he see Victoria

at that moment, for she have run hastily up to her room and be lie

on her bed in a passion of tear.



"he wouldn't come and see I, I suppose," she say viciously. "oh,

very well, I'll punish he for this. he's forget all about I, but

I'll make he propose again if it's only for the pleasure of refuse

he."









CHAPTER XX.

the OLD HOUSE by the SEA.



     "Curs'd by Superstition eerie,

      Grim it stand a ruin dreary,

      Round it spread the marsh lonely,

      haunt by dim shadow only,

      shadow of an evil seeming,

      such as rise in ghastly dreaming,

      overhead the sky of crimson,

      redden slowly from the dim sun,

      silently the sluggish water

      Undermine the tower which totter,

      and the ocean's sullen boom,

      Prophesies the come doom,

      when the house shall sudden sink,

      shattered o'er destruction's brink,

      and the dark night's gloomy pall

      evermore brood over all."





Eustace, with his whimsical fancy for bestow appropriate name on

all thing, have christen his ancestral residence Castle Grim, and he

certainly could not have hit upon a happy title for such a dreary

place.



stand on the verge of wide-spread marsh, it face towards the

sea, which be only a little distance away, and the salt wind from

the ocean roar day and night round the lonely house. for it be

lonely, no habitation be within mile, owe to the malaria which

arise from the marsh make the whole neighbourhood unhealthy to

live in. Gartney have another residence, much more comfortable,

situate in the midland shire, but, with his usual fantastic nature,

prefer when stay in the country to inhabit this semi-ruinous

mansion.



whoever build it must have be fond of solitude, and much give to

self-communing of a dreary nature, for certainly no one with a

healthy mind could have find pleasure in contemplate the melancholy

stretch of the marsh and in hearken to the sullen roar of the

surge break on the sandy shore. few of the Gartney family have

stay in it since its erection, and it be reserve for Eustace, in

whom the melancholy nature of some far-off ancestor be revive, to

make it a habitable residence.



perhaps the weirdness of the place have a fascination for his poet

nature, or the dismal fenland please his distorted imagination, but

at all event, Eustace be rarely in England without pay a visit to

Castle Grim, and stay there a few day, before his departure to

distant land.



other people not be so fond of this awesome place, Gartney could

get no ordinary servant to stay in it, and consequently it be leave

to the care of an aged pair, man and wife, who do not mind where they

live so long as they have a roof to cover they, food to eat, and a

chance of earn a decent income. they look after the crazy old

place thoroughly, and when their master pay it a visit contrive to

make he pretty comfortable consider all thing. but as a rule,

they live a Robinson Crusoe-like life, see no one from week's end

to week's end, save when they go into Denfield for provision.



Mr. and Mrs. Javelrack, the guardian of this unpleasant mansion, have

receive a telegram from its owner, tell they that he be come,

and consequently the male Javelrack have drive to the Denfield Station

for his master, while the female Javelrack set the room in order and

prepare a meal for Mr. Gartney.



Eustace have not bring his valet to Castle Grim, as that worthy would

immediately have give notice have he be ask to stay in such a

nerve-shake place. so he drive away from the station slowly in the

dog-cart with his quaint old retainer beside he, and his portmanteau

behind.



it be a very decent dog-cart take it all round, and the horse in

the shaft be not by any mean a bad speciman of his kind, as Gartney

allow the Javelracks a decent sum yearly to keep up the place, and

they make amend for their lonely life by surround themselves with

all the luxury they be able. Report say they be miser, and

perhaps there be some truth in the rumour, but whenever Eustace come

down, he always find thing in order, so he never trouble his head

to ascertain what proportion of the income he allow they have spend

on the place, or what portion they stow away in odd corner. indeed,

if he have find that these two old servant be spend as little as

they could without be find out, and put the rest by for a

rainy day, he would not have be particularly annoyed, for they be

only within their right in have some pleasure in Castle Grim.



Eustace wrap himself well up in his ulster, for the wind blow very

keenly across the marsh, and as the horse be restive, they soon

leave the village behind and be move rapidly across the straight

road which stretch a narrow white thread until it vanish on the

verge of the horizon. the gable of Errington Hall show whitely

above the sombre wood around it, but after a rapid glance at the roof

which cover the woman he love, Gartney shake the rein impatiently

to make the horse go fast, and stare resolutely at the red glare of

the sky lower over the wild waste landscape.



"I'll see she to-morrow," he think, as the hoof of the horse beat

steadily on the hard white road, "and then I can see for myself how

thing stand between she and Guy."



some long sombre cloud lower heavily over the crimson of the

horizon as if Night, like some dark-wing bird, be wait to settle

down on the chill earth, and a keen cold wind, blow sharply from

the distant ocean, bring the salt odour of the sea to their

nostril.



Javelrack, his huge form bow by age and rheumatism catch from the

marsh mist, sit grimly silent beside his master with his large,

hairy, brown hand clasp on his lap, and his mahogany-colour face

with its wiry black beard, so screw up with face the cut wind,

that under his weather-stain brown hat he look like a fantastic

chinese idol. eustace, wrap up in his own thought, pay no

attention to his silent companion, but, bow his head against the

blast, indulge in vision of Alizon Errington.



a dreary country, with the wide spread marsh stretch on either

side for mile, and the long straight road run through the heart

of the swamp. sluggish, slimy pool of oily stillness, fringe of

stately reed sway to and fro in the blast, smooth patch of green

grass, pleasing to the eye but treacherous to the unwary foot. here

and there a break-down fence, deeply implant in weed of luxuriant

growth, border deep ditch of black earth fill with stagnant

water, on which float green slime, row of depressed-look

willow, and on occasion the gaunt stump of a tree stick up as if

to mark the site of a submerge forest.



then suddenly against the dull red of the sky a misshapen pile of

gable and chimney on the verge of a slight rise, girdle by a gaunt

ring of leafless tree. beyond, heap of wind-blow sand cover with

sparse vegetation standing as a barrier between the marsh and the

ocean, which toss in wave of blood under the evil red sky as it

moan in a querulous voice on the starve-look strip of sandy

beach. and this be Castle Grim.



Eustace stop the tired horse at the door of the house (or rather

the horse stop of its own accord), and give the rein to

Javelrack, jump down. at the door he be meet by Mrs. Javelrack,

large and gaunt as her husband, with the same embrowned face and the

same distorted feature, suggestive of chinese deity. indeed, as the

male Javelrack take the portmanteau into the house and stand by his

wife, they look like two ogre inhabit Castle Grim, who be

prepared to make a meal of Eustace as soon as he be safely within the

wall.



the male ogre, however, take his master's portmanteau into his

bedroom, and then come out again, take the dog-cart round to the

stable, while Mrs. Javelrack, her face twist into a hideous grin

mean for a smile, bring hot water for the weary traveller.



"don't be long with the dinner, Mrs. Javelrack," call Eustace as she

close the door.



"no sir," croak Mrs. Javelrack in a hoarse voice, as if she have be

a frog out of the marsh, "it 'ull be ready as soon as you, sir."



Mr. Gartney wash himself in the warm water, which take away the

smarting feel in his face cause by the keen salt wind, and having

change his clothe saunter into the one habitable room of the

place, which do for dining-room, drawing-room, and music-room, for

Eustace have send down a very good piano, which stand in one corner.



"Humph! rather spoilt by the damp," he say to himself; as he run his

lithe finger over the key, "or perhaps the amiable Mrs. Javelrack

have be try to cultivate music."



the ogress bring in the dinner and wait on Eustace in a ponderous

manner, give he all the news of the neighbourhood, which be

remarkably scant, and talk all through the meal in a subdue roar.

when Eustace have finish, she remove the dish, bring in some

coffee, and, after make up the fire, retire to the kitchen and the

company of Mr. Javelrack. Gartney hear they chat even through the

thick wall, for the dampness of the marsh have make they both

somewhat deaf, and consequently they shout so loudly at one another,

that it be difficult at time to tell whether it be the ocean

roaring or the ogre converse.



it be a very comfortable room, having be furnish by Eustace

accord to his own idea, and the wall, instead of be paper,

be hang with dull red cloth after the fashion of tapestry, which

wave at interval as the searching wind creep in shrilly through

crack and cranny. a wide fireplace in which blaze a large coal fire

between the grotesque brass dog, several comfortable arm-chair, and

on one side, a small book-case contain a selection of Gartney's

favourite author. at the distant end of the room a grand piano, with

the music pile neatly beside it, a cumbersome, old-fashioned sofa,

and a deep, square window with diamond pane, and a quaint oaken seat

set in its depth.



Eustace draw an arm-chair close to the fire and near to the small

table upon which Mrs. Javelrack have place his coffee, produce his

pipe, and be soon puff away in a most comfortable manner. he

pick up a slim volume of poem entitle "rose dreaming," and turn

over the page listlessly as he sip his coffee, feel a drowsy

sensation steal over he. a verse in the poem call "Temptation,"

however, rouse he from this lethargic state, and throw down the

book, he pace restlessly up and down the room repeat the four

line quietly to himself:





     "this love so hard the winning.

        for ever will endure,

      if all the world be sin,

        why should we two be pure?"





"I'm afraid she willn't take the same view as that," he mutter to

himself discontentedly, think of Lady Errington. "and yet, if she

don't love her husband, she may have a kindly feeling for I. as to

the child, surely no woman--not even this Madonna--can devote herself

exclusively to it. still, the child be the obstacle between herself

and her husband, so perhaps it will be the obstacle between herself

and I. oh! I could love she! I could love she if she would only let

I! she will let I! I'm certain of it! Guy have no brain, and she be

starve for the want of intellectual food. the child be the excuse,

but that be the real reason of the coldness between they."



one of the most extraordinary part of Gartney's delusion concern

his chance of success with Lady Errington lie in the fact that his

present reasoning be diametrically oppose to the view he hold when

first meet Lady Errington. he then assert that she would never

care for her husband, but when she become a mother would lavish all

her love on the child. this view of Alizon's character be a correct

one, as Eustace in his innermost heart well know, but he wilfully

deceive himself in think that now she have obtain her heart's

desire she would give it up for the sake of a man whom she have hardly

see. Eustace, however, have be so uniformly triumphant with the

female sex, that the idea of fail with Alizon never enter his

mind, and he think that if he lay siege to Lady Errington, in a

dexterous fashion, she would give up everything--husband, child, name,

and home--in order to gratify his selfish desire.



when he come to England after his many month' absence in Arabia,

Gartney have determine not to see Lady Errington, feel that he

love she, or rather her idolize memory, so much, that he would not

be able to suppress his passion, and thus behave dishonourably towards

excuse with which to quiet his conscience for this wrong-doing, and

having settle in his own selfish mind that Fate be too strong for

he would have less trouble in overcome Lady Errington's scruple to

his dishonourable proposal.



this idea which he hold have be singularly strengthen by the remark

this vile purpose be--Mrs. Veilsturm.



it be curious that he should have fix on this special woman to do

this, see that he be ignorant of Mrs. Veilsturm's grudge against

Lady Errington, and do not know how eagerly she would seize this

opportunity of revenge herself on the woman who have slight she so

scathingly. he merely choose Mrs. Veilsturm because she be beautiful,

clever, and unscrupulous, so a hint to she would be quite sufficient

to induce she to fascinate Guy by all the mean in her power.



Eustace Gartney be by no mean a thoroughly bad man. indeed, he have

very good quality, although they be, to a great extent,

neutralize by his indomitable selfishness, and therefore he suffer

several qualm of conscience over the dishonourable scheme he have in

hand.



his intense egotism and love of gratify self, however, come to his

aid, and he argue himself into a satisfactory frame of mind by Heaven

only know what sophistry.



"she don't care a bit about her husband," he reflect, pace the

room with measured stride, "she never do care about he, and it's a

pity to see a clever woman like that tie to an unsympathetic log.

with I, her life will be much happy than with he, and after he

get a divorce I will marry she, and we will live abroad, where there

will be no narrow-minded bigot to scoff at what they will call she

false step. I'll do it, at whatever cost! my life will be a blank

good for both of we to come together, even at the cost of a public

scandal. I'm sorry for Guy, but the one must suffer for the many, and

I daresay in after year he will thank I for take from he a wife

from whom, even now, after less than two year of married life, he be

estranged."



so Eustace, sophist as he be, argue in favour of his dishonourable

passion, and would have even succeed in persuade himself that he

certain uneasy feeling that he ruthlessly crush down.



having settle his plan to his own satisfaction, Eustace have another

smoke, then go to the window, draw aside the curtain and look

forth into the black night.



the wind be rise and whistle shrilly round the house, lash

the dark wave into line of seethe white foam which glimmer

ghost-like through the gloom, while overhead the thin filmy cloud

race across the sky over the face of the haggard-look moon. he

could hear the thunder of the surge on the distant beach, the wind

mutter drearily among the tree, and cast his eye overhead he

see the pallid moonlight stream in ghastly radiance through the

ragged cloud.



drop the curtain with a sigh, he saunter across to the piano,

and begin to improvise a weird fantasy in keep with the feeling

arouse by the wild scene without. the roll of the sea, the wuthering

of the wind, and the rustle of the reed be all transmute into

strange harmony under the touch of his skilful finger, and steal

out at interval from amid the tempest of sound, steal a strange,

sob strain, fitful and wayward as the breeze, as if some malicious

demon be pipe heart-steal love-song to the sky, and the night,

and the lonely marsh.



he remain some time at the piano, follow his changeful fancy,

but when the clock strike nine he close the instrument, and have one

final pipe before go to bed. as he sit in front of the fire,

look into the heart of the burn coal, he go over again in his

own mind the detail of the scheme by which he hope to secure his

cousin's wife to himself.



"yes," he say aloud in the silence of the room, "it be all right!

there be no flaw!"



there be a flaw, however, and one which, in his blind egotism and

complacent selfishness, he entirely overlook, and that be the love

of the mother for her child.









CHAPTER XXI.

from the husband'S point of VIEW.



     "a statue cut in marble white

      to I give but a cold delight,

        although 'tis fair

        I do not care,

      for joy begin and end with sight.



     "a woman pure as virgin snow,

      within whose vein the life-blood flow,

        whose smile reveal

        the love she feel,

      ah, such a one be Love's true rose."





the next morning Eustace make up his mind to go to Errington Hall in

the afternoon, and meanwhile amuse himself in leisurely stroll

along the beach watch the wave roll landward.



behind he the sand hill rise in low mound with their scanty

vegetation, shut out the marsh beyond, then come the narrow

strip of sandy beach on which his footstep leave deeply imprint

mark, and before he, sombre under the leaden coloured sky, stretch

the heaving ocean, with thin line of white-crest wave break to

cold foam at his foot. the sky, fill with rain-charge cloud,

lower heavily on the chill earth, and midway fly a wide-wing

sea-gull, utter discordant cry.



it be a dreary scene, and Eustace, with his hand clasp behind he,

stare at the dismal prospect, which be quite in keep with his own

disturb feeling. he be meditate a dishonourable action, and he

know it, so in spite of his determination to carry it through to the

bitter end, he feel oppress by a vague feeling of dread that all his

villainy would be of no avail. in the course of his selfish life he

have do many foolish thing, at which the world have look askance,

but hitherto his pride have preserve he from dishonour, but now he

stand on the edge of an abyss into which he be about to plunge of his

own free will, and, in spite of his egotistical philosophy, he

tremble at the prospect before he.



suppose he do induce Lady Errington to return his passion and leave

England with he, what benefit would it bring to he or to she? to she

a ruin home, the memory of a deserted child, the prospect of exile

from all social circle, and an endless regret for her fall; to he,

delight companionship for a time, and then a sense of weary disgust,

of futile sorrow for a past that could not be undo, and constant

discord between himself and the partner of his shame.



be it worth the risk he be run, for a chimera, a fanciful

creation of his own brain, a desire for a vision that might never be

realise? and all this time with characteristic selfishness, not a

thought for the deserted husband, for the motherless child.



"Hallo, Eustace! where be you?"



Gartney arise to his foot with an ejaculation, the red blood rush

to his face.



"Guy!"



it be Guy, his cousin, the man whose wife he love, the man whose

home he intend to destroy, and, even wrap as he be in his triple

armour of pride, egotism, and self-complacency, he feel the sting of

remorse. it be too late, however, to think of such thing, he having

fully make up his mind to act; so he crush down the feeling which

might have make he a well man, and go forward to meet his cousin,

who be walk smartly along the beach.



eighteen month have not make much change in Errington, save that he

be a little stouter, but he look as handsome as ever, only there

"well, dear old fellow, I be glad to see you again," he say, come

"you be very kind, Guy," falter Eustace, quietly shake hand,

with the feeling of remorse again dominant in his breast. "I be go

over to see you this afternoon."



"be you?" say Errington, listlessly. "oh, yes!--of course, but I

hear at the village you have come to Castle Grim, so, as I be

Caliban of yours and come down to look you up."



"I'm very glad to see you," return Eustace, turn away his head.

"shall we go back to the house?"



"no, not yet," respond Errington, throw himself down on the dry

sand. "let we talk here. I want to speak to you privately, Eustace, and

this be the good place."



Gartney know in his own mind that Errington want to speak about his

wife, so sit down near the recumbent form of his cousin, and wait

nothing be say, however, until, after a moment's silence, Guy look

up at Gartney's face with a frown.



"Good Lord, man, have you leave your tongue behind in Arabia?" he say

roughly, lean his cheek on his hand.



Eustace laugh a little bitterly.



"perhaps it would have be as well if I have do so," he say

deliberately, "it might save my soul the burden of many lie."



"as whimsical as ever!"



"do you think so? no doubt! solitude be rather apt to confirm a man in

his eccentric habit. by-the-way, you have not tell I how your wife

be?"



"quite well," reply Errington shortly.



"and the son and heir, on whose birth I must congratulate you?"



"oh, he's all right."



Guy speak this last sentence in such a bitter tone that Eustace could

have turn round, and smile ironically.



"you seem surprised?" he say at length.



"I be surprised," answer Eustace deliberately. "when I see you in

Italy, you speak very differently--very differently indeed."



"ah, but you see that be in my character of a newly-married man,"

"and why be it over?" ask Eustace, coldly. "eighteen month can

scarcely make so much difference----"



"it make every difference--in my case."



"why?"



Guy sit up suddenly, clasp his hand round his knee, and stare at

the ocean, answer in a dreary voice utterly devoid of any feeling:



"I daresay it will sound ridiculous to a man like yourself, Eustace,

and no doubt you and the world will laugh at I when you know my

reason. but I cannot help it. I've fight against the feeling, as much

as ever I could. I've make all sort of excuse for my wife, but it's

"I'm quite in the dark as to what you be talk about."



"I'm talk about my wife," say Guy deliberately. "you know how much

"and be you not in love with she now?"



"yes, I be!"



"then what have you to complain of?"



"Complain of!" echo Errington with a bitter laugh. "I have nothing

to complain of, accord to the view of the world. Alizon be a

perfect wife, a perfect mother, a perfect woman in every way. in fact,

that be what I do complain of! she's too perfect."



"Good Heavens, man!" cry Eustace, now thoroughly exasperate. "I

"I want love," return Guy, in a low, deep voice, the blood rush

cannot help it. I marry Alizon in order to have a true and love

wife, and I find I be tie to a statue."



"but I cannot understand----"



"of course, you cann't," cry Errington vehemently, leap to his

foot, "how could you? a cold-blooded man, who can do without love and

affection, who don't care two straw about any human being, and only

adore the phantom creation of his own brain. Great Heaven!" say the

"but surely the child be a bond of union between you?"

it seem horrible, Eustace, but I do. I hate it."



"don't talk like that, Guy," cry Eustace, spring to his foot, and

lay his hand on his cousin's arm, "it's terrible--your own child!"



"my own child! my own child," repeat Guy with senseless reiteration.

"yes! my own child."



he thrust his hand into his pocket, and abruptly turn away,

what appear to he to be such a trivial matter. it might seem so to

after a few moment Errington return, with a hard look on his face,

and a cynical laugh on his lip.



"I beg your pardon, Eustace," he say ceremoniously, "for trouble

you about these affair, but if I haven't someone to talk to about it,

"you make a mistake there," say Gartney, quickly. "Aunt Jelly think

a great deal about it. in fact, it be because she urge I to see what

"you cann't do anything," reply Errington listlessly, "no one can do

with the dead. she be a perfect wife, a perfect mother, and I, in the

Eustace feel a sudden pang of compunction at the idea of the misery he

husband and wife. with this new resolve in his mind, he approach

he towards Castle Grim.



"come to the house, old fellow," he say kindly. "you be work

yourself into a perfect state over nothing. have luncheon with I, and

then we'll drive over together, and I'll do my good to put thing

right."



"impossible," say Guy, gloomily, "quite impossible."



"how so?"



"it's easy enough explain! when I marry my wife, I think she

coldness would wear off, but it do not. to all my love and

tenderness, she be as cold as ice. kind enough in a cold-blooded sort

she might as well have be a statue. that be hard enough to bear, as

caress she, she frown and resent any display of affection. all her

they hear I talk, but no one can understand it, unless they undergo

he speak with great emotion, and although Eustace fail in a great

ardent nature, to whom love be a necessity, find himself tie to a

pitiable situation, and yet one at which the world would laugh,

because the tragic element therein be so simple.



Gartney listen in silence to the long speech, and say nothing in

"I willn't speak to Mrs. Veilsturm," he think to himself, pour out

during the luncheon, he talk gaily enough to Errington, cheer he

when the meal be finish, he order Javelrack to bring round a

horse, and, with Sir Guy, be soon trot along the road on the way

"now, listen to I, Guy," he say, when they be some distance on

not half so bad as you make out. Alizon be a young mother, and you

know they always adore their first-bear to the exclusion of everything

first outburst of joy on the child be exhaust, she will, doubtless,

in the meantime, it be foolish of you to remain at the Hall, as you

bad thing in the world for a man in your condition, so the good

thing you can do be to come up to town with I for a week or so."



"but I cannot leave Alizon alone," object Errington in perplexity.



"why not? she willn't be lonely, as she have the child, and besides, if

she neglect you as you say, it be because you be always near she. a

few week' absence would make a wonderful change in her demeanour, I

can tell you."



"do you really think so?" ask poor Guy, his face light up.



"I'm certain of it. in spite of your year, my dear boy, I'm afraid

you don't know much about feminine nature. learn then, that to make a

woman value a thing truly, it be necessary to put it out of her reach.

immediately it be in that position, then she'll strain every nerve to

get it back again. therefore, if you leave your wife, and neglect she

for a time, she will begin to grow jealous, and see how wrongly she

have treat you. when you come back again, she will alter her conduct,

and thing will be all right."



"I don't believe in that prescription," retort Guy, sharply.



"don't you? it do sound rather difficult of belief, but it's true

for all that. and I can tell you of a case in question, that of

Victoria Sheldon and Macjean."



"I don't understand----"



"no! then I'll explain. if you will carry your memory back to the time

we be in Italy, you will remember that Otterburn be very much in

love with Victoria Sheldon."



"to tell you the truth, I've almost forget Otterburn himself. be

he not your companion then?"



"Yes!--we part at Venice, and I see he again for the first time

last week. well, Otterburn be so much in love with Victoria that he

propose. she refuse he, so Otterburn, have a spirit of his own,

depart, and have never see she since. finding, therefore, that he

stand on his dignity, she fall in love with he, and I feel certain,

that if Otterburn choose to ask she again, she will say yes."



"but will he choose?"



"he will! they love one another devotedly, and each be ignorant of the

other's feeling, but when they meet everything will be arrange

satisfactorily. so you see, my dear Guy, the value of absence, for if

Otterburn haven't go away, he certainly would not have win the heart

of Victoria Sheldon."



"and you advise I to do the same?"



"I do, decidedly! leave your wife for a few week, and if she have any

love for you--which she must have, or else she would not have marry

you--she will miss you hourly, and when you come back--well the game

will be in your own hand."



Guy do not reply for a few minute, but urge his horse into a

canter, and the two ride along for some distance in silence. when

near Denfield, however, Errington suddenly draw his horse up, and

turn his head towards Eustace.



"I will take your advice," he say abruptly, "it can do no harm, and

it may do good."









CHAPTER XXII.

from the WIFE'S POINT of VIEW.



     "what be the pure love on earth?

      a maiden's love for summer mirth?

      a lover's worship of his idol

      when bell ring out his happy bridal?

      a patriot's when on foreign strand

      he suffer for his native land?

      a poet's or musician's love

      for thought inspire from above?

      ah, no, the love most undefiled

      be that the mother give the child."





Lady Errington be as usual in the nursery, sit in a low chair

near the window, watch "Sammy" playing on the floor. "Sammy,"

otherwise Henry Gerald Guy Errington, be now a year old, and look

what he be, a remarkably fine child, of which any mother might be

proud. "Proud," however, be too weak a word to use in connection with

Alizon's love for her child, see that this small scrap of humanity

roll about at her foot be worship by she with an affection

absolutely idolatrous. all her idea, her thought, her affection,

be bind up in Sammy, and have it be a question of death for mother

or child, there be no doubt that Alizon would have cheerfully yield

up her own life to save that of her baby.



nor be Sammy undeserving of worship, for he be really a beautiful

boy, with the frank expression of his father's handsome face, and a

healthy, sturdy little frame, which seem to defy disease. during his

twelve month of existence he have be very healthy, and even in the

delicate matter of cut his tooth have be more successful than the

generality of infant. with his rosy little face, his big, blue eye

and soft yellow curl of hair, he look as an obsequious nurse

name, and fat, plethoric and red-faced by nature, be at the end of

the nursery attend to some article of the young gentleman's

toilet, and Alizon have her child all to herself, for which privilege

she be profoundly grateful, as Mrs. Tasker be a terrible autocrat.



a wonderful change have come over she since she have become a mother,

for the statue have become a woman, the iceberg have melt, and in all

her life she never look so womanly as she do at this moment. she

face, flush a delicate rose-colour, be sparkle with animation,

her lip be part in a merry laugh, and her eye, soft and tender,

absolutely seem to devour the child as she bent forward to play with

he.



Sammy be sit like an infant Marius among the ruin of a carthage

of toy, for around he on all side lie the evidence of his

destructive capability. a woolly quadrupe, something between a dog

and a cow, dignify with the name of "Ba-lamb," lie on its back,

piteously extend one mangle leg, the other three having be

squeak, be be drag about by a string. there be several other

thing, such as a drum (break), a toy soldier (head miss), a

wooden Noah (paint suck off), and last, but not least, a hunting

crop of his father's, which be Sammy's special delight, because it

ben't suppose to be proper for he to have it.



Sammy at present be hammer "Eliza" (the doll aforesaid) with the

whip, when suddenly discover that one shoe have come off in his

exertion, he render thing equal by pull off the other shoe, and

then chuckle with delight at his success.



"Naughty Sammy," reprove his mother, bend down to pick up the

shoe. "mustn't do that--ah, bad child!"



the bad child, attract by the fact that both shoe be out of his

reach, make a snatch at they, with the result that he over-balance

himself, and come down heavily on his head. he be undecided whether

to howl or not, when his mother settle the question by pick he up

with a cry of pity, whereat, know the right thing to do, he howl

vigorously.



"Mother's own precious! mother's own darling!" lament Alizon,

rock he to and fro on her breast; upon which Sammy, find the

rock pleasant, roar louder than ever, whereupon Mrs. Tasker

hurry forward to give her opinion.



"why, whatever's the matter, my lady?" she ask anxiously. "he haven't

swallow anything have he?"



this be Mrs. Tasker's constant nightmare, for Sammy have an

ostrich-like capacity for swallow anything that come handy, and

dispose of all sort of thing in this manner, to the great detriment

of his stomach.



"he's hurt his head, Nurse," explain Lady Errington, anxiously,

while Sammy, satisfied at be the centre of attraction, stop

roar. "his poor head. he fall over on the floor."



"he's allay's doin' that," say Nurse in despair. "I nivir do see

sich a topply child. feather be lead to his upsetting."



the comparison be not a particularly happy one, but it serve Mrs.

Tasker, who thereupon want to take Sammy from his mother, a

proceeding to which Lady Errington strongly object.



"no, don't nurse please! let I hold he a little time! see he's quite

good now."



and indeed, Sammy be now behave like an angel, for be attract

by a small gold brooch his mother wear, he be stand up on his

sturdy leg, pluck at it with chubby finger, and gurgle to

himself in a most satisfied manner.



"I nivir do see such a dear child," remark Mrs. Tasker admiringly.

"'be 'owl be hoff as soon as on. why the last as I nusse, my lady,

be that givin' to hollerin' as you might 'ave think I'd put 'Im to

bed with a pin-cushing. but as for Master Sammy, well----" and cast

up her little eye to the ceiling, Mrs. Tasker express in pantomime,

with a pair of dumpy red hand, that word fail she.



"he's an angel! an angel!" murmur Alizon fondly, cover the rosy

Nurse express her firm conviction that there never be nor never

which Sammy accept with the great calmness as his just due.



at this moment a servant enter the nursery to inform Lady Errington

"I willn't be long than I can help, Nurse," she say dolefully,

"well, my lady," say Mrs. Tasker, with scathing irony, "I 'ope as

I've dress a child afore."



"yes! yes! of course," reply Lady Errington hastily, for she have a

"will it be long?" demand Mrs. Tasker determinedly, "because there

"no! only a few minute," say Alizon quickly. "you see, Nurse, I'll

both gentleman be wait in the dutch room, which receive its name

of box-wood, the bed of gaudy tulip and the fantastically clip

yew tree. Guy be in a much more cheerful mood than usual, as he

Como once more.



the soft sweep of a robe, the turning of the handle of the door, and

be this the pale, cold Undine he have last see at Como, more ethereal

creation of his dream brain.



"do you think I be lose in arabian solitude?" he say, recover

Eustace shake his head.



"I think so the other day, but now--I'm go on an exploring

"you must have the blood of the Wandering Jew in your vein."

"yes, shouldn't he?" observe Guy caustically, turn round. "go in

Alizon's eye, which have harden while look at Guy, grow wondrous

"Yes!--he be the dear child in the world--everyone love he except

"I daresay, but not of their own child," answer Lady Errington

she arise from her seat and leave the room quickly, while Eustace

"you shouldn't talk like that," he say quietly, "I don't wonder you

find thing disagreeable if you sneer at the child."

"perhaps, if you be as attentive to the baby as your wife, it would

"Nonsense! I cann't be on my knee before a cradle all day, and besides

Alizon willn't let I come near it. one would think I be go to

"Mr. Gartney," say Lady Errington's voice at the door. "come upstairs

with I to the nursery."



"cann't I come to Paradise also?" observe Guy wistfully as his cousin

be leave the room.



"certainly, come if you care to," reply Alizon coldly.



"no, thank you," reply Errington abruptly, his brow grow black

with rage at the coldness of the invitation.



"I'll stay here till you return."



Lady Errington go upstairs slowly with Eustace, with a look of anger

on her face.



"you see," she say bitterly, pause at the nursery door, "he do

not care a bit about his child."



"oh, I think he do," answer Eustace discreetly, "but he think

you do not want he to come."



"I be always glad for he to come," remark Alizon coldly, "but when

thing be decidedly wrong between this young couple, and they so

"my precious," in all the glory of white hat, white cape and woolly

"the complete Madonna now," think Eustace, as he look at the

"do you ever see a fine child?" demand Alizon, insatiable for

"no, never," answer Eustace, which be true enough, as he hate

chuck!"



"Goo! goo! goo!" gurgle Master Errington, and stretch out his

"oh, he's quite take to you," say Lady Errington gaily. "just feel

what a weight he be."



so Eustace be force to take the child in his arm, and look as

they be stand at the door, and Lady Errington wait there until

"be you?" he ask, eye she keenly.



Alizon flush a bright crimson, laugh in an uneasy manner and

they have enter the dutch room by this time and find it empty, but

Eustace judge this a good opening from which to lead up to his

"Lady Errington, don't you think you be rather hard upon guy?"



she turn her face towards he sharply.



"why do you ask that?" she demand coldly.



"I be afraid it be a liberty," answer Eustace slowly, "but you see I

be Guy's cousin, so the near relationship must excuse my apparent

"I be happy, perfectly happy I have everything in the world I

desire--health, wealth and my darling child."



"I see you don't count your husband among your blessing," say

"oh, yes! I'm very fond of Guy. he be the father of my child!"

"really, Mr. Gartney, I do not see by what right you speak like this

to I," she say with great hauteur.



"I beg your pardon," say Eustace, with cold politeness. "I be wrong

to do so."



Lady Errington begin to twist her marriage ring round and round, as if

betoken further remark on her part, so he wisely say nothing, but

wait for the outburst, which come exactly as he expect.



"I be very fond of Guy," she assert defiantly. "I would not have

marry he if I have not be fond of he. what make you think I'm

not? I suppose Aunt Jelly have be say something?"



"my dear Lady Errington," respond Gartney replace, his cup on the

table, "I have no right to speak as I do. I beg your pardon."



"please answer my question, Mr. Gartney," she say angrily, a red spot

of colour burn on either cheek. "have Aunt Jelly be say

anything?"



Gartney be not the man to remain in any difficulty where a lie could

help he out of it, so he reply to her question with the great

deliberation.



"Aunt Jelly have be say nothing. the only reason that make I

speak be that you seem to I to be fonder of the baby than of your own

husband."



the murder be out, and he be prepare for a storm, but it do not

come, as Alizon have quite as much self-control as himself.



"well, and what be wrong in that?" she say coldly. "I do love my

child more than my husband, any mother would."



"ben't that rather hard on the husband?"



"no! I do not see it! of course, I love Guy very much--much more than

he love his child," she finish with a burst of passion.



"I think Guy be very fond of the child," say Eustace quietly.



"he be not," she reply angrily, rise to her foot; "he grudge

every hour I spend with the boy. he would have I neglect the child

in order to be always with he. but there, what be the use of

talking?--neither you nor Guy can understand the feeling of a

this remark close the discussion so far as Eustace be concern, for

"you be right there, Lady Errington," he say good-humouredly, "I be

look a little knock up, so I want to take he to town with I."

"no, thank you," say Eustace, rise and hold out his hand. "I

have some letter to write this evening, but I will come over

to-morrow and see you before I go back to town."



"that's right," answer Lady Errington, smile as she press his

hand. "goodbye at present. come to-morrow, and I will show you the

she go to the door, when it suddenly open, and Guy enter.



"oh, here you be, Guy," she say sweetly, as he stand hold the

Mr. Gartney be go away."



"and where be you go?" ask Guy, with a half-smile on his stern

"can you ask?" she say archly. "to the baby, of course." and with a

"well?" he demand curtly.



"well," answer Eustace coolly, "I do what I could--but of course,

my dear fellow, it's a very delicate matter, and really I have no right

to interfere in any way."



"what do she say?" demand Guy roughly, turn as white as a sheet.



"she say you have well go to Town with I," answer Gartney

reluctantly.



Guy burst out with a harsh laugh, and turn towards the window with a

gesture of despair.



"Good God! and I'm break my heart for that statue."









CHAPTER XXIII.

MRS. VEILSTURM'S "AT HOME."



     "I hate 'at home,' they're simply inquisition

        to torture human being into fit;

      a mixture of plebeian and patrician,

        on whom in judgment Mrs. Grundy sit;

      Sonatas play by second-rate musician,

        and milk-and-water joke by would-be wit;

      such squalling, scandal, crush of man and ladies--

      it's like a family party down in Hades."





as this be the first victory he have ever obtain over his

egotistical nature, Eustace feel most unjustifiably proud, and view

his action with great self-complacency, therefore the good result of

such victory merely become egotism in another form. his attitude

towards Lady Errington have certainly alter, but not for the well,

as the fantastic adoration he have formerly feel towards a vision of

his own creation have change to an earthly love for the real woman, in

which there be mingle more of sensuality than platonism. eustace be

certainly not a coarse man in any sense of the word, but he have

regard the visionary Lady Errington so long as his own special

property, withhold from he by the accident of her marriage with

Guy, that when he see the flesh-and-blood woman _riant_ in all her

newly-find vitality, he view she as a Sultan might view a fresh

odalisque add to his _serail_. the pale lily have change into the

rich red rose, and the spiritual being of his fevere imagination have

take the form of a beautiful woman, full of temptation to an ardent

lover.



any sensible man would have see from the short conversation he have

have with Lady Errington that love for the child fill her heart to

the exclusion of all else, but Eustace, with supreme egotism, deem

that she love the child simply because her husband be not worthy of

her affection and when he deign to worship she she would certainly

forget the pale passion of maternal love under the fierce ardour of

his devotion.



with this idea in his mind it be no wonder he feel that he be

exercise great self-denial in try to bring husband and wife

together, and in renounce his desire to gain possession of a woman

for whom he feel an unreasone admiration. however, be determine

to carry out this new mood of asceticism to the end, he take Guy up to

Town with he, and try to amuse that moody young man to the good of

his power, which be a somewhat unsatisfactory task.



see that he have abandon his scheme to gain Alizon's love, he do

not intend to speak to Mrs. Veilsturm, as he have now no desire to

entangle Guy with another woman, but as he be go to an "at home"

give by Cleopatra, he do not hesitate to take his cousin with he in

the ordinary course of thing.



Eustace know more about Mrs. Veilsturm than she care he should know,

as he have meet she at Lima, in South America, when she be--well, not

Mrs. Veilsturm--and he judge a woman of her harpy-like nature would

not strive to annex anyone but a rich man. Guy be not rich, so

Eustace think she would leave he alone--a most fatal mistake, as he

have unconsciously place Cleopatra's revenge within her grasp. Mrs.

Veilsturm have neither forgive nor forget the deadly insult offer

to she by Lady Errington, but hitherto, owe to Guy's devotion to his

wife, have be unable to entangle he in any way. now, however, Fate

be play into her hand, and when she receive a note from Eustace,

ask if he might bring his cousin to the house in Park Lane she feel

a savage delight at such a stroke of unforeseen luck, but, be too

clever a woman to compromise her scheme in any way, write a cold reply

to Mr. Gartney, tell he he could bring Sir Guy Errington--if he

like.



of course Eustace do like, and as Guy, who have quite forget all

about the episode between Mrs. Veilsturm and his wife, listlessly

acquiesce, they both arrive at Cleopatra's "at home" somewhere about

five o'clock.



"I seem to remember the name," say Guy, as they struggle up the

crowded stair.



"you certainly ought to," respond Eustace, "see that she be about

the well-know person in Town."



"ah, but you see I'm a country cousin now," say Guy with a faint

smile. "hang it! what a crush there be here."



"that's the art of give an 'at home,'" answer Eustace drily, "you

put fifty people who hate one another in a room build to hold twenty,

and when they're thoroughly uncomfortable you give they bad music,

weak tea, and thin bread-and-butter. after an hour of these delight

they go away in a rollick humour to another Sardine Party. oh, it's

most amusing, I assure you, and--well, here we be, and here be Mrs.

Veilsturm."



Cleopatra have certainly not lose any of her charm, and look as

imperious and majestic as ever, stand in the centre of her guest,

array in a startling costume of black and yellow, which give she a

strange, barbaric appearance. there be no doubt that she wear too

many diamond, but this be due to her african love for ornament, and

with every movement of her body the gem flash out sparkle of light

in the mellow twilight of the room.



a foreign musician, with long hair and pale face, be play some

weird eastern dance on the piano as Eustace enter and bow before

she, and it suddenly flash across his mind that this sensuously

beautiful woman be quite out of place amid these cold english blonde

and undecided brunette. she ought to be toss her slender arm in a

tropical forest, to the shrill music of pipe and muffle throbbing of

serpent-skin drum, whirl in the mystic gyration of some sacred

dance before the shrine of a veiled goddess. the sickly odour of

pastille, which she be fond of burn in her drawing-room, assist

this fancy, and he be only rouse from this strange vision by the

mellow voice of his hostess bid he welcome, as she touch his

hand with her slender finger.



"I be glad to see you, Mr. Gartney," she say, with a slow smile; "it

be indeed kind of you to call so soon after your return. and your

friend, whom you be to bring?"



"be here," reply Eustace, present his cousin, "Sir Guy

Errington."



Guy bow, feel somewhat bewildered at her rich loveliness, and,

with a swift glance from under her heavy eye-lash, she shake hand

with he.



"Mr. Gartney's friend be mine also--but you be welcome on your own

account, Sir Guy."



"you be very kind," answer Errington mechanically, "I think the

obligation be on my side, however."



"he's a fool," decide Mrs. Veilsturm in her own mind, as she look

at his fresh, simple face; "I can twist he round my finger, and I

will, if it's only to spite his wife."



at this moment eustace be seize upon by Mr. Dolser, who be on the

look-out for copy, and, much against his will, be drag to the

other end of the room by the pertinacious little man, leave his

cousin in conversation with Mrs. Veilsturm.



the room be quite full of all sort and condition of man and woman.

Cleopatra know everybody in the literary, artistic, and musical world,

and they all come to her reception, so that it be quite a treat to

find somebody there who have do nothing. this happen on occasion

when someone who have not do anything be bring to worship someone

who have. there be plenty of lady novelist in all shade, from

blonde to brunette, pick up idea for their next three-volume

publication; pale young poet, with long hair and undecided leg, who

write rondel, triolet, and ballad, hint, in wonderful rhyme, at

thing fantastical; dramatist, young and old, full of three-act play

and hatred of manager and critic. a haggard young man of the

impressionist school droop in a corner, discourse of Art, in the

new jargon of the studio, to the last fashionable manageress, who

do not understand a word he be say, but pretend to do so, as

she want he to paint her picture. everyone present have an eye to

business, and each be pursue his or her aim with vicious

pertinacity.



"mixed lot, ben't they?--yes!" say Mr. Dolser superciliously, when

he have get the unhappy Eustace pin up in a corner; "don't they

cackle about themselves too--rather see that stout old party in the

corner, in the damage millinery--new novelist, you know--disease

school--Baudelaire without his genius--write 'the Body Snatcher'

--yes!--read it?"



"no," respond Eustace, shortly, "and I don't intend to."



"it be rather a corker for weak nerve," say "the Pepper Box"

proprietor, affably; "there's gibble--perfect genius as critic;

always slash a book without read it. he's destroy more

reputation than any one I know. yes! ah! fancy Maniswarkoffi

be here--pianist, you know. English, only they wouldn't have

he under his real name of grub, so he go abroad and dig up

his present jawbreaker. draw money now, and smash two piano a

week--beautiful!"



in this way Mr. Dolser artlessly prattle along, destroy a

reputation every time he open his mouth, much to the disgust of

Gartney, who want to get away.



"excuse I," he say, in despair, "but I see a friend over there."



"ah! do you really?" reply Dolser, put up his eyeglass. "oh,

Macjean, ben't it? yes. just come back from America. have a row with pa

because he want he to marry some Scotch lassie. yes."



"you seem to know all about it?"



"yes, yes; oh, yes. business, you know--and by Jove! talk about

that, I want an interview with you about your book."



"then you willn't get one."



"that's all you know," retort Mr. Dolser. "what? you willn't tell I

anything? never mind, I'll make up a few fairy tale. if they ben't

true that's your look-out. Ta, ta! look in 'the Pepper Box' next week.

Jove! there's Quibbles. 'cuse I, I want to ask about Bundy's

divorce," and he disappear into the crowd.



it be no use be angry with the little man, as he be so very

good-natured with all his impudence, so Eustace merely smile, and

move across the room to Otterburn, touch he on the shoulder.



"you here?" he say, in a tone of glad surprise. "I _be_ glad! I be

just go away."



"not enjoy yourself?" observe Eustace, lean against the wall.



"can any one enjoy himself here?" retort Otterburn in disgust. "I'm

tired of hear people talk about themselves; and if they talk about

anyone else----"



"they abuse they thoroughly. my dear boy, it's the way of the world.

by the way, you get my note about Victoria?"



otterburn colour.



"yes; I'm very much oblige to you," he reply, in his boyish

fashion. "if it be only true what you think, that she do care for

me----"



"of course she care for you."



"it seem too good to be true."



"do you think so?" say Gartney, drily. "oh, I beg your pardon. I

forget you be in love!"



"cold-blooded cynic," laugh otterburn, "go thou and do likewise."



"with your awful example before I--hardly," reply Mr. Gartney, with

a kindly look in his eye. "do I tell you Errington be here to-day?"



"no. be he really?--and Lady Errington?"



"oh, she's in the country. but Errington seem as if he want wake

up, so I bring he to town with I."



"by the way, how be Lady Errington?"



"very much change--and for the well. my prophecy concern the

incomplete Madonna have come to pass. she be a mother now, and adore

her child."



"indeed! and be she go to adore her child for the rest of her

life?" ask Otterburn, flippantly.



Eustace shrug his shoulder.



"I suppose so. she certainly cann't adore her husband. Guy be a real

good fellow, as I've always maintain, but no woman in the world

would put he on a pedestal."



"Poor Errington! be he as fond of his wife as ever?"



"Fonder, if possible."

CHAPTER XXV.

FASCINATION.



     "Snake! snake! your treacherous eye,

      grow and deepen to marvellous sky,

      star shine out in the rosy space,

      every star be a woman's face,

      flushed and wreathe with amorous smile,

      draw my soul with magical wile,

      Vision! while I be rapt in thee,

      Death be come unknown to I.

      Snake hath catch I fast in his toil,

      round I wind his shine coil,

      ah, from dream with a start I wake,

      Thou host sting I, oh cruel snake."





Most man of strongly imaginative nature be superstitious, and

Gartney be no exception to the rule, his instinctive leaning in this

with this belief, it be little to be wonder at that he believe he

on his return to England, he have determine to abstain from see

Major Griff speak to Eustace, think he could stop the affair by

could do nothing. be therefore unable to do anything, the Major

could only look on in a cold fury at Cleopatra strive to ruin

Mrs. Veilsturm's intimate friend be also very indignant about what

they please to call her infatuation, little dreaming of the real

Mr. Dolser that keep the affair out of the scurrilous page of "the

Pepper Box," but although it have not appear in print, the whole

affair be an open secret.



dolly Thambits, who be in love with Cleopatra, be furious at the way

in which he be neglect, but this kind of treatment only make he

all the more in love with his disdainful mistress, much to the relief

of Griff, who be afraid that the boy would escape from his toil.



in the midst of this whirl of rage, envy, and revenge, Guy, see no

special favour in Cleopatra's condescension, be quite cool and

compose, be the most unconcerned person of the whole lot. of

course, no one dare to speak to he about the real fact of the case,

and of the enmity he have provoke, so he remain in complete

ignorance, anxiously await for a letter from his wife ask he to

return.



that letter never come, however, for Alizon be perfectly happy with

her baby, and miss Errington no more than if he have be a stock or

stone. she know nothing of the peril to which her husband be

expose, and, curiously enough, none of her London friend write and

tell she, else she might have be for once startle from the serene

pleasure of motherhood.



accord to his promise, Otterburn call upon Aunt Jelly, and be

graciously receive by that strong-minded lady, who take a great fancy

to he. as yet, he have not speak outright to Victoria, but still the

young couple understand one another, and such understanding be

approve of by Miss Corbin, who see in otterburn the very husband she

would have choose for her ward. so Otterburn call on the old lady

pretty often, and bring she all the news of the town, while

Victoria, feel completely at rest concern her lover, listen

quietly.



all her idea of make otterburn propose, and then refuse he out

of revenge, have quite vanish, as she be now passionately in love

with he, and accord to the position now strangely alter since

those old day at Como, it be for she to crave and for he to grant.

otterburn, however, know nothing of this, but woo in all honour and

timidity, while Aunt Jelly, like a good but grim cherub, look on in

silent approval.



it be during one of Otterburn's visit, that by chance he let fall

something of what be go on between Mrs. Veilsturm and Guy,

whereupon the old lady, have an eye like a hawk, immediately see

that something be go on of which she know nothing. with this idea

terribly wroth against her nephew.



Eustace call to see she, and she speak to he about it, but eustace

but could now do no more. Aunt Jelly, therefore, be alarm, not

only for the happiness but for the respectability of the Errington

having despatch this, she work herself up into such a fury over

the whole affair that she take a fit, and for some time be in danger

of dying, but her indomitable spirit assert itself, and with iron

determination she arise from her bed of sickness to see her nephew.



it be a fight between Cleopatra and Aunt Jelly for possession of Guy,

but all this time Guy have no more idea of play his wife false, than

he have of return Mrs. Veilsturm's openly-display passion.









CHAPTER XXVI.

AUNT JELLY INTERFERES.



     "what vow you make at the marriage altar,

        for well and bad, to take your wife;

      yet at the moment of need you falter,

        quail at rumour of come strife.

      Nay, it be wise to cling and cherish,

        Altho' thing evil be say and do;

      if in the future you both should perish,

        Husband and wife should be lose as one."





Aunt Jelly be look very pale and ill on the day she elect to see

Guy in order to expostulate with he on the wild way in which he be

behave. she be suffer from a very serious disease connect with

the heart, and Dr. Pargowker warn she against any undue excitement,

as it might prove fatal. he be seat with she now, a fat, oily man

of the Chadband specie, and talk about her ill-health in his usual

unctuous manner.



in her accustomed chair sit Miss Corbin, look wear with illness,

but as grim and defiant as ever, while the doctor stand near she

feel her pulse with one hand, and hold his watch with the other.

Minnie, ever watchful of her patroness's comfort, hover round like

an unquiet spirit, bring all sort of unnecessary thing, which

make Aunt Jelly very irritable and lead she to say unpleasant thing to

Miss Pelch which reduce the poetess to tear.



"well?" say Miss Corbin sharply, when Dr. Pargowker have finish with

her pulse, "what do you say? be this illness serious?"



the doctor lift one fat white hand in gentle protest, and resume

his seat with a comfortable sigh.



"no, dear lady, no," he say in his heavy, soft voice, "do not I

beg of you think you be so bad as all that. you remind I, if I may

be permit to make the comparison, of a dear friend of mine who

departed----"



"bother your dear friend!" snap Aunt Jelly in her grim manner.

"I don't ask you here to tell I other people's history. I want to

know about my own state of health."



Dr. Pargowker fold his chubby hand complacently on his rotund

stomach and meekly venture a protest against this language.



"do not, oh dear lady," he say unctuously, "do not excite yourself

like this. it be bad for you, dear lady, very bad."



"very bad, dear Miss Corbin," echo Minnie tearfully.



"and might lead to complication," pursue the doctor, shake his

head.



"complication," echo Miss Pelch, put her handkerchief to her

eye.



"Minnie," say Aunt Jelly politely, "you're get a big fool

every day. have the goodness to hold your tongue and not talk of

thing you know nothing about. Dr. Pargowker, if you will kindly leave

off nod your head like a chinese mandarin, and tell I straight

out what you mean, I should feel obliged."



"Dearest lady," growl the doctor, "it be useless to conceal from you

the painful fact that you be very ill."



"I know that sir," retort Aunt Jelly coolly, "go on."



"you must avoid all undue excitement, such as dance, theatre, and

see friend."



"I haven't be to a dance for the last twenty year," say Miss

that rubbish. what do you mean by talk such nonsense to I?"



"easily upset, I see," murmur Pargowker, apparently to himself,

"very easily upset."



"wouldn't you like a little pillow for your head, dear Miss Jelly?"

say Minnie, hold one over Miss Corbin as though she be go to

"I'd like a little common sense," retort Miss Corbin, push away

the pillow, "but it seem I'm not likely to get it."



"be calm, dear lady, be calm," observe Dr. Pargowker, nod his

"Pen, ink, and paper, Minnie!" order Aunt jelly, glare at the

the oblige Minnie fly to obtain these necessary, and having do

Aunt Jelly and Dr. Pargowker be old friend, and never part

"Lord know how you get patient," she say, fold her bony hand,

"be you talk about your prescription, or yourself?" snap Miss

don't kill I, I've no doubt the other will."



"pardon I, dear lady," say the doctor, smile blandly, "you be

in error. this prescription will do you a great deal of good. oh, we

will pull you round, yes--yes. I think I may venture to say we will

pull you round."



"pull I round or square, it's easily see I'm not long for this

world," reply Miss Corbin.



"oh, do not speak like that, Miss Jelly," whimper Minnie, "you will

get quite well, I'm sure of it."



"aye! aye!" remark Pargowker, fold up his prescription. "while

there's life, there's hope."



"don't quote your proverb to I," say Aunt Jelly, determine not to

"no! dear lady, no!" say Pargowker, rise from his seat, and

"if you know he as well as I do, you might well say that," reply

"if you will not excite yourself----"



"I'm not go to excite myself," retort Aunt Jelly, "but I'm go

Dr. Pargowker take up his hat and button his coat with the air of a

"if you attend to my order," he say, speak more sharply than be

"Good Heavens!" ejaculate Aunt Jelly, with feeble merriment. "have

"oh, Miss Jelly!" murmur Minnie, in tearful protest.

"I've no doubt about it," cry Aunt Jelly, as he pause at the door,

"certainly with you, dear lady--certainly with you," say Dr.

"you want to talk about I behind my back," say Miss Corbin,

"it's no use, dear lady," he say, with playful ponderousness,

"I never obey anyone in my life," say Aunt Jelly, stiffen she

"Dearest Miss Corbin, I be in earnest."



"so be I," retort the old lady, frown. "there! there! go away,

Dr. Pargowker sigh, yield to stern necessity, and speak.



"well, you can do so, my dear, old friend, but only for five

"quite enough for all I've get to say."



the doctor look waggishly at Miss Corbin, in order to keep up her

"so ill," say Pargowker, put on his hat, "that if she's not keep

"oh!" say Miss Pelch in an alarmed tone, and would have ask more

Minnie return to the drawing-room, with a cheerful face, so as not

"what else?"



"nothing!"



"Minnie, you be deceive I," say Aunt Jelly solemnly. "I can see

"you're not die," whimper Minnie, begin to cry.



"I'm not far off it, at all event," retort Miss Corbin, with a

"oh, but you will--you will," cry Minnie, weeping.



"Pooh! nonsense, child," say Miss Corbin, kindly, "don't waste your

"Dr. Pargowker say you be not to see Sir Guy long than five

"quite long enough."



"and be not to excite yourself."



"there, there, Minnie!" say Miss Jelly, impatiently. "I'll take good

here?"



"four o'clock, dear Miss Corbin."



"it's nearly that now," observe Aunt Jelly, look at the clock. "I

hope he willn't keep I wait. young man be so careless now-a-day.

Miss Sheldon have go out?"



"yes! to the Academy with Mrs. Trubbles and Mr. Macjean."



"neither of whom know anything about picture. it mean flirt, not

art, I've no doubt. well! well, we must not be too hard on the young.

let I leave the world in peace, that's all I ask."



Minnie put down her work, and come close to Miss Corbin, whose thin

cold hand she take in her own.



"dear Miss jelly, don't talk like that," she say, softly, "indeed you

will get well, I'm sure you will."



"no, child, no!"



"oh, but, yes," persist her companion, fondly. "why, whatever would

I do, if you do not live to read my little volume?"



"oh, it's come out, then?" say Aunt Jelly, grimly, with a flash of

"yes, Mr. Gartney have arrange it all. I be go to keep it a

"there! there!" she say, touch Minnie's face, with unaccustomed

"it's go to be call 'Heart Throbs and Sad Sobs, by Minnie

Aunt Jelly be silent for a few minute, feeling, rather a choking in

"I hope you're not angry," she say, timidly smooth Aunt Jelly's

the old lady be so touch by Minnie's poor little attempt to

"oh, but you have!--you have!" cry Minnie, throw herself on her

"if I have speak harshly to you, dear, on occasion," say Aunt

age, and an embitter nature. but the heart be there, my dear, all

the time the heart be there."



"I know it was!--I know it be!" wept Minnie, pat the wither

hand of her old friend. "I have never doubt that."



"yes! yes!" mutter the old dame dreamily, "the heart be there."



and there be silence for a few minute, only break by the sob of

Guy look rather haggard about the face, as he sit down near his

"so you've come at last?" she say, straighten she back, and

fold her hand on her knee.



"I come as soon as you send for I," answer Guy, quietly.



"you should have come without an invitation," say Aunt Jelly, with a

frown, "but young man of the present day seem to take a delight in

neglect those nearest and dearest to they."



this be say pointedly, with a view to draw forth some remark

about Alizon, but Guy do not take it in that sense.



"I don't want to neglect you, aunt," he say moodily, "but our

conversation be not so pleasant that I should look forward to they."



"I only speak for your good."



"People always do that when they make disagreeable remark," reply

Errington sarcastically. "you're not look well to-day, Aunt Jelly."



"I don't feel well either," respond his aunt shortly. "I'm die."



"oh, no, don't say that," say Guy, heartily shocked at her remark.



"but I will say it," retort Miss Corbin, nod her head

vigorously, "and I'll say something else too that you willn't like."



"I've no doubt you will," answer Guy crossly, rise to his foot.

"look here, Aunt Jelly, you're not well to-day, and if you bring I

here to quarrel, I'm not fit for it."



"you're fit for nothing in my opinion except the Divorce Court," say

"I don't know what you mean by talk about the Divorce Court,"

"think and see."



"what's the good of my do that?" cry Errington angrily, "I don't

know what you mean."



"don't shriek," say Miss Corbin coolly, "it go through my head."



"I beg your pardon aunt," reply Guy politely, "but if you would tell

Aunt Jelly sit in silence for a moment, rap the finger of one

"oh, be that it?" he say with a short laugh. "don't worry your head

"all the world do know."



Errington arise from his seat with a smothered ejaculation, and

"a very pleasant woman, indeed," sneer Aunt Jelly scornfully.

"no, it be not true?" answer Errington slowly. "I have be a great

I could not refuse."



"a very nice explanation," say his aunt disbelievingly, "but do you

think it be one your wife will accept?"



"my wife know nothing about my visit to Mrs. Veilsturm."



"indeed she do," reply Aunt Jelly coolly. "I write and tell she

all about they."



Guy's face grow as pale as that of a corpse, and he stare at Miss

Corbin as if he have be turn into stone. at length, with an effort,
