voice.



"you write and tell she all about they?"



"yes! I  do not think your conduct be right, so, as your wife have

most influence with you, I write and tell she to call you back to

Ellington."



all the blood in his body seem to surge up into his head with the

violent effort he make to suppress his anger. have it be any one else

but this feeble old woman, he would have simply let his passion master

he, but in this case, with such an adversary he could do nothing.



"God forgive you, Aunt Jelly," he say at length, "you've do a cruel

thing," and he turn and walk slowly to the door.



"I have do what be right," say Miss Corbin bravely. "you be

deceive your wife, and I be determined she should know of your

deception."



Sir Guy turn towards she as he pause at the door, and when she

finish speak, answer she slowly and deliberately.



"you be quite wrong. I be not deceive my wife, as I can prove to

you. as you know, my wife have treat I very cruelly during the last

year, and neglect I in every way, give all her love to the child.

Eustace come down the other day, and advise I to leave my wife for a

few week, think she would not be so indifferent on my return. I

take his advice and come up to Town. Eustace take I to Mrs.

Veilsturm, and find she a very pleasant woman, I simply go there

in order to amuse myself. but as for care about she, I love and

respect my wife and my name too much to degrade myself so far.

unluckily, until the other day, I do not remember that Alizon

dislike Mrs. Veilsturm, because she be mix up with her father in

some way, and forbid she to visit at the Hall. have I remember this,

I would not have go there, but it's too late now to think of it. by

believe all these malicious story, which I give you my word of

honour have no foundation, and write to she, she will believe that I

go to see this woman on purpose, and she will never forgive I. I be

go down to the Hall by to-night's train, and will try and explain

everything to she, but I'm afraid she will not believe I. no doubt

you act for the good, Aunt Jelly, but in do so you have simply

ruin my life."



"Guy! Guy!" moan the old woman, who have listen to all this with a

sense of stunned amazement. "forgive I! I  do it for the good, but I

will write again and tell she how wrong I have be."



"it be too late," he reply sadly, "too late."



"no, it's not too late, Guy. but forgive I! forgive I!"



Errington look at she coldly.



"if my wife forgive I I will forgive you," he answer, and leave the

room.



Aunt Jelly stare at the closed door, and strove to call he back, but

her voice die in her throat, a mist come before her eye, and

overwhelm by the fatal discovery she have make, and the excitement

she have undergo, she fall back in a dead faint.









CHAPTER XXVII.

the deity call FATE.



     "believe I, sir, the deity call Fate,

      be strong than the strong of we all,

      Fate! Fortune! destiny! what name you will!

      we be the sport of some malignant power,

      who twist and turn the action of our life,

      in such strange fashion that our good intent

      --Not evil in themselves--breed evil thing,

      and wreck our fair venture, tho' we strive

      to bring they holily to some quiet port."





on leave Miss Corbin's house Errington's first impulse be to drive

straight to the railway station, catch the six-thirty train, and go

down to the Hall at once, in order to explain matter to his wife. a

moment's reflection, however, convince he that this would be a

foolish thing to do, as he could not possibly reach home before eight

o'clock, and his late arrival at such an hour without be expect

would be sure to cause comment among the servant. they already

guess more of the strained relation between himself and his wife

than he like, so in order to avoid the slight chance of any

further remark be make, he determine to go down to Denfield next

day in the ordinary course of thing.



he therefore drive back to his hotel, and while dress for dinner

ponder deeply as to the good course to pursue with Alizon. on this

night he be engage to dine with Macjean at the Soudan Hotel, and

recollect that his cousin be to be of the party. eustace be a man

in whom he have a profound belief, and frequently defer to his

cousin's judgment in delicate matter, so on this present occasion he

make up his mind to speak to Gartney, whose clear head would doubtless

be able to solve the problem.



it be true that Mrs. Veilsturm expect he to call for she at the

Marlowe Theatre, where she have a box. but the idea of be in her

company again after what have transpire be too much for he, so he

hastily scribble a note excuse himself on the plea of sudden

indisposition, and send it off to Park Lane by a special messenger.



"Macjean and Laxton can go to the theatre as arrange," he think, as

he go slowly down the stair, "and I'll make eustace take I to his

room, where we can talk over thing at our ease."



with this determination he jump into a hansom and drive off to the

Soudan Hotel in Piccadilly, where he find otterburn wait for he

in company with Laxton.



"where's Gartney?" ask the Master after greet his friend, "he

promise to be early."



"Eustace's promise be like pie crust," reply Errington, give his

cloak and hat to the waiter, "make to be break."



"you look very broken yourself," remark Macjean meditatively, as the

gaslight fall on Guy's face. "what be the matter? have you have bad

news? will you have a glass of sherry?"



"nothing be the matter," reply the baronet categorically. "I have

not have bad news, and I will take a glass of sherry."



he really feel very worried over the position in which he now find

himself regard his wife, but it be well he should dine in

company than alone, as a solitary meal would only make matter appear

much bad than they really be. besides he be go to consult

Eustace, who, he feel certain, would advise he for the good, so he

put the good face he could on the matter, and chat gaily over his

sherry to the two young man while wait for his cousin.



presently Eustace, cool, calm and unconcerned, arrive, with a large

appetite and an apology for be late.



"I've get a man who be in the habit of mislay thing," he explain

as they all sit down to dinner, "he mislay his brain when he be

bear, and haven't find they yet, so I suffer in consequence. no sherry

for I, thank you! water, please!"



"Ugh, London water," groan Laxton, hold up his sherry to the

light.



"Water," remark Mr. Gartney sententiously, "be the pure of all

element."



"not in town," retort Macjean with a grimace. "I don't believe in

Adam's wine."



"no Scotchman ever do as far as I know," say Eustace drily.

"presbyterian wine be what you all prefer north of the Tweed."



"and a very good idea too," observed Guy, contribute his quota to

the conversation, "especially on wet day."



"that's why such a lot of whisky be consume in the Land o' Cakes,"

explain Eustace gravely, "it's always wet up there. Scotch mist and

Scotch whisky invariably go together."



"this," remark Laxton, allude to the conversation, "be not a

teetotol meeting."



"no one could possibly accuse it of be that," retort Gartney,

with a significant glance at the full glass, "but if you three

gentleman don't mind talk, I'll eat in the meantime. the Soudan

cook be a good one, the Gartney appetite be a large one, so thank God

for all his mercy and leave I to pay attention to the good thing

of this life."



his three friend laugh at his humorous way of put thing, and

devote themselves to the fish. the conversation go on in a more or

less frivolous fashion, the last scandal, the blunder of the Cabinet,

the new novel of the realistic school, the prospect of a war in the

East--all these be discuss in their turn by the quartette, and

then Laxton begin to argue with Otterburn about the african

expedition, so seize the opportunity Guy bend forward to speak to

Eustace.



"I want to talk to you after dinner," he say in a low voice.



"certainly," reply Gartney carelessly, "but will you have time? what

about the theatre?"



"I've change my mind," say Guy quickly, "so I send an excuse to Mrs.

Veilsturm. have you anything particular to do? if not we can go to

your room. I willn't detain you long."



Eustace flash a keen look on his cousin, and pause a moment before

reply:



"I be go to look in at one or two drawing-room to-night," he say

at length, "but as my engagement really ben't very particular, I'll

not trouble about they, so I will be at your disposal."



"thank you," answer Guy, draw a long breath.



"nothing wrong, I hope?"



"well that be as it turn out. I see Aunt Jelly to-day."



"ah!" say Eustace in a significant tone, know that an interview

with Aunt Jelly always mean trouble of some sort. "I think I can

understand. however, let we go on with our meal. pleasure and appetite

first, business and Aunt Jelly afterwards. what be those two boy

fight about?"



the two boy be still engage in the african argument, and have

arrive at a dead lock, each be firmly convinced in his own mind

that his view of the subject be the right one.



"you're all wrong, I tell you," say Otterburn hotly, "you're talk

just like you do at Montana. Africa ben't America."



"nobody say it be," return Laxton ungracefully, "but I daresay the

sport be very much the same in both place. Africa be not a new

planet."



"you might as well say that pot walrus in the Arctic region be

the same as jungle shooting in India."



"it's merely a matter of temperature," declare Laxton decidedly.



"oh, if you pin your faith to the thermometer, I've nothing more to

say," reply Otterburn, throw himself back in his chair with the

air of a man who have crush his opponent.



"I haven't the least idea what you be talk about," observe

Eustace leisurely, "and judge from what I've overhear you both seem

to be in the same predicament."



"we'll discuss it later on," say otterburn gaily. "what a pity I

cann't come out with you to Africa, Laxton, and settle the argument

that way."



"well, why don't you come?" demand Laxton quickly.



otterburn redden and laugh in an embarrassed fashion, while

Eustace throw a roguish glance at he, and make answer for the bashful

lover.



"don't you bother your head, Laxton there be more important thing

than shoot expedition in this world--at least, otterburn think

so."



Laxton be quite in the dark regard the meaning of these mystic

utterance, when it suddenly dawn on he that the lady whom

Otterburn have speak about in America might have something to do with

the turn the conversation have take, and lift his glass with a smile

as he look towards Macjean.



"to the health of the something more important than shoot

expedition," he say gravely, and finish the wine.



"thank you," respond otterburn laughing. "may I some day drink the

same health to you?"



"never!"



"never's a long time."



"and talk about time," remark Guy, glance at his watch, "if you

two boy have any idea of the theatre to-night you'll have to be off."



"ben't you come too?" chorusse Otterburn and his comrade.



"no! I  receive an important piece of news to-night, about which I

wish to speak to my cousin."



"what will Mrs. V. say?" ask Laxton gaily.



"who can foretell a woman's remark?" say Eustace quizzically, see

that Guy be disinclined to speak.



"depend upon how much you know of the woman," respond otterburn

smartly.



"Woman," retort the cynic, "be an unknown quantity."



"what about quality?"



"this conversation," say Eustace, look at his glass of water, "be

get problematic. after dinner be a bad time to solve puzzle,

therefore--coffee."



it seem a good suggestion, so they all adjourn to the

smoking-room, and indulge in further conversation while they enjoy

their coffee and cigarette. shortly afterwards Otterburn and his

friend depart for the Marlowe Theatre, while Eustace in company with

Guy go off to his room in Half Moon Street, Piccadilly.



use as he be to hardship in foreign land, Eustace always take care

to make up for his deprivation by make himself very comfortable at

home, consequently his room leave nothing to be desire in the way of

luxury. his valet be well accustomed to his master come in at all

kind of unexpected time, consequently when they arrive the room

be well light, the chair dispose in tempting corner, and a

spirit-stand with glass and soda-water stand ready for any thirsty

soul.



Eustace place his cousin in a well-cushion chair, give he an

excellent cigar, then, light one himself, take his seat opposite to

Guy and prepare to play the part of father confessor.



it be a hot night and the window be stand slightly open,

let in the pleasant, confused noise of the street, with its

rattling of cab, voice of people, and footfall of innumerable

pedestrian. the faint sound of a barrel organ play the last new

tune, "oh, she's leave I for another," come softly to their ear, and

they sit smoke silently for a few moment until Errington speak.



"I tell you I see Aunt Jelly to-day."



"yes and what do she say?"



"a good many disagreeable thing," reply Guy bitterly; "accord to

her showing, I must be a singularly wicked man."



"Aunt Jelly," observe Eustace philosophically, "know very little

about the actual world, and having live apart from her fellow

creature for many year, have form in her own mind an ideal life to

which she expect all her friend and relation to conform.

unfortunately, the majority of nineteenth century people be neither

lucrece nor bayard, consequently Aunt Jelly, in Pharisee fashion,

rail at the world and say, 'thank God, I'm not as other woman be.'"



"she be as other woman be in the matter of listen to gossip," say

Guy emphatically, "for she tell I it be common talk that I have leave

my wife for the superior attraction of Mrs. Veilsturm."



Eustace look up suddenly in dismay.



"my dear fellow, you must be make a mistake."



"I'm make no mistake," return Guy doggedly. "Aunt Jelly say it be

common talk. have you hear anything about it?"



"you know I never pay attention to gossip," say Gartney evasively, "I

don't even listen to it, but you may be certain that anyone who pose

as the _cher ami_ of Mrs. Veilsturm willn't escape calumny."



"I don't pose as the _cher ami_ of Mrs. Veilsturm," say Errington

fiercely. "I don't care two straw about she."



"action speak louder than word. you certainly have act as if you

do."



"Good Heavens, Eustace, you surely don't believe all these lie?"

retort Guy wrathfully, rise from his chair.



"I never say I do," answer his cousin coolly, "but I'm look at

it now from the world's point of view. Mrs. Veilsturm have certainly

make a dead set at you, and you, think it be natural amiability,

have play into her hand. you, no doubt, call it friendship, but the

world don't."



"it be friendship. indeed, hardly that as far as I be concern, as I

don't care if I never see Mrs. Veilsturm again. she have take an

unaccountable fancy to I, and I'm no Joseph where a pretty woman be

concern, but as for leave my dear wife for a meretricious woman

like that--good God!"



"well, let the world talk as it like, so long as it ben't speak

the truth," say Eustace impatiently. "who care? if you expect

justice from your fellow creature, you willn't get it. as to Aunt

Jelly, old woman be privileged gossip. it don't matter to you."



"but it do matter to I, I tell you," cry Guy violently, walk

to and fro, "she have write all about these lie to my wife."



the barrel organ outside be still grind out the popular tune,

be now assist by the shrill voice of a girl singe the word of

the song.





     "oh, she's leave I for another,

        Mary Anne! Mary Anne!

      and she say he be her brother,

        Mary Anne.

      it may be true, for all I know,

      but would she kiss her brother so,

      and would she leave I for he? no!

        Mary Anne, Mary Anne!"





the regular beat of the melody seem to repeat itself everlastingly

in Gartney's ear as he sit there in silence wonder over the

statement Errington have make. if Alizon know all, she would never

forgive her husband and then--be it fate that so persistently

smooth the road for his evil do? he feel dull and stupid at the

unexpected announcement he have hear, and, after a pause, lift his

heavy eye to Guy.



"well," he say drearily, "and what do you intend to do?"



Errington sit down heavily in his chair and stretch out his hand

with a weary gesture.



"I don't know what to do," he answer in a dull voice. "I suppose the

good thing will be for I to go down and explain matter to Alizon."



"but will she accept your explanation?"



"no!"



"then why make it?"



"a drown man will grasp at a straw. I must do something! I cann't

let my wife think I have wilfully wrong she. good heaven! surely

she must know I love she dearly."



"I should think it be very probable she do," answer Eustace

slowly, "besides, I think Lady Errington be too sensible a woman to

give ear to lie report. tell she all you have tell I, and I'm

certain you will have no difficulty in make your peace with she."



"do you think so?" ask Guy, his sad face brightening, "but no, I'm

afraid not. you remember the story I tell you about Mrs. Veilsturm's

card be return."



Eustace nod.



"that be the difficulty. if it have be any other woman than Mrs.

Veilsturm--but as it be, she'll think I do it wilfully."



"surely not."



"my dear fellow, you've never love a statue," say Errington

bitterly, rise to his foot and put on his cloak, "but it's no

use talk any more. Aunt Jelly have do more harm than she know of.

I'll go down to the Hall to-morrow, and tell Alizon everything. if she

believe my explanation, well and good, if she do not----"



"well?" ask Eustace, see his cousin hesitate.



"well!" repeat the other harshly, "I shall come back to London and

Mrs. Veilsturm."



he be go before Eustace could offer a word of remonstrance on the

folly of such a determination, and then Gartney return to his seat

with an air of utter lassitude.



"Kismet," he say to himself, after a long pause. "it be Destiny."



be it indeed destiny that have interfere for the third time? be it

fix by Fate that he should be Lady Errington's lover, and lose his

honourable name for her sake? it seem like it, see that all

barrier he have set up against this illicit love, be sweep away by

the action of other people, and the field leave open to he. still,

Alison have not yet have her interview with Guy, and, as she must know

how much he love she, surely she would accept his explanation of the

lie report concern his infatuation for Mrs. Veilsturm.



if she do so, all would be well with they both, but if she refuse to

believe his story, and dismiss he coldly, then----



Eustace arise to his foot, and walk over to the window, look out

into the hot night. below, the glare and glitter of gas-lamp--above,

the luminous light of the star--and far in the east, rise over the

sombre masse of cloud, burn an evil planet, which be dread of

old by the Chaldeans.



the man look at it with troubled eye feel the twin power of good

and evil strive in his heart.



and the star gleam steadily in the thunderous sky.









CHAPTER XXVIII.

HUSBAND and WIFE.



     "you have break your oath

        and break my heart,

      oh, sorrow for both,

      you have break your oath;

      although I be loth

        in anger to part,

      you have break your oath

        and break my heart."





Alizon Errington be seat in the dutch room with Aunt Jelly's letter

clench in her hand, and Sammy play on the carpet beside she. the

child, roll among his toy be babble inarticulate word of

endearment to they, but the mother's eye be fix on the gaudy bed

of tulip blaze in the sunshine as she think over the word she

have just read.



so this be her husband! this man who have go straight from his home,

from his wife, from his child, to the arm of this infamous woman. he

know more than the world do about the character of Mrs. Veilsturm,

for she have tell he herself. he know that she, his wife, have refuse

to receive this adventuress and have return her card! he know that

Mrs. Veilsturm, Cleopatra, whatever she like to call herself, have

be connect with disreputable Gabriel Mostyn, and yet, in spite of

all this, he have dare to enter her house, to clasp hand with she as

a friend, to sacrifice his honour and that of his wife to this vile

woman.



be there any faith or honesty in man?



her father have be bad and vicious all his life; he have destroy his

daughter's belief in the male sex by the terrible revelation of his

death-bed, but her husband--oh!--she have think he well than this:

she have respect and admire he, she have be a good wife, hold

her head high and keep her honour spotless. she be a good mother

to his child, and she have do she good in all way to fulfil the vow

make at the marriage altar.



this be her reward! she be desert for another woman, for a woman

who be the vilest of her sex. her wifely honour have be drag in

the mud, her wifely name have be place with jeer in the mouth of

man and woman, and the marriage tie, so sacred in her eye, have be

violate by her husband, by the very man who should have respect its

sanctity.



her first bear be play at her foot in the happy innocence of

childhood, a pure soul fresh from the hand of God, who have give she

this treasure to nurse and cherish. yet even now, in its artless

babyhood, the shadow of a dark shame be hover over its golden

head, the name it bear be already smirch in the eye of the world,

and its father, who be responsible to God for its well-being, have

already degrade it by his own shameful passion.



ah! all man be the same. her father be only the type of many

other. they love a woman, or say they love she, and stay beside

she for a time, yet as soon as they grow weary of she, they fly to

the arm of some new fancy, and not even the sanctity of the

marriage tie could restrain their brutal nature. Guy, whom she

think so good and kind, have turn out the same as his fellow man.

he have be a good husband for a time, but now, grow weary of his

quiet home, satiate with domestic love, weary of his prattle child,

he have deliberately fling himself into the arm of this light-o'-love.

well, he would have his reward. the wage of sin be death! and he

would be drag down to destruction by those arm that encircle he

so fondly.



but what about herself? what could she do in order to free herself

from the companionship of this man who prize she less than he do his

dissolute companion? divorce! yes, that be the way to break the

chain which bind she to the husband she despise. but it be

impossible that she could take advantage of the law, for it would

reflect on the child in the future, and for the child's sake she would

have to remain in the bondage of marriage.



Tearless, cold, and pale as a lily, she sit there with her hand

clasp the hateful letter which tell she of her husband's treachery

and destroy the happiness of her life. the child, weary of its toy,

crawl across the carpet to her foot, and clutch her dress raise

itself to its foot with a plaintive cry. she look downward in

dry-eyed misery, see the wax-like tiny hand clasp her dress, and

hear the thin little voice utter its inarticulate prayer to lie on

her breast.



the full horror of her position break on her dull brain like a flash

of light, and with a burst of tear she take up the child and strain

he convulsively to her bosom.



ah, how those tear fall--hot, scald tear that blister she

cheek, that burn into her very soul, and that fall on the

frightened face of the baby like rain, bitter and salt as the wave of

the sea. the child be afraid at this passionate outburst of sorrow

and begin to cry, but she hold he close to her breast and,

restrain her tear, hush he to slumber with a low lullaby

rock to and fro, her heart heavy as lead.



"Alizon!"



with a cry she arise to her foot, the sleep child in her arm, and

see her husband, travel-stain, worn, and haggard, stand at the

door with a look of implore agony on his face. she draw herself up

to her full height and shrink against the wall, with one arm stretch

out to keep he off, the other hold the tiny form of the child, and

at her foot the crumpled letter that have be the cause of all this

undoing.



Guy make a step forward and stretch out his arm.



"Alizon!"



"Don't--don't come near I!" she say in a low, hoarse voice, with a

look of horror on her pale face.



"I come to explain----"



"nothing can explain that," she answer, point to the letter on

the floor, "nothing can explain that."



"I can explain it, if you will only listen," he say vehemently. the

mark of tear be still on her cheek, but no other trace of

emotion remain to show how she have suffer.



as her husband speak, a cold, scornful smile creep over her face, and

she sign to he to go on, still shrink against the wall with her

arm fold round the child as if she would keep it from be

contaminate by its father.



"I see Aunt Jelly," say Sir Guy hurriedly, "and she tell I what she

have do. write to you about--about Mrs. Veilsturm."



he bring out the hate name with a great effort, but his wife,

neither shrink nor wincing, stare straight at he with that

terrible frozen smile on her face.



"she write under a mistake," pursue Errington, clasp the back of

a chair in his strong finger as though he would crush it to dust. "it

be not true what she say. I tell she all about it and she believe

I. I be go to tell you now, and you will believe I, will you not,

Alizon?"



"I cannot tell."



the word drop slowly from her mouth, and he fling out his arm

towards she with a cry of anguish.



"you must believe I--you must, I tell you," he say breathlessly. "it

be not true about that woman. I go up to Town with Eustace, and

call at she house----"



a flush of angry red pass over her face, and she turn on he like

a tigress.



"you call on she! you call on that woman!" she say in a clear,

vibrate voice, tremulous with anger. "the woman about whom I tell

you--whom I would not receive, and you--you--my husband, dare to put

this insult upon I."



"Alizon----"



"don't speak far! I have hear enough. that letter be true, and

you cannot deny it."



"I do deny it," he cry fiercely. "I tell you it be all a mistake. I

forget all about your refusal to receive Mrs. Veilsturm. have I

remember I would not have go."



"ah!" she say with ineffable scorn, "if you have remember. what

excuse be that to make? do my word weigh so lightly with you that you

could forget they so easily? it be not for anything that Mrs.

Veilsturm have do to I that I decline to receive she. but I hear

my father, on his death-bed, speak of she--speak of she as man such as

he be speak of such a woman as she be. I tell you this, and yet you

forget my word and visit she."



"as God be my judge, I do forget," he say desperately. "I do not

think about it until it be too late."



"ah, you do remember at last."



"yes! only it be too late. I have be to her house and she----"



"and she," echo his wife bitterly. "oh, I well know what you be

go to say. she do she good to captivate you with her vile art,

try she hard to win your heart from me----"



"but she do not succeed--she do not succeed," he say earnestly.



"do you think I care if she do or if she do not?" reply Lady

Errington scornfully. "do you think I would place myself in rivalry

with that woman? no! you have choose she in preference to I, your

lawful wife. go to she as soon as you like, but don't dare to come

near I."



"I will come near you," say Guy desperately. "you have no right to

judge I like this."



"I have the right of a wrong woman."



"no, no! I  swear you have not. on my soul; on my honour----"



"on your honour," she interrupt with a sneer, "the honour of a man

who could act as you have do!"



"whose fault be it if I have act badly?" he cry, render

desperate by her jeer.



"do you mean to infer it's mine?" say his wife quietly.



he gnaw his moustache viciously and do not respond, whereupon she

be about to ask the question again, when a knock come to the door and

startle they both.



"it be the child's nurse," say Lady Errington, go to the door.

"wait a moment."



Guy turn towards the window so that the servant should not see how

upset he be, and Lady Errington, open the door, keep she face bent

over the sleep child as she place it in Mrs. Tasker's arm.



"he's sound asleep, Nurse," she say quietly, as the old woman take

he. "take he up to the nursery, and I'll come to he in a short

time."



her voice be perfectly under control, and Mrs. Tasker never for a

moment suspect anything be wrong between her master and mistress as

she toil slowly up the stair carry the child tenderly in her

stout arm.



Lady Errington draw a long breath as Mrs. Tasker disappear, and

then, close the door quietly, turn once more to her husband, who

still stand look out at the bright sunshine, which seem to mock

his misery by its glare and cheerful brilliancy.



"I be wait for your answer," say his wife's steady voice behind

he, whereupon he turn swiftly round, and cross to where she

stand, stern and silent by the table, catch one of her hand before

she could prevent he.



"Alizon," he say earnestly, "for your own sake, for the sake of our

child, listen to I quietly, and I will try and explain thing to your

satisfaction. I do go to Mrs. Veilsturm's, but I swear by all that be

sacred, that I do not remember anything about she. not even her name.

think for a moment, the whole affair pass in five minute--your

explanation be a hurried one, and you never refer to it again. it

be eighteen month ago, and since then her name have never be

mention between we, so you can hardly wonder that I quite forget

about the woman. have I remember, I would not have go--give I at

least that credit. I go innocently enough with Eustace, and Mrs.

Veilsturm, I suppose out of revenge for the slight she receive from

you, be very attentive to I. I do not respond to her advance in

any way, and see as little of she as I could. I be not responsible

for the coupling of our name together. you know how the world talk

and magnifie the most innocent thing into evidence of guilt. the

scandal reach the ear of my aunt, and she, innocently enough, write

that letter to you--a letter which she now bitterly regret having

send to you. when she tell I about it, I explain all, and she ask

my pardon for having write the letter. I come down here at once to

tell you everything, and I have now do so. on my honour, Alizon,

that be the whole affair. I act wrongly in forget about Mrs.

Veilsturm's past, and I ask your pardon. let this misunderstanding

cease between we. I love you dearly. I have always love you, never so

much as now. do not let our life be blight like this. I have act

wrongly, and I ask your pardon. you in your turn grant it to I, and

let we forget this terrible mistake."



all the time he be plead, she listen to he without any sign of

emotion, her face look impassable as a marble mask, but at the

conclusion of his speech, she withdraw her hand from his with a cold

smile of disbelief, which show how little his tenderness affect

she.



"your explanation would satisfy the world," she say with chilly

dignity, "but it do not satisfy I. I cannot believe that you forget

about my refusal to receive Mrs. Veilsturm. even if you do forget,

that only make your conduct bad, for you still go to visit she

after you recollect the affair, as you acknowledge yourself. I have

be a good wife to you, I have be a good mother to your child, and

in return you have not even give I the common fidelity of a husband,

which every woman have a right to expect."



"I see it be no use plead to a cold piece of perfection like you,"

say Guy, draw himself up with dignity. "I have stoop to explain

this affair, and you decline to believe I. I can do no more. you be

convince, without the shadow of a reason, that I be vile, and it be

impossible for I to undeceive you far than I have do. under

these circumstance it be impossible for we to live together as man

and wife. you doubt I, and I resent your do so, therefore it will

be good for we to at once make some arrangement about our future

life."



he speak calmly enough, but his heart be hot with indignation, that

he should receive such treatment from the woman he love well on

earth. he be innocent, and he know himself to be innocent, therefore

all his nature rise in revolt against the unjust attitude take up by

his wife.



she, on her side, be also indignant, deeming that his explanation be

false from beginning to end, so she refuse to forgive he, or to

believe the skilful tissue of falsehood he have put forward as a plea

for her mercy.



it be a case of misunderstanding on both side, and as the stubborn

pride of each refuse to bend, nothing be now leave but separation.



"for the sake of the child," she say coldly, "I be unwilling there

should be any scandal, so it will be good for I to stay down here to

look after the boy, and you can take up your abode in London, or

wherever else it please you. regard money matter, I presume you

will allow I sufficient to live on in a style befit the mistress

of this place. my life will be devoted to bring up the child,

your--well, I have nothing to do with that, and you be free to act

as you desire. these be the only term upon which I will consent to

pass over the matter, and I think there be nothing more to be say."



slowly and deliberately she utter these cruel word, which fall like

ice on his heart, and show he how utterly futile it be to hope for

any reconcilement with this pure woman, so pure that she could neither

understand nor forgive the infidelity of which she accuse he. all

his manhood arise in rebellion against such treatment, and, mad with

anger, he step to her side as she turn to leave the room.



"there be more to be say," he cry furiously. "I have tell you the

truth, which you decline to believe. but if I have conduct myself as

you say--if I have voluntarily go to this woman whom you hate, who be

to blame, you or I? have I not be a good husband to you since our

marriage? have I not strive by every mean in my power to win your

heart? and what have I receive in return?--cold word and frigid

smile. do you think that I do not feel all this? yes, I do feel it,

but you, wrap up in your icy nature, cannot understand my feeling."



"I have treat you with all respect----"



"respect! respect!" he reiterate bitterly. "I ask for love, you give

I respect. I ask for bread, you offer I a stone. all the feeling of

my heart have be crush down by your cold superiority, by your

chilly self-respect, which forbid you give to I those attention

that other man receive from their wife."



"you dare to talk to I like this," she say angrily, "you, who have

have no respect either for I or for your child!"



"ah, the child," he retort with a sneer laugh, "it be the child

that come between we. you have lavish upon it all the love and

affection which be due to I. be I not the child's father? why should

you treat I as if I be a block of marble? in my own house I have

be lonely. in my own house I have be neglect, while you, leave

I to starve, give all your love to the child."



"be it a crime for a mother to love her child?"



"no, it be no crime. I do not say it be. but it be a crime--bad

than a crime--to cherish and love the child to the exclusion of the

husband and father. the husband have the first claim on the wife's

heart, the child the second."



"you be wrong."



"no, I be right," he reply vehemently, "and if drive forth by

neglect, and hungry for love, I leave my home to go to another woman,

you reproach I for what be your own work! but I have not do so. I

have be as true to you as you have be to I. Alizon, let thing be

as they be before this miserable misunderstanding, and let there be

love and affection between we. I will forgive you all the neglect I

have suffer these eighteen month, if you will overlook my

forgetfulness about Mrs. Veilsturm, and act towards I as a wife

should act."



"you forgive I," she say contemptuously, "you forgive I? no. it be

I who have the right to do that. I do not forgive you. I never shall."



"be those your last word?"



"my last word."



Errington look at she in silence for a moment, and then, without a

word, walk towards the door of the room, at which he pause.



"I have implore and entreat you to be merciful," he say, with

terrible calmness, "you have refuse to grant what I ask. now I go

back to London, to Mrs. Veilsturm, the woman you despise so much. you

have drive I to this, and the result of it rest on your own head.

you do not love I, you never have love I, so I leave you alone in

your immaculate purity, to forget the man whom you have despise and

wrong."



he be go before she could utter a word, and she be leave alone in

the room, alone in the world, with nothing but her child to comfort

she in the hour of need.









CHAPTER XXIX.

the question of marriage.



     "the sea be cruel, its white wave hide I,

      Lo I be weary and scant of breath,

      Thou to a haven of safety guide I,

      stretch out thy hand, lest I swoon to death.



     "thou art my God in this hour of peril,

      yet in thy sight, I be lose and vile,

      all thy love, as the sea be sterile,

      I sink, I perish, beneath thy smile."





there be always two side to a question, especially to the question

of marriage.



one side be invariably take by the husband, the other by the wife.



both claim their side to be right, and, as this be an impossibility,

one side must be wrong.



which?



it be a difficult question to settle, more difficult than the judgment

of Solomon, more difficult than the judgment of Paris, and though the

world, represent by the Law, generally play the part of arbitrator

in conjugal dispute, in this case it be refer to neither by the

husband nor the wife.



under these circumstance it will be as well to argue both side

fairly, and pronounce a verdict in favour of the strong.



a case for the opinion of Society, unrepresente by any legal

tribunal, the party concern conduct their own case personally.



on the part of the wife--



"when I marry Guy Errington, I have no belief whatsoever in the

masculine sex, such scepticism be due to my knowledge of the

character of my father, Gabriel Mostyn. before his illness I live in

almost conventual seclusion, and from the reading of book form an

ideal world, which I have since find to be as unreal as the fantastic

vision of oriental dreamer.



"my world be base upon a delusive belief in the chivalry of man and

the purity of woman, and resemble in its visionary loveliness the

Garden of Eden, before Eve tempt Adam with the fatal fruit. in this

unreal world man be always young, handsome and true-hearted, while

the woman be beautiful in their form and face, pure in their

life. I dream that some day I, an inhabitant of this beautiful

universe, would meet with a lover who would dedicate his life to mine,

and we would go through life side by side in love and purity, until we

exchange this heaven upon earth for one even fair.



"alas! these be but the virginal dream of a girl, unsullie by

contact with the world, and my ideal life be shatter by the vile

cynicism of my father, who take a delight in destroy all my

illusion, and in drag I down from the light of fancy to the

darkness of reality.



"so evil have be his life, that no one would stay by he in his hour

of need, and I, a young girl, unsophisticated and innocent, be force

to remain beside his bed. to he I dedicate my youth, my innocence,

my womanly feeling, my filial tenderness, and receive as a reward a

brutal unveiling of the most horrible thing on earth. when I go to

his bedside at the beginning of those four bitter year I be an

innocent girl, when I turn away, leave he stiff and stark in his

coffin, I be, in knowledge, an accomplished woman of the world. I

believe in no one. I doubt the motive of all. I look upon my

fellow-creature as bird of prey who would turn and rend I be I

not dexterous enough to foil they with their own weapon. be it then

to be wonder at that I dread marriage with a man who would

doubtless be as evil in his thought and deed as be my father?



"have I be in receipt of a sufficient income to keep I from

starve, have I be able to earn my own living, I would never have

marry; but under the grudging hospitality of my relative, and the

iron grip of poverty, the strong resolution must give way. I be no

heroine to battle with the merciless world as represent to I by my

father, so, in despair, I marry Guy Errington.



"to my surprise and delight, I find he to resemble the ideal

inhabitant of my fanciful world, and honour and respect he for

those quality which I have never see in my father. he be good,

kind, loving and tender, all of which quality to I, in a man, be

like a revelation from God. still, the teaching of my father could

not be easily eradicate, and I dread lest some chance should rend

the veil which hide his real nature and show I the innate brutality

which my father assure I exist in all mankind.



"meanwhile, I be thankful for his kindness, and strove to show by

every mean in my power how I reciprocate his love. if he accuse I

of coldness, I can offer no defence. I be not a demonstrative woman,

as all my timid outburst of affection be ruthlessly crush by my

father, and self-restraint have become a habit with I. besides,

dread lest my married happiness should not last, I wear my coldness

as an armour against a possible disappointment.



"I love my husband, but the invincible mistrust which my father have

inculcate in my breast isolate I during the early portion of our

married life, and I be afraid to let my husband see how much I love

he, lest he take advantage of such confidence. still, I want

something to love, something that I could worship, could cling to,

something that I could trust in fully and that would not deceive I.



"it come at last, a pure, little, white soul from the hand of God; and

to my child I give the whole of the love, the adoration, the passion,

which have be pen up in my breast for so many year for want of some

one on whom I could bestow they without fear of the consequence.



"my husband hate to see I so fond of the child, for his jealous

nature would be content with nothing but undivided love, and in spite

of my desire to make he happy, I could not leave my child unloved in

order to pander to his selfish passion. he resent my reproval of his

folly and withdraw himself from my society, so that I have no one to

love but my child, and, although we live in the same house, the pole

be not far asunder than we be.



"then she come between we--that vile woman whom my father know in

South America--and my husband, weary of his home, of his wife, of his

child, leave all to go to she. what wife could put up with such an

insult? have it be any other woman, it would have be bad enough,

but this special woman whom he know I despise, whom he know from my

lip to be an infamous creature, this be the woman for whom he

forsook I.



"how can I believe his explanation? they be all false, glibly as

they be utter. no! I  be deceive no long, he be the same as my

father, and seek only the selfish gratification of his own appetite.

the end have come, as I know it would--the mask be tear off, and I see

my husband, whom I love and trust during the early day of our

marriage, as he really be. my father be right; there be no faith,

honour, honesty, nor truth, in man; and I have only act rightly in

refuse to live with a man who could behave so to his wife and child.



"even now he be with that woman, on the feeble plea that my coldness

drive he away. do that excuse his vice? no! he should have wait

until perfect love, perfect understanding, be establish between we,

but now we be part for ever. he have go back to the life most

congenial to he, and I--I, like many other woman, can do nothing but

pray that my son may not grow up to follow in the evil footstep of

his father."



on the part of the husband--



"saint do not live among man, except in the canonization of the

Church, and before my marriage I be neither well nor bad than any

other young man. but without be either a Saint Anthony or a Saint

Francis, I do my good to lead a decent life in every way, and if I

have a few vice--or what ascetic term vice--they be so small that

they be invisible except to the microscope of certain Pharisees who

pass their life in find out their neighbour' fault, and thank

God they be not as other man be.



"I love my wife from the first moment I see she, be in the first

place attract by the beauty of her person, and in the second by the

difference in her nature to that of other woman. I do not put myself

forward either as a deep thinker or as a student of humanity, but must

confess I grow weary of the ordinary Society woman, married or

unmarried. they talk in a frivolous fashion of the most trivial

thing, but Alizon Mostyn attract I by the charm of her

conversation, not that she be very learn, or particularly

brilliant, but she talk of ordinary matter in an original way,

which be wonderfully fascinating. I love she dearly, and see in this

pale, quiet girl, one who would be a companion to I, who would make

I a well man, and aid I to lead my life on a high plane to that

which I have hitherto do.



"it be for this reason I marry she, and though she be cold in her

manner towards I, this very coldness have a certain charm about it

which I could not resist. I know that she have be badly treat by

her father, so strove in every way by tenderness and love to make

amend for the misery of her early life.



"after marriage I be perfectly satisfied with my wife, and although

at time her persistent coldness wound I, yet I think by

unfailing love and attention to make she open her heart to I. no

doubt I would have achieve this object if it have not be for the

birth of the child, which have, in a great measure, be the cause of

all the trouble of our later married life.



"I be glad to welcome the child, as I think it would form a new

link between we, and by thaw her frigid disposition draw we close

together. but, instead of do this, the boy be the cause of our

estrangement, as she lavish upon he all the love of which she

nature be capable, and I be persistently neglect.



"no doubt the world would think I have little to complain of--my wife

be perfect, both in her conjugal and maternal capacity--the only

trouble be the cherishing of the child to the neglect of the

father.



"but, look at the matter from my point of view. I have marry my wife

for companionship, for the sake of satisfy the craving of human

nature to be love, and instead of my idea be realize, I find

myself shut out of Paradise, while my wife, with her child, rest

happily within. she be never away from the boy, and day after day I

be force to live a lonely life, neglect and uncare for by a woman

I adore. all her idea, conversation, and desire, be bind up in

the child, so that she have neither the time nor inclination to take an

interest in my pursuit, or in my life. we dwelt together as man and

wife, to all appearance we be a happy and attached couple, yet the

child stand between we, like an evil shadow, which isolate we the one

from the other. often I try to break down this barrier, by praise

the child, but the mother seem jealous even of the father; she

want the child all to herself, and, secure in such possession, be

content to treat her husband as an ordinary friend.



"I resent this state of thing, I revolt at be condemn to

occupy such an isolated position, but I could do nothing. my wife be

perfect in every other way, and to have complain would have be

ridiculous, so I be force to suffer in silence. God alone know how

I do suffer in the solitude to which I be condemn, at see the

love and caress bestow on the child, love and caress in which I

have no share. all her life be in the child, and she possess he. my

life be in she--and I be a stranger to she in every way.



"under the circumstance I think it well to go away for a few week,

think that she would miss I in some little measure, and would be

more affectionate and tender when I return. whether such an idea be

right or wrong I do not know, I never shall know, for between our

parting and our meeting occur the episode of Mrs. Veilsturm.



"on my honour, I go innocently enough into the presence of this

woman. I have forget all about my wife's refusal to receive she, for

have I remember I certainly would not have go. but, as I say

before, I have forget. I have never see the woman; I do not even

know her name. how then be I to recollect the episode of eighteen

month before?--an episode the memory of which have not last long

than a few day.



"I go to Mrs. Veilsturm's 'at home.' I find she a charming woman,

and, at her express invitation, I go often to her house. she be

different from the ordinary run of woman, and I take pleasure in her

society, but there be no warm feeling between we, at least, not on

my part. with the scandal of the world I have nothing to do, sin and

purity be treat the same way, and the mere fact of my be once or

twice see with Mrs. Veilsturm be sufficient to set afloat the lie

story which come to my wife's ear through the medium of Aunt Jelly.



"to my wife I tell the whole story, but she refuse to believe I. I

confess that I have remember about Mrs. Veilsturm when it be too

late, but she accuse I of know the truth from the first, and of

having wilfully act as I have do. nothing I could say could shake

her belief in this matter, and she swear she would never forgive I

for the insult I have place upon she.



"what could I do? nothing! except retire from the scene. in vain I

assure she of my complete innocence. she refuse to believe my

statement, and drive I from her presence--from my home--with cruel

word. this woman, wrap up in an armour of purity--of selfish

purity--could not credit my innocence in any way. she judge I from

the 'i-would-not-have-act-thus' standpoint, and insist that I have

betray she basely, although she have no further proof than the gossip

of the world.



"I leave she. I come back to London to see Mrs. Veilsturm again. it be

wrong--I know it be wrong--but what be I to do? live an isolated

existence, pass day and night of abject misery, only to pander to

her self-righteous idea? for eighteen month, in spite of all my

tenderness and love, she have wilfully neglect I, she have refuse to

acknowledge that I have be a good husband, she have render my life

miserable, and now she have drive I forth from my own home on account

of a sin--if it can be call so--of which I be guiltless.



"what be I to do? live the life of a hermit in order to right myself

in her eye and be call back and pardon, as if I be indeed

guilty? no! I will not do so. it be her fault, not mine, that I be

place in such a miserable position. unable to win she by tenderness,

by love, I will henceforth live my own life and see what neglect will

do. for every pang she have inflict upon I I will inflict a pang

upon she, for her month of neglect I will repay she in full, for she

coldness I will give coldness in my turn, and to any remonstrance she

may offer I will say then what I say now--'It be your work.'"





so far the case of husband and wife, each argue from their own

point of view. now which of they be right, the man or the woman? the

husband who strove to win his wife's love, or the wife who refuse to

give the husband that love which be his due.



Errington be now act wrongly, as he himself know; he be

voluntarily fling himself into the arm of a woman whom he know to

be worthless, but who can say he have no provocation? he have do his

good to win his wife's love, he have suffer in silence during the

period of his married life, and in return she have shamefully neglect

he, and have finally, with hardly any proof, accuse he of

voluntarily make a friend of a worthless woman. outrage by this

treatment, the husband leave her presence, and she have drive he into

the very jaw of destruction.



Doubtless he should have stand firm, and by year of patient

self-sacrifice show she that she be wrong. but how many of we be

capable of such asceticism? how many of we would stand for long year

in the outer darkness, know himself to be guiltless of the crime

lay to his charge?



this woman--pure wife, affectionate mother, as she be--have act as

if she be above the weakness of human nature. she have arrogate to

herself the function of the deity in judge and condemn a poor

human soul, who, weary with beseech for what it never receive,

fall away in despair into the gulf of sin and misery.



who be wrong--the man who seek evil in despair, or the woman whose

coldness and purity have deny he the mercy which would have save

he?









CHAPTER XXX.

CLEOPATRA VICTRIX.



     "to my chariot wheel have I bind he,

        to bear he in triumph away;

      as master and king have I crown he,

        to reign but the length of a day.

      I woo but to kiss and betray he,

        we meet but a moment to part;

      in the hour of his joy will I slay he,

        my wheel will go over his heart."





Mrs. Veilsturm's drawing-room be not by any mean an artistic

apartment, be full of violent contrast in the way of decoration

and furniture, yet not without a certain picturesqueness of its own.

it be bizarre, gaudy, fantastic, strange, and a faithful reflection

of the curious mind of its mistress. the european side of her nature

inspire she with a certain amount of artistic taste, while the

african blood in her vein make her delight in brilliant colouring and

barbaric ornamentation. the eye ache as they rest on the confused

mass of tint, variegate as a flower-garden, and yet there be a

certain design and harmony throughout, something like the tangle

pattern of those oriental carpet, those indian shawl, which

represent the cloudy splendour haunt an eastern mind.



the paper on the wall of this room, oblong and lofty, be of a

dark-red tint, stamp with golden sunflower rise from their

velvety-green leave. delicate lace curtain of milky white,

interweave with thread of silver, fall before the three long window,

from under massive gilt cornice. the carpet be of black and yellow

stripe in undulate line, like the skin of a tiger, and here and

there a rug of silky-white hair contrast curiously with the

fantastic ground upon which it rest. the furniture be of dark

walnut, upholstered with bright yellow satin, smooth and shine as

the inside of a buttercup.



in the corner of the room stand slender palm with heavily-droop

leave, vividly-green fern with feathery frond, prickly, fleshy

cactus and spiky, fan-shape plant, suggestive of tropical

sky--some rise from the porous red jar of Egypt, other spring

from misshapen vase of porcelain, on which, in crimson and green,

sprawl the sacred chinese dragon, and a few grow in shallow

basin of pale-yellow pottery.



at the end of the room, behind the veil of indian bead curtain, be a

cool-looking conservatory, skilfully light by electric lamp in

globe of pale green, which diffuse a kind of fictitious moonlight.

in the drawing-room the mass of colour, strange and incongruous, be

soften, blend, and confuse by the tremulous red light that

stream from the tall brass lamp with their umbrella-like shade of

crimson silk. add to this fantasy of light and colour, the sickly

odour of the pastille constantly burn, and it can be imagine how

curiously appropriate this strange room be to the rich eastern beauty

and oddly barbaric costume of Cleopatra.



on this night, having be down at Hurlingham, she be too tired to go

out, so preferred to remain at home and receive a few friend.



at present, she be lie negligently back in a low chair, array in

her favourite costume of amber and black, but, despite the attention

of Dolly Thambits, who be talk to she, she seem to be out of

temper. Mr. Jiddy, seat on the extreme edge of a chair like a white

cat, be listen to the conversation of Major Griff, who, stiff and

grim, be lean against the mantelpiece. no other people be

present, nor do Mrs. Veilsturm seem very much inclined to receive

company, for she yawn once or twice, and look at the Major

significantly, as if to hint that he might take away Mr. Thambits and

friend as soon as he like.



the Major, however, want to speak to Mrs. Veilsturm himself, so he

do not take the hint, but resolutely wait on, in the hope that the

two young man would shortly depart and leave he alone with the

charming widow. meanwhile he chat about pigeon-shooting to Mr.

Jiddy, who know nothing about it, and thambit bore Mrs. Veilsturm to

death by his dreary small talk.



"I say, you know," drawl dolly, after a pause, during which Mrs.

Veilsturm have be wonder how she could get rid of he, "what about

your fancy-dress ball?"



"oh, I've put it off," reply Mrs. Veilsturm idly, "a week or two

do not make much difference, and my costume be not ready."



"what be you go to appear as?"



"ah! that be the question," say Cleopatra smile. "I'm not go to

tell you. I'm not go to tell anyone. I will appear at my own ball

in the most unexpected fashion."



"like a surprise packet?"



"yes! as you elegantly put it--like a surprise packet."



"oh, that's jolly," observe Mr. Thambits brilliantly, then relapse

into silence.



"I say, Mrs. Veilsturm!" he say at last.



"yes."



"Errington's go to the country again."



Mrs. Veilsturm could not suppress an angry start at this information.

she have miss Guy for the last three or four day, and, having hear

nothing from he since she receive his note excuse himself from

come to the Marlowe Theatre, be considerably enrage at this

neglect. she be too clever, however, to betray herself to dolly

Thambits, who be jealously vigilant, so she ask quietly:



"indeed! who tell you so?"



"Gartney! he go about four day ago. got tired of Town, I suppose."



"no doubt! town do get wearisome at time."



"I don't think so while you be here," say Mr. Thambits tenderly.



"what a charming compliment," answer Mrs. Veilsturm with a forced

laugh, shut her fan savagely, for when dolly be amorous he be

simply detestable.



"not to you," he murmur softly.



"More compliment," she say coolly. "you must pass your day make

they up. by-the-way, would you mind tell I the time?"



"certainly. it be now a few minute past nine."



"oh, I say, be it?" cry Mr. Jiddy, jump up from his chair. "I

say, dolly, we've get to go to Lady Kalsmith's you know."



"I think you be come also, Mrs. Veilsturm?" say dolly, rise

reluctantly.



"I! no," she answer, lift her eyebrow. "would I be dress like

this if I be go?"



"Mrs. Veilsturm," explain Major Griff, graciously, "be too tired to

go out to-night, and think a rest will do she good."



"I'm afraid we've tired you," say thambit, look at his divinity.



"oh dear, not at all," respond Mrs. Veilsturm, lie with the utmost

dexterity. "so glad to see you. _Au revoir_ at present."



"I'll call and see if you be well to-morrow," say dolly, make

his adieux with manifest reluctance.



"delighted! goodbye, Mr. Jiddy! major?"



Grill take the hint, and usher dolly and his friend out of the room

before they have time to change their mind, and having see they

safely bestow in a hansom, return to Mrs. Veilsturm, whom he find

sit in her old place, frown savagely at the fireplace. the

Major resume his lounge attitude on the hearthrug, and light a

cigarette.



"don't smoke," say Mrs. Veilsturm sharply. "I don't want my

drawing-room to smell like a bar."



"there's not much chance of that," retort the Major coolly, throw

the match into the fireplace, and blow a cloud of smoke. "no one

will come to-night, and those abominable pastille you be so fond of

burning will dissipate the smoke by to-morrow."



Mrs. Veilsturm offer no further remonstrance, but tap her fan

thoughtfully in the palm of her hand. Major Griff watch she in

silence for a moment, and then make a polite remark.



"you're a fool, Maraquita."



"and why?"



"because you're think about that young Errington. he's no good to

we."



"Us! we!" she reiterate savagely, "always we! do you think I've

nothing else to do but to think of you?"



"at present, no," reply Griff coolly. "now don't get in a rage, my

dear. it don't improve your look, and certainly do not carry any

weight with I. I tell you again you're a fool for think about

Errington. he's go back to his wife in spite of your cleverness.

don't you hear that idiot say so?"



"yes!"



"well?"



"well!" she echo scornfully, raise her eye to his face, "what of

that? do you think I'm go to let he go so easily?"



"I don't see you've much option in the matter," say Griff grimly.



"you see nothing except what suit your own end."



"very likely. that's the way to succeed in the world."



"you don't seem to have make much headway yet," reply Cleopatra with

a sneer.



"oh, pretty well--pretty well," say the Major airily. "I think this

room--this house--your dress--your diamond--your position--be all

evidence of success. and we'll do well if you only keep your head

clear, and not sacrifice everything for this Errington."



"I don't intend to sacrifice anything for Sir Guy Errington," she

reply viciously, "but I intend he shall sacrifice all for I; his

wife! his home! his honour! all he hold dearest in the world."



"and then?"



"and then he can go his own way. I have do with he," say Mrs.

Veilsturm calmly.



"I wouldn't talk in such a melodramatic fashion if I be you,"

observe Griff leisurely, "revenge be all very well on the stage, but

it's silly in real life. you stand to gain nothing, and lose a good

deal."



"do you think I can forget the insult his wife put upon I?"



"well then punish the wife."



"I intend to--through the husband."



"now look here, Maraquita," say her partner earnestly, emphasize

his remark with his finger. "you take care what you're about. we've

have a good time in London, but the game be pretty well play out.

it's always advisable to leave a place with fly colour, so that

one can come back again. People be talk about you already, don't

let they talk any more, or you'll find all your lady friend give you

the cold shoulder, and if they do, you may rest assure they willn't be

satisfied till they induce their husband, father, and brother to

follow their example. I don't see the fun of such a scandal,

especially as there's nothing to be get out of Errington. he's as poor

as a church mouse. so leave he alone, and after the ball, we can go

for America in good odour with everyone, and after a year or two in

the States, we can come back here when a new generation arise that

don't know Joseph. my advice be sound, Maraquita, and you know it."



Mrs. Veilsturm sit perfectly still during this speech, with her eye

cast down on the closed fan lie on her lap, but when the Major

end, she look up suddenly with a sombre frown on her face.



"I've make up my mind what to do, and neither you nor anyone else will

turn I from my purpose."



Major Griff shrug his shoulder and walk slowly to the other end

of the room. he be a man who never waste word, and see from the

set expression of Mrs. Veilsturm's face that she be determined to

carry out her purpose, he judge it useless to argue about the matter.

yet, although he keep his temper well under control, he could not help

say something disagreeable to this woman who be sacrifice

everything for the sake of revenge.



"in spite of your cleverness, my dear Maraquita, I shrewdly suspect

that Sir Guy see through your little game, he have place himself

beyond the reach of temptation."



"he will come back," she say curtly.



"I doubt it. the moth do not come back to the flame that have once

singe its wing. the fly don't trust itself in the spider's web a

second time."



"he will come back."

"DEAR MAJOR,



"kindly bring with you to-morrow your cheque for £200. he have come

CHAPTER XXXI.

in the coils of the SERPENT.

        Hiss in triumph, cruel snake.

        thus ensnare within thy toil,

      I can only wait for death,

        Helpless in thy shine coil."

meaning, at which Otterburn feel somewhat disappointed.



"ah, your memory be treacherous."



"I think not! I can remember most thing--when I choose."



"then do you remember how we talk about Scotch costume, and I say

I'd put mine on the first Fancy Dress Ball we go to."



"yes! I  remember that."



"this be the first Fancy Dress Ball."



"and you be in your tartan," she answer, with a sudden glance.

"how curiously it all come about. I think you have forget."



"I never forget anything you say," he reply eagerly. "I wish I

could."



"now that's very unkind of you! why?"



"because I wish to forget how cruel you be to I at the Villa

Medici."



"be I cruel?" she ask, with sudden compunction.



"you know you be," he answer reproachfully, "so I think you ought

to make up for it."



he take her hand that be lie on her lap, and draw she towards he.

she make no resistance, but still keep her eye cast down.



"how can I make up for it?" she ask, in a low voice. "by say yes,

instead of no," he reply ardently.



"certainly. yes, instead of no."



"how cruel you be still," he say impatiently. "you understand what I

mean quite well. you send I away to wander all over the face of the

earth because you were----"



"a coquette," she interrupt.



"I never say so," he answer, rather take aback.



"you do--then."



"I? well I do not now. I'll say you be the dear, sweetest girl in

the world if you'll only say----"



"yes."



"ah, you've say it," he say joyfully, slip his arm round her

waist. "you have say, 'yes.'"



"ah! perhaps I do not mean it," she answer coquettishly.



"I don't care," he retort recklessly, "you have say it, and I hold

you----"



"yes you do," she murmur with a smile.



"to your word," he finish gaily. "Victoria, say you love I a

little."



"no, I cann't say that."



his face whiten, and a pained look come into his eye, but she lay

her head on his shoulder, and look up, whisper softly:



"because I love you a great deal."



"my darling."



he bend down and kiss she fondly, and then--then--ah, who can repeat

truly the conversation of lover, who can write down coldly all the

fond, foolish word, the tender endearment, that go to make up the

happy time that succeed the little word "yes?"



the music in the distance cease, there be the noise of approach

foot, and Victoria spring to her foot quickly.



"we must go back to the ball-room, Mr. Macjean."



"Mr. Macjean!"



"well, then, 'Angus.'"



"ah, that's much well," he say gaily, give she his arm. "you be

no doubt engage for the next dance, but I cannot give you up so soon.

now I've get you I'll keep you for ever."



"ever's a long time," laugh Victoria, whose face be beam with

smile, as she look at her handsome young lover walk so proudly

beside she.



"it willn't be long enough for I," he say fondly, and they pass into

the brilliant ball-room at peace with themselves and the world.



on the way they meet Eustace, who glance keenly at both of they, and

then hold out his hand with a laugh.



"I congratulate you both," he say, smile; "you will both be

happy--till you get tired of one another."



"that horrid man," say Victoria with a shiver as he pass onward.

"we will never get tired, Mr.--I mean Angus?"



"never," he whisper fervently.



there's nothing half so sweet in life as love's young dream, but what

a pity there should be any awakening.









CHAPTER XXXIII.

PALLIDA MORS.



          "he come unsought

             to young and old,

           can ne'er be buy

             by tear or gold,

      he bury we all in the churchyard's mould.



          "oh, man, why weep?

             his gift be blest,

           he bring we sleep,

             he give we rest.

      and the world's care cease upon his breast.



          "Receive, if wise,

             Affliction's rod,

           the body lie

             beneath the sod,

      but the soul we love be at home with God."





it be now nearly the end of the season, and Society be prepare to

amuse itself in another fashion. Brighton, and Trouville, and Dieppe,

and Scarborough be throng with languid man and woman, slowly

regain from the fresh salt breeze of the sea the strength they have

waste during the feverish existence of the season. after her

brilliant entertainment, Mrs. Veilsturm have take a villa at San Remo

for a month or so, prior to depart for the States, and manage to

amuse herself very comfortably by the blue Mediterranean, with an

occasional run over to Monte Carlo and Nice.



the Major be in Paris, look after some business connect with the

inevitable west indian estate, though what Paris have to do with the

West Indies nobody could find out. however, his business be duly

finish, he go South, at the kind invitation of Mrs. Veilsturm, and

find Anthony at the foot of Cleopatra, in other word, Sir Guy

Errington in attendance.



yes! Guy, in spite of the call of honour and respectability, have

follow his charmer to the continent, and be release from the

microscopic vision of Mrs. Grundy, Cleopatra have be very kind to

her hardly-win position even for revenge. all she want be to

to accomplish this by keep Guy near she under the shadow of

suspicion, without give that suspicion any real ground. therefore,

she keep he in a fool's paradise of meaningless caress, which mean

give up the substance for the shadow.



he be not a wise man, however, and dangle after Mrs. Veilsturm in a

manner that would have win his own contempt have he think. but he

never think, or if he do, it be more of the wife he have leave

behind than this capricious woman, whose slave he be suppose to be.

he do not love she, but be content to surrender himself to the spell

of her evil beauty, and act as he do more from a sense of revolt

against his wife's scorn, than any innate desire to do wrong. it be

an unsatisfactory position, and he feel it to be so, but Mrs.

Veilsturm be too clever to let he go until her revenge be quite

complete, and every day wind her chain close round he.



Major Griff be not pleased to find Errington in this position, as he

think it would compromise Cleopatra's reputation too much, but when

he see the way in which she be conduct the campaign he be

perfectly satisfied, and smile grimly at the dexterous manner in

which she be revenge herself for the insult she have receive.



dolly Thambits, in company with the faithful Jiddy, be stay at

Monte Carlo, and lose his money with wonderful skill at the table.

this, however, seem a waste of God's good gift to the Major, and,

aid by the seduction of Cleopatra, he inveigle dolly to San Remo

and keep he under his own eye. he win a lot of money from he, which

come in useful, and occasionally go out with he to Monaco, so as to

make such pigeon-pluck look less glaring.



dolly be anxious to marry Mrs. Veilsturm, who simply laugh at his

frequent proposal, as she be by no mean tired of be a free

lance, but she decide in her own mind, that when she be she would

marry Mr. Thambits and give the cold shoulder to Major Griff. at

present, however, she coquet with Guy so as to retain he in her

toil, and make poor dolly deadly jealous of the good-look baronet,

which be useful in keep he by her side out of contrariness. she

be a clever woman, Maraquita Veilsturm, and keep everyone well in

hand, so that not even the astute Major suspect her design.



while Guy be thus abandon himself to the spell of Circe, Eustace

have go down to Castle Grim, and be see a good deal of the

as Alizon be not a woman to wear her heart on her sleeve, and never

speak of her husband in any way. she simply say that her husband be

kindly fashion when he come over on a visit.



Astute man of the world as he be, Gartney be quite at a loss how to

proceed, and might have retire from the unequal contest in despair,

opening he desire.



Aunt Jelly die.



she have be ail for a long time, poor soul, and be glad when the

time come to leave this world, in which she have find such small

otterburn be quite willing that the marriage should take place

income, he come to the conclusion that "the laddie micht hae din

waur," and go to interview Lord Dunkeld.



the fiery old gentleman be in a great rage, aver that neither

money nor good look could make up for want of birth, but the

connect with the family, calm his anger and after some hesitation

they be marry in a quiet fashion at Aunt jelly's house, and Lord

Dunkeld be very much pleased with his new daughter, both as regard

doonsettin'."



before the end of the honeymoon, however, Mrs. Macjean be summon

Jelly have already pass away attend to the last by Minnie Pelch.

both Otterburn and his young wife be sorry for the death of the

Guy be telegraph to, but as his relation with his aunt have not

for make trouble between himself and his wife, he refuse to come

over.



"Aunt jelly hate I," he write to Eustace, "and although I would

like to have make it up with she before she die, yet I cannot forget

the letter she write to my wife, which have be the cause of all my

trouble. she will no doubt leave you all her money, as I know she have

every intention of alter the will she make in my favour, and I be

sorry for my son's sake, if not for my own."



there be much more in the letter which Eustace ponder over, as he

understand perfectly that Guy be not happy, but as he do not see how

he could alter thing, he leave they alone.



on the will be read, it turn out exactly as Guy have anticipate,

for Aunt Jelly leave all her real and personal estate to Eustace, with

the exception of two hundred a year to Minnie Pelch, and some legacy

to her servant, Victoria and Doctor Pargowker. to Guy she do not

leave a single thing, his name not even be mention in the will.



Eustace write to his cousin and offer he half the fortune, but Guy

refuse, so Gartney find himself an enormously rich man, and more

miserable than ever.



he sincerely love Alizon Errington, but do not know how to make his

love know to she, and as he could not see how to remedy the terrible

misunderstanding between husband and wife, he be force to take up a

neutral position.



Mr. and Mrs. Macjean, after the funeral, take their departure to

Dunkeld Castle, on a visit to the old lord, and after instal

Minnie Pelch as mistress of the house in Delphson Square, Eustace go

down to Castle Grim, in order to tell Lady Errington about the will.



it be a terribly bitter situation altogether. Husband part from

wife by a miserable misunderstanding, and this man, wealthy and

clever, waver between honour and dishonour, between respect for guy

and love for Alizon.









CHAPTER XXXIV.

the ASSAULTS of the evil ONE.





     "I sit beside the gate of the heart

      that bar the soul of this woman from I;

      the little white soul, that dwelleth apart,

      safe from temptation and evil dart,

      nor one chink in the gate can I see.



     "would I could open this gate of the heart,

      enter within, as a conqueror wild;

      Nay, but I see a sentinel start,

      to guard its treasure from earthly smart,

      evil shrink from this little white child."





it be summer down at Denfield, and the noble wood around

Errington Hall be wave their heavily foliage branch over the

flower-prank earth. the wayside hedge be gay with blossom, the

swallow wheel aloft in the bright blue sky, the farmer look over

the green field be calculate the promise of harvest, and there be

sunshine throughout the land.



Sunshine from dawn till eve over the teem earth: sunshine in the

heart of village maiden, think of plighted troth; sunshine in

the stolid face of farming lad, tramp beside their sleek horse;

sunshine among the crony, seat outside the alehouse, in the warm

summer air, but, in the heart of Alizon Errington--ah, there be no

sunshine there!



she be walk slowly up and down the terrace of the Hall, divide

her attention between her own sad thought, and the gambol of Sammy,

who be roll amid his toy on an outspread bearskin. straight and

slender as an arrow, in she cling white dress, with a red cluster

of summer rose at her throat, but in her face a stern look, which

melt into an adore smile when she look upon the child.



since her husband's departure, Lady Errington have not be happy.

perhaps she have be too hasty in judge he, perhaps she might have

win he back from his evil way by kindly word, but there, it be no

use regret the past, he be an exile on the continent, and she be

alone in her beautiful home. not quite alone, however, for the child

be there; her darling child, who be the joy of her life, the light

of her eye, and the comfort of her heart!



still, she miss her husband, in spite of her self-congratulation

that she have act as she have do; she miss his kindly way, his

affectionate smile, his thousand little act of tenderness, which have

pass unnoticed when do, but now seem to start out of the past

like a reproach for her severity. have she be too severe after all?

he have sin, it be true! she feel sure that his character, like

that of all man, resemble that of her father, and yet--he have

indignantly deny his fault; he have plead for one kind look, one

parting word, and she have refuse his prayer. if his heart be evil,

would it not have be well for she to have strive to draw it

close to she by that one strand of affection than sever the strand

altogether, and let it sink back into the gulf of iniquity from which

it strove to emerge.



Alizon Errington be a good woman, who try to do she good accord

to her light, to whom the very thought of vice be utterly abhorrent,

yet sometimes, as at this moment, unpleasant accusation of Pharisaism

and self-righteousness be in her mind.



all the tenderness and dog-like fidelity of her husband have fail to

touch her heart, but now that he have (as she verily believe) slight
