she feel that he be more to she than anyone else in the world, save

his child. if his heart, if his instinct, be as evil as she

believe, all the more credit to he for the way in which he strove to

act honestly, but if on the other hand she have misjudge he and

drive a good man into evil by cruel word and harsh look, then

indeed she be to blame. either way she look at thing now it seem

to she that she be in the wrong, and yet she could not, would not,

acknowledge that she have not act justly.



"if he have only wait for a time," she tell herself restlessly. "if

he he have only show by his action that he desire to do right, I

would have believe he in time. but to go back to that vile woman

after what I say--no!--he be like all the rest--he make evil his

God, and would break my heart, and ruin his child's future, soon

than deny himself the gratification of his brutal instinct."



strong word certainly, but then she feel strongly. she be not a

broad-minded woman, for the horror with which her father have inspire

she, have narrow down her view of life to Puritanical exactness. she

demand from the world purity--absolute purity--which be an

impossibility. what man could come to a woman and say, "I be as pure

in my life as you be"? not one. why then do she demand it from her

husband? but this be quite another view of the question. her thought

have go from one thing to another, until they have become involved and

complex.



with a weary sigh she shake her head, as though to drive away all

those idea, and sit down in a low chair, in order to play with the

boy.



"Sammy! Sammy! you musn't put pitty thing in mouse mouse."



"Mum! mum!" from Sammy, who be make a bold attempt to swallow his

coral necklace. find this a failure he crawl quickly across the

bearskin, draw himself up to his mother's knee, and stand grin,

in a self-congratulatory manner, on his unsteady little leg.



"come, then," say Alizon, hold out her arm.



Frantic attempt on the part of Sammy to crawl up on her lap, end

with a fall, and then a quick catch up into the desire place under

a shower of kiss.



they make a pretty picture, mother and son; the pale, sad-look

woman, with the fresh, rosy boy, and Eustace pause a moment,

at the end of the terrace, to admire it. the boy have catch the

the fair ringlet came fall over her breast in a golden shower.



"oh, naughty Sammy," she say, gaily toss he in the air with her

two hand. "look at poor mother's hair--bad child!"



Sammy, however, appear to have a different opinion, and chuckle

indistinctly to himself, until he catch sight of Eustace, of whom he

be very fond, and stretch out his arm with a merry crow.



"Mr. Gartney,' say Lady Errington, flush a rosy red at the

"Young Turk!" say Eustace, take the boy with a smile, while Alizon

"quite well, thank you," she reply quietly, as he sit down near she,

"so I be. come down last night," answer Gartney, while the baby

make futile grab at his watch chain. "well, my prince, and how be

"he's never ill," say the young mother, with great pride. "I never

see such a healthy child. not an illness since his birth."



"Lucky Sammy! if his future life be only as pleasant as the first year

of it, what a delightful time he will have."



Lady Errington's face have grow very grave during this speech, as she

he have go up to town.



"you go to the funeral?" she ask, the colour flush in her face.



"yes!" he reply, smooth the child's fair curl with gentle hand.

"I go to the funeral. poor Aunt Jelly! I don't think she be sorry

to die."



Alizon make no reply, but sit perfectly still, look steadily at he

"to you?"



"believe I, I neither expect nor desire it," he cry hastily. "I

"my poor child!"



that be all she say--not a thought, not a word of pity for her

"I assure you," begin Eustace, feel like a robber, "that I----"

her voice end in a tragic wail, and it be with difficulty that she

"Lady Errington," say eustace earnestly after a pause, "believe I, I

"he could do no less," she answer dully. "what right have we to rob

"it's not robbery," he reply vehemently. "I have more money than I

want. whenever Guy like to accept, he shall have half the property."



without answer his question, she look down at the baby play at

"on the continent--at San Remo."



"With!--with that woman?"



"I!--I don't know," reply Eustace in a low voice, turn his face

away.



"Mr. Gartney?"



"yes, Lady Errington."



"look I in the face."



he do so unwillingly, and find her eye fasten on his with a

determined expression.



"be my husband with that woman?"



"no! I don't think so, but I certainly hear she be at San Remo," he

answer evasively.



"ah!" she draw a long breath, and a look of anger sweep across her

"you must not be too hard on guy," say Eustace, very feebly it must

be confess.



"hard on guy," she repeat scornfully. "hard on a man who leave his

wife and child for a vile woman like that. you, of course, take his

"why should I?" demand Eustace hotly, "because I be his cousin?"



"no, because you be a man. man always stand up for one another. it's

a kind of _esprit du corps_ with they I suppose. it be no wrong to

betray a woman in their eye."



"I don't know why you expect I to stand up for my sex, I'm sure,"

be quite incompetent to undertake the Herculean task of defend

"I've no doubt," reply Lady Errington bitterly. "you man be all the

same."



"I sincerely hope not," retort Eustace imperturbably. "I've no

desire to resemble certain fool of my acquaintance. my character be

no well nor no bad than my fellow-creature', and have some good

woman like yourself take charge of my life I might have improve."



"you ought to get marry."



"do you think so--from your own experience?"



she flush crimson, and in order to hide her confusion stoop down

to pick up the child.



"Marriages be make in heaven," she say, try to pass the thing off

"I understand there's a tradition to that effect," respond Eustace,

indolently. "if that be the case, it be a pity Heaven give a woman to

one man who don't care about she, instead of bestow she on

another who cannot be happy without she."



"be that your case?"



"yes."



there be a pause, during which she look at he curiously. he meet

her gaze calmly, and not an idea of his meaning cross her mind.



"so you love a married woman?"



"I do, and therefore no doubt be an object of horror in your eye?"



the child have fall asleep on her breast, and rise to her foot she

"I have no right to judge you," she say evasively, "but you can

hardly expect I--a wife and a mother--to say that I approve of such a

Eustace wince at the scorn of the last word.



"no, I cannot," he answer slowly, "but let I put the case before

you in another way. suppose a woman be married to a man who care

absolutely nothing about she, neglect she in every way, insult she

"oh!" she cry, shrink as if he have strike she a blow.



"I be put a supposititious case, remember," he say hastily.

"well, this woman have a lover who adore she, but who have never

venture to express this passion, which the world call dishonourable.

the woman return that passion and have only to say one word to the

lover in order to be release from the curse of a loveless marriage, a

neglectful husband, an unhappy home. what should that woman do in such

a case?"



"remain true to her marriage vow," she say grandly.



"but if the husband be not true."



"be she to sink to the level of the husband? no, Mr. Gartney. let the

wife shame the husband by her fidelity to the vow which he have

break."



"and the lover?"



"be not a true lover, or he would not wish to drag the woman he

profess to love through the mud of the world."



"so you would condemn two life to perpetual misery for the sake of

one man, who do not appreciate the sacrifice?"



"not for the sake of the one man, but for the sake of virtue, of

honour, of uprightness."



Eustace be silent under the cold purity of her look. this woman be

no dreamer as he have think, but have a soul like that Roman Lucrece,

who prefer death to dishonour.



"your creed be severe," he say at last, with a frown on his strongly

marked feature.



"my creed be right," she reply simply.



"yes! accord to the world."



"no! accord to God."



as a rule, Gartney be not to be daunt by any woman, but there be

something about Alizon Errington that make he afraid to talk in his

usual cynical vein. stand a short distance away, with the child in

her arm and the golden glory of the sunshine behind she, this young

mother look like the realisation of the Madonna. so pure, so calm,

so lovely, with the look of motherhood in her eye that he

involuntarily turn away his head, as though he be not worthy to

profane such purity even by a glance.



"you talk above my head," he say at length, rise to his foot,

"it be the language of an ideal world, not to be realize in

this matter-of-fact century. but if you will forgive I, Lady

Errington----"



"why not call I Alizon?" she say cordially. "we be cousin, you

know, and title be so formal--Eustace."



"it's very kind of you to grant I such permission," reply Gartney

frankly, take the hand she hold out to he. "Goodbye--Alizon."



"not goodbye, but _au revoir_."



"may I come over again?" he ask eagerly.



"of course. I be always glad to see you, besides Sammy love his kind

friend who play with he."



"and you?"



their eye meet, a wave of crimson pass over her face, and with an

air of displeasure, she turn away coldly, without answer his

question.



"Goodbye, Mr. Gartney."



see that his freedom have offend she, he be too wise to make any

further remark, but bow slightly walk slowly away.



at the end of the terrace he look back, and see she be bend over

the sleep child, croon some cradle-song to soothe his slumber.

CHAPTER XXXV.

for MY child'S sake.



     "I'll look my dear boy in the face

        in after year,

      without the shadow of disgrace

        or shameful tear.



     "oh, folly do I sin with you,

        and cause he pain,

      if hand be clean, and heart be true,

        his be the gain.

        come dull or fair,

it be very dull down at Castle Grim, for even the bright sunshine of

I.



     "I love a star that shine above

        when day be blend with the night,

      Alas, what pain this foolish love,

        such worship bring but cold delight.

      I cannot scale the twilight sky,

        my love to tell in accent sweet;

      it come not down altho' I sigh,

        and so my star I ne'er can meet.



II.

        your love I dare not hope to gain;

      yet still you shine each night afar,

        to mock my anguish and my pain

      and yet thou art so sweet, so pure,

the death of the FIRST-bear.

after the departure of Eustace, life go on in the same old fashion

at the Hall. Alizon pass her day and night with Sammy, receive

the few visitor that call, and be as happy as she could be under

his instinct resemble those of the rest of his sex."

always come between they; so far as she could see, nothing remain to

make her life happy but the child.



Sammy be the idol of her heart. she forget everything when she have

he in her arm, and she feel that the whole world might go to ruin as

bosom of his mother. in her daily life she adapt all thing to suit

the living of her child, and never know a happy moment when she be

away from his side.



the first thing in the morning the child be bring down to her

with happy eye, babble fond nonsense suit to his baby

he play at her foot for hour, and she sit beside he in a low chair

he, she be never tired of talk about his clever way, his

infantile wisdom, his love disposition, and when he be lay to rest

at night, she hang over he like an enamoured lover breathing

blessing on his unconscious head.



the world will doubtless laugh at such tender devotion, at such

intense absorption in an unformed infant, but no one but a woman, no

one but a mother, can understand the wondrous power of maternal love

that dominate every other feeling in the feminine heart. all the

passion of lover, the ecstacie of poet, the blind adoration of man

for those they love, pale before this strong of all feeling

implant in the human breast. perhaps some will say that

self-preservation be strong, but this be not so, as a mother in an

extreme case will sacrifice her life for that of her child, thereby

prove the superiority of the maternal feeling.



in this worship of the child she forget earth, she forget heaven, she

forget God.



and God punish she.



Sammy be cut his tooth, and be feverish and fretful for some

day, but although every care be lavish upon he neither Alizon nor

Mrs. Tasker deem the illness to be anything bad than a slight

affright the agonise mother, do what she could under the

Dead!



when the doctor tell she, she could not believe it, and throw

sign of life. alas it be all in vain, and after an hour of agonising

dread she be oblige to accept the inevitable.

with ashen lip, and restless hand pluck at her dress.



"it be the judgment of God, because I love his creature well than

himself."



there be no grief so terrible as that silent, self-concentrate agony

Mrs. Tasker, wise old woman that she be, know that unless something

and Guy?

Eustace, as he stand on the deck of a Channel steamer in the dark

the fact be, that for the first time in his life Gartney be

CHAPTER XXXVII.

the truth about MRS. VEILSTURM.



     "we all have skeleton, everyone,

      we hide away from the cheerful sun,

CHAPTER XXXVIII.

the last temptation.



     "death ever rend asunder marriage bond,

      so should he die, this husband undesired,

      she would be free to woo and wed again

      and I might haply gain her hand, her heart.

      yet there be folly in this argument,

      for such a course would breed but sterile love,

      see the first link in the chain of circumstance

      be ominous indeed--a dead man's grave."





having thus route the enemy, Eustace return to his hotel very well

of good in remove the obstacle to the reconciliation of husband

and wife. for his own part, he feel considerable astonishment at the

least anticipate by the tempt.



of course, he tell Dr. Storge about his success in the delicate matter

doctor be not infallible, however, and the result of this attempt to

which state of the case considerably dismay both Storge and Gartney.

that she have never love he, and that Cleopatra be the only woman

every day, when tender message arrive for he with present of fruit

seem as though nothing could avert the threaten attack of brain

Veilsturm have depart from San Remo and take up her abode at Nice,

she find the italian watering-place dull and prefer the lively

this sudden exodus, and his excited brain suggest a thousand reason

afraid of excite he. she have be call to England on business!

what could be the reason of this sudden change from attention to

neglect, from warmth to coldness? and day and night, and night and

day, the weary brain puzzle over these perplexing question,

suggest and discard a thousand answer with every tick of the

clock.



Eustace do his good to allay his cousin's excitement without tell

"thing cann't go on like this much long," he say decisively, "if my

cousin be ill when I arrive, he seem to I to be much bad now."



"it's a very difficult case," remark Storge musingly. "so difficult,

that I hardly know what step to take. I've make he keep to his room,

see no one, give he sedative, and yet he be no well. in fact, I

think we're only at the beginning of the trouble."



"well, I've get that woman out of the way," say Eustace bluntly, "so

"I'm not so sure of that," reply the doctor, bite his nail, a

to take in order to cure my patient."



"it be perplexing," sigh Eustace, drum with his finger on the

angel--faugh! to what length will love carry a man."



"but you say he be not in love with she."



"neither be he! this be one of those rare case which be veritable

"'thou cans't not minister to a mind disease," quote Storge,

such a turmoil. you cure his mind, Gartney, and I'll cure his body."



"it's all very well say that," reply Eustace pettishly. "you give

I the hard task."



"suppose you send for his wife?"



"she willn't come."



"but surely when she knows----"



"I tell you she willn't come," repeat Eustace sternly, "she think he

Storge run his hand through his hair in a most perplexed fashion, but

"I tell you what," say Gartney, after a pause, during which he have

"and then," finish the doctor caustically, "you'll fix he up nicely

for a very bad attack of brain fever."



"that be one presumption!"



"the only one."



"I don't agree with you! I'll undeceive he about Mrs. Veilsturm, and

then he'll see the snare he have escape."



"oh, and do you think that will quiet he?" ask Dr. Storge

sarcastically.



"I think it will turn his thought back to his wife. if so, I'll write

to she to come over----"



"what about the forgiveness?"



"I'll tell she it's a case of life and death. that will surely soften

she."



"you whirl about like a weather-cock, my friend," say Storge grimly,

then you say she might soften--which view be the right one?"



"Both."



"impossible!"



"nothing be impossible with regard to a woman. but what do you say to

my plan?"



"I don't know what to say."



"then I'll try it," say Eustace determinedly.



"I don't approve of it," remark Storge in desperation, "still, as

it's a case of brain fever if thing go on like this, the chance of

accelerate the disease don't make much difference, so you'd well

begin your disillusionising at once."



"very well," reply Gartney with a sigh of relief, and this close

the conversation.



it be a disagreeable task to undertake, but not more so than that

connect with Mrs. Veilsturm, and Eustace make up his mind to speak

"the soon thing be bring to a crisis the well," he think, as

he go up to his cousin's room. "as they stand now, it's quite

impossible to move either way."



Guy be lie with his arm outside the counterpane, when Mr. Gartney

"what?"

"yes! yes! you can go, Albert. Mr. Gartney will stay with I."



the well-train valet bow his head in answer, arrange a few thing

"your friend!" repeat Eustace scornfully. "yes, the same kind of

"Eustace!"

"my wife?"



"yes! I have a confession to make to you. I love your wife."



Guy look at his cousin vacantly, and as if he do not understand.

then the child die--the child whom she worship--the child who be

solitude--rob of her nearest and dear--wait for the sound of

her husband's voice, for the clasp of his arm, for the touch of his

lip."



in his fervour, he have slip from his chair, and be now kneel

beside the bed, hold his cousin's hot hand in his own. the sick man

disengage hand with a bitter cry.



"no! no! it be too late, it be too late."



"it be not too late," say Eustace, earnestly. "I have tell you the

but the sick man roll his head from side to side on the pillow with

"Liar! you cannot prove it!"



"I can, and by her own handwriting."



Guy snatch the letter Eustace hold out to he, tear open the

"you see now, my dear Guy, what this woman really be," say Gartney

the baronet uncover his face, and look at Eustace in a vacant

"no! no! it's impossible," cry Eustace in alarm, hold he back;

"be reasonable, Guy, be reasonable. stay where you be, Guy!"

Albert enter, and, see the state of affair, shout for aid, and

wife."



"I suppose I must."



"it's a case of life and death," say Storge, and walk out of the

in two minute eustace be on his way to the telegraph office. as he

walk rapidly down the street, the temptation come, the terrible

temptation that whisper to he not to send for Alizon.



"if you do not," whisper the devil on his left, "Guy will die, and

you will be able to gain she for your wife."



"no," say the good angel on his right. "she can never love you, you

could buy nothing, not even happiness, at the price of your cousin's

death."



so Eustace walk along with these two angel, the bad and the good,

whispering in his ear, now incline to one, now to the other,

fight desperately against the temptation of the devil, and again

yield to the insidious whisper of future joy to be win by a simple

act of neglect. in that short walk a whole life-time of agony pass,

but no one look at this stalwart, calm-faced man stride along the

Street, could have guess the hell that rage within.



the power of good and evil fight desperately for the possession of

this weak, wavering soul, that be in such sore strait, but in the

end the good angel prevail, and Eustace sit down to write his

telegram.



he write one to Alizon, as strongly word as he be able, and a

second to Otterburn, tell he he must bring Lady Errington over at

once. in both he write the word, "it be a case of life and death,"

those word that have be ring in his ear ever since the doctor

have say they.



then, as he hand the telegram to the clerk, the temptation again

assail he. it be not too late, let he withdraw the message, tear

they up, and there would be a chance of his win the woman he love

instead of go into voluntarily exile. but at the price of a man's

life? no! that be too big a price to pay, and yet--he put down the

money demand by the clerk and walk out of the post office.



outside in the sunshine he stand with drop of sweat on his forehead,

and the soul that have be save from the commission of a great crime,

put up a prayer of thank to God that this last temptation have pass,

and that the power of evil have not prevail in the hour of weakness.









CHAPTER XXXIX.

"and KISSED again with TEARS."



     "you have return with your face so fair,

      your sweet blue eye and your golden hair,

      again to cherish--again to share

      this life of mine with its joy and care.



     "alas, my dearest, the day be long,

      when memory come in a countless throng,

      to sing to my heart such a haunt song,

      of thing once right that have change to wrong.



     "you have return just to heal the smart

      that Sorrow make with her cruel dart,

      never again will we sigh and part.

      you once more be my leal sweetheart."





the Hon. Angus Macjean's experience of early married life could

hardly be call pleasant, see the demand make upon himself and

his bride by their mutual friend. shortly after their marriage, Aunt

Jelly have die, thereby cause they to return to London before the

end of the honeymoon, then, during their visit to Lord Dunkeld, Mrs.

Macjean have be summon south in order to console Lady Errington for

the loss of her child, and now as Eustace have telegraph Alizon to

come over to her sick husband at San Remo, it be necessary that

otterburn should escort she, for it be impossible, in her present

state of grief, that she could travel alone. the young couple,

therefore, do not get so much of each other's company as they

desire, and it say a great deal for the good nature of both, that

they be so ready to comfort the mourner, at the sacrifice of their

own desire, and the upsetting of all their plan.



life at Errington Hall be very dreary after the death of the heir, as

Victoria be constantly with the unhappy mother and Otterburn be leave

to wander about with nothing but his own thought, which be not

particularly cheerful in the present aspect of affair. then come the

funeral, which Macjean have to look after entirely by himself, as

Eustace and Errington be both absent. the young man have receive a

letter from Gartney, state that Guy be too ill to travel, and

Victoria have show it to Alizon, but, wrap up in the selfishness of

grief for her great loss, she have make hardly any remark about this

new blow.



then come the peremptory telegram summon the wife to the bedside

of her sick husband, and Otterburn, through his wife, delicately

offer to accompany Lady Errington to San Remo as soon as she be

ready to start.



Alizon be a long time make up her mind about go, as she

consider that her husband have grossly insult she by his

openly-display passion for Mrs. Veilsturm. still, on calm

reflection, she see that she be to a great extent blameable for his

folly, and as the death of Sammy have considerably soften her heart

towards his wrong-doing, she determine to fulfil her duty as a wife

and go across to the Riviera at once. the child's death have leave a

blank in her heart, and she feel that she must have someone to love

and console she, or she would go mad in the loneliness of her grief;

so with these thought in her heart she send a telegram to Eustace,

announce her departure, and prepare for the journey.



she accept Otterburn's escort as far as San Remo, but promise that

as soon as she be establish by Guy's sick bed, Angus should return

to his wife, who be to be leave behind at Errington Hall. Angus agree

to this, and in company with the young man and her maid, she leave

Victoria Station _en route_ for the italian Riviera.



the whole journey seem to she like a dream; the bright english

landscape, which she know so well; the breezy passage across the

Channel, with the tossing wave and blue sky; Calais, with its

bustling crowd of native and tourist; the long journey through the

pleasant Norman country, and then Paris, gay and glittering, where

they stay all night. next morning again in the train rushing

southward, past quaint, mediæval town, with their high-peaked house,

over slow-flow river, through ancient forest already bear the

touch of Autumn's finger--still onward, onward, till they reach

marseille, sit by the blue water of the Mediterranean.

afterwards they continue their journey through smile Provence,

along the sunny Riviera--Cannes, Mentone, Nice, all pass in their

turn; a glimpse of Monte Carlo, where the Goddess of Play sit

enthrone on high--palm-crown Bordighera--deserted Ospedaletti, with

its lonely Casino--and at last San Remo, amid her grey olive-grove,

at the foot of the blue hill.



Eustace be wait for they at the railway-station, look very

grave, and bow silently to Lady Errington, as she step out of the

carriage.



"be he well?" she ask, look haggardly at he, a tall slender

figure in her sweeping black robe.



"I'm afraid not--still we hope for the good."



she make no reply, so after greet Otterburn, Eustace conduct they

to a carriage, and they drive to the Hotel de la Mer. Alizon lose no

try to prepare her mind, so that the shock of see he should not

be too much, but she disregard all his entreaty, and go up to

the darken apartment where her husband be lie. one question only

she ask Gartney before she enter:



"be that woman here?"



"do you think I would have send for you have she be?" he reply,

deeply hurt. "no I she have leave San Remo, and will trouble you no

more."



"your doing?"



"yes."



she give he her slender, black-gloved hand for a moment, and then

pass to her husband's bedside, where her place be henceforth to be.



the next morning otterburn, having discharge his duty, return to

his wife, and Lady Errington be leave alone with Eustace to nurse the

man whom she never think to meet with kindly feeling again.



Guy be terribly ill for a long time, but as out of evil good

sometimes come, there be no doubt that this illness be beneficial,

inasmuch as it show Alizon the true state of her husband's heart. in

those long, dreary hour, as she sit beside the bed listen to his

health--would have convince she of the truth, and there would always

have lurk in her heart a terrible suspicion that she have be sin

against, which would have embitter her whole life. she would have

perhaps forgive her husband, but she nevertheless would have believe

reproach to the purity of her soul. but these wild muttering, these

soul, and how needful to its well-being be love and tenderness.



again, since the death of her child a terrible sense of utter

this woman, who have only respect and admire he when he be well

care to know be that this obstacle that have stand between herself

and her husband have be remove, and that the true feeling of that

husband have be reveal to she by the hand of God.



during all this time Eustace act the part of a brother, and never by

word or dee betray the true state of his feeling. Heaven alone

know how he suffer in maintain a cold, patient demeanour towards

the woman he love, and his life, from the time of her arrival till

owe to the unintermitte care of Dr. Storge, the careful nursing of

moment of joy she have know since the death of her child come to

faintly by her name.



"Alizon."



"yes, dear!--your wife."



his wife!--was this his cold, stately wife who kneel so fondly beside

he? be those eye--shine with love, wet with tear--the cold blue

eye that have so often freeze all demonstration of affection? be

that face, full of joyful relief and emotion, the marble countenance

No!--it could not be Alizon--it be some deceptive vision of the

brain, paint what might have be and yet---- she see his state of

"it be I--your wife!--wife not in name only, but in love and trust."



a smile of joy flit across his worn face, and he strove to put out

one weak hand.



"Forgive," he say faintly, "forgive."



"it be I who should ask forgiveness," she reply in a broken voice;

"I be harsh and cold, my dearest, and I do ask your forgiveness. hush

do not say a word--you be very weak, and must not talk. let I nurse

you back to health again, and then I will strive to be a well wife

to you than I have hitherto be."



he say nothing, but lie on his pillow, with eye shine with love,

a contented smile on his lip, and fall asleep, still hold his

wife's hand in his own.



after this he mend quickly, for with the return of Alizon's

affection the desire of life have come back, and each day he grow

strong because the vex brain be now at rest, and the love of his

wife be a well medicine than any drug of the doctor.



"you see," say Storge to Eustace on leave the chamber one day when

medicine, but his wife's affection. ah, Shakespeare be a wise man

when he say, 'thou canst not minister unto a mind disease.' love be

the only cure there."



"lucky mind to have such a cure," reply Gartney with a sigh; "some

mind have to bear their disease till the end of life with no chance

of be mend."



Storge say nothing, but he look at he curiously, for he half

guess the real state of the case, and sincerely pity Eustace for

his unhappy passion.



"poor fellow," he think as he depart, "he have wealth, health, fame

and popularity, yet he would give all these for what he will never

obtain--the heart of that woman."



Guy's complete recovery be now only a question of a few week, so

"I've just get a letter from my friend," he say cheerfully, "and he

want I to come back to England at once."



"what for?" ask Guy quickly.



"oh, our african expedition, you know," reply Eustace, smooth out

the letter. "I put it off because of your illness, but now you be on

the way to recovery I can leave you with safety in the hand of

"I never see such a fellow," say Guy, fretfully. "why on earth cann't

you stay at home, instead of scamper all round the world?"



Eustace laugh, yet his mirth be rather force.



"I'm afraid I've get a strain of gipsy blood in I somewhere," he

say, jokingly, "and I cann't rest; besides, I really and truly prefer

savage to civilized idiot of the London type. they're every bit as

decent, and much more amusing."



all this time, Lady Errington have remain silent in deep thought, but

at the conclusion of Gartney's speech, she look up with a grave

face.



"when do you start?" she ask quietly.



"to-morrow morning."



"so soon?" she say, with a start.



"hang it, Eustace, you might have give we long notice," remark

Guy, in a displeased tone of voice.



"_Cui bono?_" say Gartney, listlessly. "long leave-taking be a

mistake, I think--the opposite of 'link sweetness long draw out.' I

always like to come and go quickly, so I'll say goodbye to-night, and

be off the first thing in the morning."



Neither Guy nor his wife make any further remark, as they both feel

dimly that it would be happy for Eustace to go away as soon as

possible. it be not ingratitude, it be not a desire to lose his

company, but what he have say to the wife, and what he have say to the

husband, recur to both their memory, and they silently acquiesce

in his decision.



"before I go," say Eustace, after a pause, "there be one thing I wish

to say. can I speak to you both without offence?"



"certainly," reply Guy, wonder what be come. "we both owe you

more than we can ever repay."



"you can repay it easily," say Gartney, quickly, "by accept the

proposition I be about to make."



"let we hear what it be first," observed Alizon, look up for a

moment with a faint smile on her lip.



"it will not take long to explain," answer Gartney, in a

matter-of-fact tone. "you know I be rich enough to indulge all my

whim and fancy, so this new access of wealth from Aunt Jelly, be

absolutely useless to I. it ought to have be leave to Guy, and have I

speak to Aunt Jelly before she die, no doubt I would have make she

see this. as it be, however, it have be leave to I, and I do not want

it. Guy, however, do so. I wish to make he a free gift of all the

property before leave for Africa."



"no," say Guy resolutely, "I will not take a penny."



"why not?"



"because it be leave to you. I do not want to rob you."



"it's not a question of robbery," say Eustace, coolly, "if the money

be of any use to I, I'd keep it. but it be not. I do not even know

that I would touch it, so it's far well to be employ by you than

lie idle in my bank. what do you say, Alizon?"



she flush painfully.



"what can I say?"



"that you will persuade this obstinate husband of yours to take the

money."



"but suppose he willn't accept?"



"which be his firm intention," say Guy, quickly.



"in that case," remark Eustace grimly, "I shall simply hand it over

to the most convenient charity, say 'the Society for the suppression

of critic,' or 'the Fund for Converted Publishers'--but keep it, I

will not."



"you're talk nonsense," cry Guy, impatiently. "the sober truth, I

assure you."



there be silence for a few moment, and at last the silence be

break by Guy.



"if I think you be in earnest----" he begin slowly.



"dead earnest," say Eustace.



"then I suppose it will be good to accept your quixotic offer."



"I'm glad you look at it in such a sensible light," retort Gartney,

with an air of great relief. "you agree with Guy, Alizon?"



she raise her eye slowly to his face, and look steadily at he

before make her reply.



"yes, I agree with Guy," she answer frankly.



"then it's settle," say Eustace with a huge sigh. "I cann't tell you

how glad I be to escape be bury under this weight of wealth, like

Tarpeia under the shield of the Sabines. an old illustration, be it

not, but remarkably apt. you will be able to clear the mortgage off

the Hall, Guy, and live there in a manner befit the place. I will

see my lawyer as soon as I return to England, so you will have no

further trouble over the matter."



"and what about yourself?" ask Alizon, impulsively.



"Myself?" he echo, rise slowly from his chair. "oh, I be go

away to foreign part. the land of Khem--the blameless Ethiopians--the

secret source of the Nile, and all that kind of thing."



"but when you come back?" say Errington, raise himself on his

elbow.



"when I come back," say Eustace sadly, a presentiment of come doom

heavy on his soul, "then I'll see you both happy and honour. perhaps

you'll find a domestic seat for I by the domestic hearth, and I'll

tell story of mysterious land to future generation of Erringtons."



again silence, a painful, oppressive silence, which seem to last an

eternity.



"Goodbye, dear old fellow," say Eustace at last, with a mighty

effort.



Guy clasp his hand without a word, his heart be too full to

speak.



"and you also, Alizon."



she give he her hand also, and there they stand, husband and wife,

with their hand clasp in those of the man whom they both know have

fight a good fight--and conquer.



"Goodbye, Eustace," whisper the woman at last, with a look of

infinite gratitude and pity in her deep eye. "may God keep

you--brother."



and under the spell of that gentle benediction, he pass away from

their sight for ever.









CHAPTER XL.

a letter from HOME.



     "I think that our old life be over and do with,

        and ever apart we would wander alone,

      that Clotho have break the distaff she spin with,

        weave the weird web that make my life one with your own.



     "Yea, but this letter unbidden appeareth,

        a sorrowful ghost of the sweetness of yore.

      bring dear thought which the lonely heart cheereth,

      recall the word which the heavy soul heareth

        no more.



     "ah, but love's blossom can ne'er bloom again, love,

        Withered and brown it lie dead in my heart,

      there let it fade and broken remain, love,

      we must live ever while year wax and wane, love,

        apart."





at the entrance to a tent a man sit silent, watch the set sun.

a wild scene, truly, far beyond the bound of civilization, where the

foot of the white man have never trodden before, where the savage

tribe have live since the first of Time in primeval simplicity, where

nature, with lavish hand, spread her uncultured luxuriance in forest,

in mountain, and in plain, under a burning, tropical sky. it be a

scene far in the interior of Africa, that mysterious continent, which

have yet to yield up her secret to the dogged curiosity of european

race.



the man be read a letter, a letter that have come through swamp,

through jungle, over mountain, across plain, by the hand of savage

carrier, the last letter he would receive before plunge still

deep into the unknown land beyond, the last link that bind he to

civilization--a letter from home.



inside the tent, another man be also read letter, from friend

and club companion, which give he all the late gossip of that

London, now so far away, but he read they lightly, and toss they

aside with a careless hand. the man outside, however, have only one

letter, and, as he read it, his eye grow moist, blind he so much

that he could not see the writing, and look up, gaze at the scene

before he through a blurred mist of tear.



undulate grass plain, a wide river wind through the country like

a silver serpent, clump of tropical tree, and a distant vision of

fantastic peak, all flush with splendid colour under the fierce

light of the sunset. and the sky, like a delicate shell of pale pink,

fade off in the east to cold blue and sombre shadow, in the west

deepening into vivid billowy masse of golden cloud, which try

unsuccessfully to veil the intolerable splendour of the sink sun. a

breath of odorous wind under the burn sky, the chattering of

monkey, the shrieking of brilliant-coloured parrot, and the low,

guttural song of a naked negro clean his weapon in the near camp.



the man look at all this with vague, unseee eye, for his thought

be far away, then, dash away the tear, he once more begin to

read the letter he hold in his hand.





"MY DEAR EUSTACE,



"I can hardly believe that it be nine month since you leave we. I

wonder in what part of Africa you will read this letter, that is, if

it ever reach you, of which I have considerable doubt. the paper,

of course, inform we of your many month of delay at Zanzibar before

you could go forward, so perhaps this letter may reach you before you

get beyond the confine of civilization. I be very much astonish to

hear you be at Zanzibar, as I think you leave England with the

intention of go up the Nile, and get into the inland country

that way. however, I suppose you have good reason for change your

plan, and be now push forward into unknown land.



"I have a great deal to tell you about ourselves and friend, which I

be sure you will be pleased to hear. in the first place, both my wife

and myself be completely happy--all the cloud of our early life

have vanish, and I think that no married pair can have such perfect

confidence and love for one another. I ascribe this happy state of

thing to you, dear old fellow, for have you not make Mrs. Veilsturm

leave San Remo, and bring my wife to my sick bed, we could never

have come together again. I know, good friend that you be, you

will be pleased to hear we be so perfectly happy, and that every

year--every day--every hour, my wife grow dearer to I. as I write

these word her dear face be bend over my shoulder to read what I

have set down, and she cordially endorse what I have say.



"thank to your kind gift of Aunt Jelly's money, all thing

pecuniarily be well with I. I have pay off the mortgage on the

Hall, and invest the rest of the money, so what with the income

arise from such investment, and my rent, now regularly come to

I instead of to the lawyer, I be quite a rich man, and the

Erringtons can once wear hold up their head in the county as a

representative family.



"by-the-way, I have some news to give you about our mutual friend,

Mrs. Veilsturm, with whom I be so infatuated. she go on to New

York, follow by Dolly Thambits, and have now marry he. he be a

young idiot to be sure, but then he have an excellent income, and that

be all she care about. willn't she spend his thousand for he? well, I

think you and I agree on that subject. regard Major Griff, she

evidently find he less useful after than before she become Mrs.

Thambits, so she have pension he off with a few thousand, and I

hear the Major have go to Central America, with a view to enter

the service of one of the republic of those region. his future fate

be not hard to prophesy, as he will either become President or be

shoot, but in either event I don't think he'll trouble our fair friend

again who have retire so peacefully into married life. next year, I

believe, she be come to town, and be go to cut a great dash,

so no doubt Mrs. Thambits will be even more popular than Mrs.

Veilsturm--although, I dare say, there will not be any Sunday evening

of the Monte Carlo style.



"you will perhaps wonder at my writing so coolly about this lady, but

the fact be, I now see only too clearly the danger I escape. she

would have ruin my life, and certainly make a good attempt to do so,

only you fortunately intervene in time. what magic you use to force

she to leave I alone I do not know, but I certainly have to thank you

for extricate I from a very perilous position.



"another item of news. Mrs. Macjean have present the delighted

otterburn with a son and heir. by-the-way, I should not call he

otterburn, as, by the death of his father four month ago, he be

now Lord Dunkeld. but old habit be hard to get rid of, and I always

talk of they as Mrs. Macjean and Otterburn. they be very happy, as

they deserve to be, for Dunkeld be a real good fellow, and Lady

Dunkeld--well, she be all that be charming.



"do you remember Miss Minnie Pelch, poor Aunt Jelly's companion? she

be now down at Errington with we, as she be so lonely in town that

Alizon take pity on she, and she be instal as companion at the

Hall. her volume of verse come out in due splendour, and be entirely

overlook by the press, at which I be not sorry, as if the poem have

be notice--well, you know the poem of old. Minnie, however, think

this silence be jealousy, and quite look upon herself as a shine

light of the victorian age, so neither Alizon nor I undeceive she, for

she be a good little woman, though somewhat of a bore with her

infernal--I mean eternal--poetry.



"I really don't think there be any more news to tell you, except that

good old Mrs. Trubbles be dead--apoplexy--and her dear Harry be now on

the look-out for another spouse with political influence--I wish it

be 'poetical influence,' and we might manage to marry he to Miss

Pelch.



"Mr. Dolser and 'the Pepper Box' have both go under, never to rise

again I hope. some dreadful libel on a high personage appear, at

which the H.P. take umbrage, and the editor be now expiate his

offence in prison. I cann't say I'm very sorry, as when he be release

Mr. Dolser will no doubt leave other people's affair alone. such man

as he be the curse of the present age, and should all be sink in the

Atlantic for at least half an hour--after that I think we'd have no

more trouble with they.



"and now, my dear cousin, I must close this long letter, but first, in

confidence, let I hint to you that my wife be expect an

interesting event to take place shortly, which will once more render

the nursery a necessity. poor Alizon have bear up bravely since the

death of Sammy, but I know she long for a child of her own to fill

the vacant place in her heart. I be no long afraid of have a rival

in my child, as my wife love and trust I now, and my lot be as

perfectly happy a one as any mortal can hope for.



"so now goodbye, my dear Eustace. I hope we will soon see you back

again at the Hall, where there be always a place for you. my wife

send her kind regard to you, and so do I, thus close this

letter, and remain



"your affectionate Cousin,



"GUY ERRINGTON."





when Eustace finish read the letter he let it fall on the ground,

and laugh bitterly.



"kind regard," he say sadly, "and I give her love."



the sun be sink swiftly behind the dark hill, and Gartney, with

his hand support his chin, sit watch it, think of the day

that be no more.



so sad, so melancholy he feel, as he think of the past, of the woman

he love so fondly, whom he have restore to the arm of her husband at

the cost of his own happiness. surely, if he have be selfish, vain

and egotistical all his life, he have expiate these sin by his

voluntary sacrifice of self--a sacrifice that have banish all delight

from his heart.



and he sit there a lonely exile, with sorrow behind he, and danger

before he, while the sun sink in the burn west, and the sable

wing of night spread over the earth like a sombre pall.



there be darkness on the world, there be darkness in his heart, and

from the midst of the shadow still sound the melancholy chaunt of

the slave.





*     *     *     *     *     *





EXTRACT from "the morning PLANET."



"by a telegram from Zanzibar there now seem no doubt that the two

young Englishmen, who go into the interior of Africa some month

ago, have be massacre. only one survivor of the expedition escape

and manage to get safely to the coast. accord to his story, Mr.

Laxton be spear first by hostile native from an ambush. afterwards

Mr. Gartney meet with the same fate, although he defend himself for

some time with his revolver.



"much regret will be feel in England at this sad news, as the two

deceased gentleman be both very popular, Mr. Gartney especially

be widely know as a charming poet and essayist. he, be very

wealthy, and we hear that all his property, by a will execute before

he leave England, have be leave to Lady Errington, of Errington Hall,

Dreamshire."





*     *     *     *     *     *





so that be the end of Eustace Gartney.
